=head1 NAME

libPARI - Functions and Operations Available in PARI and GP

=head1 DESCRIPTION

X<Label se:functions>
The functions and operators available in PARI and in the GP/PARI calculator
are numerous and ever-expanding. Here is a description of the ones available
in version B<2.2.0>. It should be noted that many of these functions accept
quite different types as arguments, but others are more restricted. The list
of acceptable types will be given for each function or class of functions.
Except when stated otherwise, it is understood that a function or operation
which should make natural sense is legal. In this chapter, we will describe
the functions according to a rough classification. The general entry looks
something like:

B<foo>C<(x,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>: short description.

The library syntax is C<GEN foo(GEN x, long fl = 0)>.

This means that the GP function C<foo> has one mandatory argument C<x>, and
an optional one, I<flag>, whose default value is 0. (The C<{}> should not be
typed, it is just a convenient notation we will use throughout to denote
optional arguments.) That is, you can type C<foo(x,2)>, or C<foo(x)>,
which is then understood to mean C<foo(x,0)>. As well, a comma or closing
parenthesis, where an optional argument should have been, signals to GP it
should use the default. Thus, the syntax C<foo(x,)> is also accepted as a
synonym for our last expression. When a function has more than one optional
argument, the argument list is filled with user supplied values, in order.
When none are left, the defaults are used instead. Thus, assuming that
C<foo>'s prototype had been

S<  >
B<foo>C<({x = 1},{y = 2},{z = 3}),
>

typing in C<foo(6,4)> would give
you C<foo(6,4,3)>. In the rare case when you want to set some far away
argument, and leave the defaults in between as they stand, you can use the
``empty arg'' trick alluded to above: C<foo(6,,1)> would yield
C<foo(6,2,1)>. By the way, C<foo()> by itself yields
C<foo(1,2,3)> as was to be expected.

In this rather special case of a function having no mandatory argument, you
can even omit the C<()>: a standalone C<foo> would be enough (though we
do not recommend it for your scripts, for the sake of clarity). In defining
GP syntax, we strove to put optional arguments at the end of the argument
list (of course, since they would not make sense otherwise), and in order of
decreasing usefulness so that, most of the time, you will be able to ignore
them.

Finally, an optional argument (between braces) followed by a star, like
C<{>I<x>C<}*>, means that any number of such arguments (possibly none) can
be given. This is in particular used by the various C<print> routines.

B<Flags> A X<flag>I<flag> is an argument which, rather than conveying
actual information to the routine, instructs it to change its default
behavior, e.g.S< >return more or less information. All such
flags are optional, and will be called I<flag> in the function descriptions to
follow. There are two different kind of flags

\item generic: all valid values for the flag are individually
described (``If I<flag> is equal to C<1>, then...'').

\item binary:X<binary flag> use customary binary notation as a
compact way to represent many toggles with just one integer. Let
C<(p_0,...,p_n)> be a list of switches (i.e.S< >of properties which take either
the value C<0> orS< >C<1>), the number C<2^3 + 2^5 = 40> means that C<p_3> and C<p_5>
are set (that is, set to C<1>), and none of the others are (that is, they
are set to C<0>). This is announced as ``The binary digits of I<flag> mean 1:
C<p_0>, 2: C<p_1>, 4: C<p_2>'', and so on, using the available consecutive
powers ofS< >C<2>.

B<Mnemonics for flags> Numeric flags as mentioned above are
obscure, error-prone, and quite rigid: should the authors
want to adopt a new flag numbering scheme (for instance when noticing
flags with the same meaning but different numeric values across a set of
routines), it would break backward compatibility. The only advantage of
explicit numeric values is that they are fast to type, so their use is only
advised when using the calculator C<gp>.

As an alternative, one can replace a numeric flag by a character string
containing symbolic identifiers. For a generic flag, the mnemonic
corresponding to the numeric identifier is given after it as in

  fun(x, {flag = 0} ):

    If flag is equal to 1 = AGM, use an agm formula ...

which means that one can use indifferently C<fun(x, 1)> or
C<fun(x, "AGM")>.

For a binary flag, mnemonics corresponding to the various toggles are given
after each of them. They can be negated by prepending C<no_> to the
mnemonic, or by removing such a prefix. These toggles are grouped together
using any punctuation character (such as C<','> or C<';'>). For instance (taken
from description of X<ploth>C<ploth(X = a,b,>I<expr>C<,{>I<flag>C< = 0},{n = 0})>)

    Binary digits of flags mean: C<1 = Parametric>,
    C<2 = Recursive>,...

so that, instead of C<1>, one could use the mnemonic
C<"Parametric; no_Recursive">, or simply C<"Parametric"> since
C<Recursive> is unset by default (default value of I<flag> is C<0>,
i.e.S< >everything unset). People used to the bit-or notation in languages like
C may also use the form C<"Parametric | no_Recursive">.

B<Pointers> \varsidx{pointer} If a parameter in the function
prototype is prefixed with a & sign, as in

B<foo>C<(x,&e)>

it means that, besides the normal return value, the function may
assign a value to C<e> as a side effect. When passing the argument, the &
sign has to be typed in explicitly. As of version B<2.2.0>, this X<pointer>I<pointer>
argument is optional for all documented functions, hence the & will always
appear between brackets as in C<Z_issquare>C<(x,{&e})>.

B<About library programming>
The I<library> function C<foo>, as defined at the beginning of this
section, is seen to have two mandatory arguments, C<x> and I<flag>: no function
seen in the present chapter has been implemented so as to accept a variable
number of arguments, so all arguments are mandatory when programming with the
library (usually, variants are provided corresponding to the various flag values).
We include an C< = default value> token in the prototype to signal how a missing
argument should be encoded. Most of the time, it will be a C<NULL> pointer, or
-1 for a variable number. Refer to the \emph{User's Guide to the PARI library}
for general background and details.

=head1 Standard monadic or dyadic operators

=head2 +C</>-

The expressions C<+>C<x> and C<->C<x> refer
to monadic operators (the first does nothing, the second negates C<x>).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gneg>C<(GEN x)>X<gneg> for C<->C<x>.

=head2 +, C<->

The expression C<x> C<+> C<y> is the X<sum>sum and
C<x> C<-> C<y> is the X<difference>difference of C<x> and C<y>. Addition/subtraction
between a scalar type C<x> and a C<t_COL> or C<t_MAT> C<y> returns
respectively C<[y[1] F<+-> x, y[2],...]> and C<y F<+-> x Id>. Other
addition/subtraction between a scalar type and a vector or a matrix, or
between vector/matrices of incompatible sizes are forbidden.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gadd>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gadd> for C<x> C<+> C<y>,
C<GEN >B<gsub>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gsub> for C<x> C<-> C<y>.

=head2 *

The expression C<x> C<*> C<y> is the X<product>product of C<x>
and C<y>. Among the prominent impossibilities are multiplication between
vector/matrices of incompatible sizes, between a C<t_INTMOD> or C<t_PADIC>
Restricted to scalars, C<*> is commutative; because of vector and matrix
operations, it is not commutative in general.

Multiplication between two C<t_VEC>s or two C<t_COL>s is not
allowed; to take the X<scalar product>scalar product of two vectors of the same length,
transpose one of the vectors (using the operator C<~> or the function
C<mattranspose>, see L<Label se:linear_algebra>) and multiply a line vector
by a column vector:

  ? a = [1,2,3];
  ? a * a
    ***   at top-level: a*a
    ***                  ^--
    *** _*_: forbidden multiplication t_VEC * t_VEC.
  ? a * a~
  %2 = 14

If C<x,y> are binary quadratic forms, compose them; see also
C<qfbnucomp> and C<qfbnupow>. If C<x,y> are C<t_VECSMALL> of the same
length, understand them as permutations and compose them.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gmul>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gmul> for C<x> C<*> C<y>.
Also available is C<GEN >B<gsqr>C<(GEN x)>X<gsqr> for C<x> C<*> C<x>.

=head2 /

The expression C<x> C</> C<y> is the X<quotient>quotient of C<x>
and C<y>. In addition to the impossibilities for multiplication, note that if
the divisor is a matrix, it must be an invertible square matrix, and in that
case the result is C<x*y^{-1}>. Furthermore note that the result is as exact
as possible: in particular, division of two integers always gives a rational
number (which may be an integer if the quotient is exact) and \emph{not} the
Euclidean quotient (see C<x> C<\> C<y> for that), and similarly the
quotient of two polynomials is a rational function in general. To obtain the
approximate real value of the quotient of two integers, add C<0.> to the
result; to obtain the approximate C<p>-adic value of the quotient of two
integers, add C<O(p^k)> to the result; finally, to obtain the
X<Taylor series>Taylor series expansion of the quotient of two polynomials, add
C<O(X^k)> to the result or use the C<taylor> function
(see L<Label se:taylor>). X<Label se:gdiv>

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gdiv>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gdiv> for C<x> C</> C<y>.

=head2 \

The expression C<x \y> is the 
X<Euclidean quotient>Euclidean
quotient of C<x> and C<y>. If C<y> is a real scalar, this is defined as
C<floor(x/y)> if C<y E<gt> 0>, and C<ceil(x/y)> if C<y E<lt> 0> and
the division is not exact. Hence the remainder C<x - (x\y)*y>
is in C<[0, |y|[>.

Note that when C<y> is an integer and C<x> a polynomial, C<y> is first promoted
to a polynomial of degree C<0>. When C<x> is a vector or matrix, the operator
is applied componentwise.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gdivent>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gdivent>
for C<x> C<\> C<y>.

=head2 \/

The expression C<x> C<\/> C<y> evaluates to the rounded
X<Euclidean quotient>Euclidean quotient of C<x> and C<y>. This is the same as C<x \y>
except for scalar division: the quotient is such that the corresponding
remainder is smallest in absolute value and in case of a tie the quotient
closest to C<+ oo > is chosen (hence the remainder would belong to
C<]{-}|y|/2, |y|/2]>).

When C<x> is a vector or matrix, the operator is applied componentwise.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gdivround>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gdivround>
for C<x> C<\/> C<y>.

=head2 %

The expression C<x % y> evaluates to the modular
X<Euclidean remainder>Euclidean remainder of C<x> and C<y>, which we now define. When C<x> or C<y>
is a non-integral real number, C<x%y> is defined as C<x -
(x\y)*y>. Otherwise, if C<y> is an integer, this is the smallest
non-negative integer congruent to C<x> modulo C<y>. (This actually coincides with
the previous definition if and only if C<x> is an integer.) If C<y> is a
polynomial, this is the polynomial of smallest degree congruent to C<x> modulo
C<y>. For instance:

  ? (1/2) % 3
  %1 = 2
  ? 0.5 % 3
  %2 = 0.5000000000000000000000000000
  ? (1/2) % 3.0
  %3 = 1/2

Note that when C<y> is an integer and C<x> a polynomial, C<y> is first promoted
to a polynomial of degree C<0>. When C<x> is a vector or matrix, the operator
is applied componentwise.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gmod>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gmod>
for C<x> C<%> C<y>.

=head2 ^

The expression C<x^n> is X<powering>powering.
If the exponent is an integer, then exact operations are performed using
binary (left-shift) powering techniques. In particular, in this case C<x>
cannot be a vector or matrix unless it is a square matrix (invertible
if the exponent is negative). If C<x> is a C<p>-adic number, its
precision will increase if C<v_p(n) E<gt> 0>. Powering a binary quadratic form
(types C<t_QFI> and C<t_QFR>) returns a reduced representative of the
class, provided the input is reduced. In particular, C<x^1> is
identical to C<x>.

PARI is able to rewrite the multiplication C<x * x> of two \emph{identical}
objects as C<x^2>, or C<sqr(x)>. Here, identical means the operands are
two different labels referencing the same chunk of memory; no equality test
is performed. This is no longer true when more than two arguments are
involved.

If the exponent is not of type integer, this is treated as a transcendental
function (see L<Label se:trans>), and in particular has the effect of
componentwise powering on vector or matrices.

As an exception, if the exponent is a rational number C<p/q> and C<x> an
integer modulo a prime or a C<p>-adic number, return a solution C<y> of
C<y^q = x^p> if it exists. Currently, C<q> must not have large prime factors.
Beware that

  ? Mod(7,19)^(1/2)
  %1 = Mod(11, 19) /* is any square root */
  ? sqrt(Mod(7,19))
  %2 = Mod(8, 19)  /* is the smallest square root */
  ? Mod(7,19)^(3/5)
  %3 = Mod(1, 19)
  ? %3^(5/3)
  %4 = Mod(1, 19)  /* Mod(7,19) is just another cubic root */

If the exponent is a negative integer, an X<inverse>inverse must be computed.
For non-invertible C<t_INTMOD>, this will fail and implicitly exhibit a
non trivial factor of the modulus:

  ? Mod(4,6)^(-1)
    ***   at top-level: Mod(4,6)^(-1)
    ***                         ^-----
    *** _^_: impossible inverse modulo: Mod(2, 6).

(Here, a factor 2 is obtained directly. In general, take the gcd of the
representative and the modulus.) This is most useful when performing
complicated operations modulo an integer C<N> whose factorization is
unknown. Either the computation succeeds and all is well, or a factor C<d>
is discovered and the computation may be restarted modulo C<d> or C<N/d>.

For non-invertible C<t_POLMOD>, this will fail without exhibiting a
factor.

  ? Mod(x^2, x^3-x)^(-1)
    ***   at top-level: Mod(x^2,x^3-x)^(-1)
    ***                               ^-----
    *** _^_: non-invertible polynomial in RgXQ_inv.
  ? a = Mod(3,4)*y^3 + Mod(1,4); b = y^6+y^5+y^4+y^3+y^2+y+1;
  ? Mod(a, b)^(-1);
    ***   at top-level: Mod(a,b)^(-1)
    ***                         ^-----
    *** _^_: impossible inverse modulo: Mod(0, 4).

In fact the latter polynomial is invertible, but the algorithm used
(subresultant) assumes the base ring is a domain. If it is not the case,
as here for B<Z>C</4>B<Z>, a result will be correct but chances are an error
will occur first. In this specific case, one should work with C<2>-adics.
In general, one can try the following approach

  ? inversemod(a, b) =
  { my(m);
    m = polsylvestermatrix(polrecip(a), polrecip(b));
    m = matinverseimage(m, matid(#m)[,1]);
    Polrev( vecextract(m, Str("..", poldegree(b))), variable(b) )
  }
  ? inversemod(a,b)
  %2 = Mod(2,4)*y^5 + Mod(3,4)*y^3 + Mod(1,4)*y^2 + Mod(3,4)*y + Mod(2,4)

This is not guaranteed to work either since it must invert pivots. See
L<Label se:linear_algebra>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gpow>C<(GEN x, GEN n, long prec)>X<gpow>
for C<x^n>.

=head2 X<divrem>divremC<(x,y,{v})>

X<Label se:divrem>creates a column vector with two components, the first being the Euclidean
quotient (C<x \y>), the second the Euclidean remainder
(C<x - (x\y)*y>), of the division of C<x> by C<y>. This avoids the
need to do two divisions if one needs both the quotient and the remainder.
If C<v> is present, and C<x>, C<y> are multivariate
polynomials, divide with respect to the variable C<v>.

Beware that C<divrem(x,y)[2]> is in general not the same as
C<x % y>; no GP operator corresponds to it:

  ? divrem(1/2, 3)[2]
  %1 = 1/2
  ? (1/2) % 3
  %2 = 2
  ? divrem(Mod(2,9), 3)[2]
   ***   at top-level: divrem(Mod(2,9),3)[2
   ***                 ^--------------------
   ***   forbidden division t_INTMOD \ t_INT.
  ? Mod(2,9) % 6
  %3 = Mod(2,3)

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<divrem>C<(GEN x, GEN y, long v = -1)>X<divrem>, where C<v> is a variable number.
Also available is C<GEN >B<gdiventres>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gdiventres> when C<v> is
not needed.

=head2 X<lex>lexC<(x,y)>

X<Label se:lex>gives the result of a lexicographic comparison
between C<x> and C<y> (as C<-1>, C<0> or C<1>). This is to be interpreted in quite
a wide sense: It is admissible to compare objects of different types
(scalars, vectors, matrices), provided the scalars can be compared, as well
as vectors/matrices of different lengths. The comparison is recursive.

In case all components are equal up to the smallest length of the operands,
the more complex is considered to be larger. More precisely, the longest is
the largest; when lengths are equal, we have matrix C< E<gt> > vector C< E<gt> > scalar.
For example:

  ? lex([1,3], [1,2,5])
  %1 = 1
  ? lex([1,3], [1,3,-1])
  %2 = -1
  ? lex([1], [[1]])
  %3 = -1
  ? lex([1], [1]~)
  %4 = 0

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<lexcmp>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<lexcmp>.

=head2 X<max>maxC<(x,y)>

X<Label se:max>creates the maximum of C<x> and C<y> when they can be compared.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gmax>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gmax>.

=head2 X<min>minC<(x,y)>

X<Label se:min>creates the minimum of C<x> and C<y> when they can be compared.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gmin>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gmin>.

=head2 X<shift>shiftC<(x,n)>

X<Label se:shift>shifts C<x> componentwise left by C<n> bits if C<n E<gt>= 0> and right by C<|n|>
bits if C<n E<lt> 0>. May be abbreviated as C<x> C<E<lt>E<lt> > C<n> or C<x> C<E<gt>E<gt> > C<(-n)>.
A left shift by C<n> corresponds to multiplication by C<2^n>. A right shift of an
integer C<x> by C<|n|> corresponds to a Euclidean division of C<x> by C<2^{|n|}>
with a remainder of the same sign as C<x>, hence is not the same (in general) as
C<x \ 2^n>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gshift>C<(GEN x, long n)>X<gshift>.

=head2 X<shiftmul>shiftmulC<(x,n)>

X<Label se:shiftmul>multiplies C<x> by C<2^n>. The difference with
C<shift> is that when C<n E<lt> 0>, ordinary division takes place, hence for
example if C<x> is an integer the result may be a fraction, while for shifts
Euclidean division takes place when C<n E<lt> 0> hence if C<x> is an integer the result
is still an integer.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gmul2n>C<(GEN x, long n)>X<gmul2n>.

=head2 X<sign>signC<(x)>

X<Label se:sign>X<sign>sign (C<0>, C<1> or C<-1>) of C<x>, which must be of
type integer, real or fraction.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gsigne>C<(GEN x)>X<gsigne>.

=head2 X<vecmax>vecmaxC<(x)>

X<Label se:vecmax>if C<x> is a vector or a matrix, returns the maximum
of the elements of C<x>, otherwise returns a copy of C<x>. Error if C<x> is
empty.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<vecmax>C<(GEN x)>X<vecmax>.

=head2 X<vecmin>vecminC<(x)>

X<Label se:vecmin>if C<x> is a vector or a matrix, returns the minimum
of the elements of C<x>, otherwise returns a copy of C<x>. Error if C<x> is
empty.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<vecmin>C<(GEN x)>X<vecmin>.

=head2 Comparison and Boolean operators

X<Boolean operators>The six
standard X<comparison operators>comparison operators C<E<lt>= >, C<E<lt> >, C<E<gt>= >, C<E<gt> >,
C< == >, C<! = > are available in GP. The result is 1 if the comparison is
true, 0 if it is false. The operator C< == > is quite liberal : for
instance, the integer 0, a 0 polynomial, and a vector with 0 entries are all
tested equal.

The extra operator C< == = > tests whether two objects are identical and is
much stricter than C< == > : objects of different type or length are never
identical.

For the purpose of comparison, C<t_STR> objects are strictly larger than any
other non-string type; two C<t_STR> objects are compared using the standard
lexicographic order.

GP accepts the following synonyms for some of the above functions:
C<|> and C<&> are accepted asX<bitwise and>X<bitwise or>
synonyms of C<||> and C<&&> respectively. Also, C<E<lt>  E<gt> > is accepted
as a synonym for C<! = >. On the other hand, C< = > is definitely
\emph{not} a synonym for C< == >: it is the assignment statement. (We do not
use the customary C operators for bitwise C<and> or bitwise C<or>; use
X<bitand>C<bitand> or X<bitor>C<bitor>.)

The standard boolean operators C<||> (X<inclusive or>inclusive or), C<&&>
(X<and>and)X<or> and C<!> (X<not>not) are also available.

=head1 Conversions and similar elementary functions or commands

X<Label se:conversion>
Many of the conversion functions are rounding or truncating operations. In
this case, if the argument is a rational function, the result is the
Euclidean quotient of the numerator by the denominator, and if the argument
is a vector or a matrix, the operation is done componentwise. This will not
be restated for every function.

=head2 X<Col>ColC<({x = []})>

X<Label se:Col>
transforms the object C<x> into a column vector.
The vector has a single component , except when C<x> is

\item a vector or a quadratic form (in which case the resulting vector is
simply the initial object considered as a column vector),

\item a matrix (the column of row vectors comprising the matrix is
returned),

\item a character string (a column of individual characters is returned),

\item a polynomial or a power series. In the case of a polynomial, the
coefficients of the vector start with the leading coefficient of the
polynomial, while for power series only the significant coefficients are
taken into account, but this time by increasing order of degree. In this last
case, C<Col> is the reciprocal function of C<Pol> and C<Ser>
respectively.

Note that the function C<Colrev> does not exist, use C<Vecrev>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtocol>C<(GEN x = NULL)>X<gtocol>.

=head2 X<List>ListC<({x = []})>

X<Label se:List>
transforms a (row or column) vector C<x> into a list, whose components are
the entries of C<x>. Similarly for a list, but rather useless in this case.
For other types, creates a list with the single element C<x>. Note that,
except when C<x> is omitted, this function creates a small memory leak; so,
either initialize all lists to the empty list, or use them sparingly.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtolist>C<(GEN x = NULL)>X<gtolist>.
The variant C<GEN >B<listcreate>C<(void)>X<listcreate> creates an empty list.

=head2 X<Mat>MatC<({x = []})>

X<Label se:Mat>
transforms the object C<x> into a matrix.
If C<x> is already a matrix, a copy of C<x> is created.
If C<x> is a row (resp. column) vector, this creates a 1-row (resp.
1-column) matrix, \emph{unless} all elements are column (resp.S< >row) vectors
of the same length, in which case the vectors are concatenated sideways
and the associated big matrix is returned.
If C<x> is a binary quadratic form, creates the associated C<2 x 2>
matrix. Otherwise, this creates a C<1 x 1> matrix containing C<x>.

  ? Mat(x + 1)
  %1 =
  [x + 1]
  ? Vec( matid(3) )
  %2 = [[1, 0, 0]~, [0, 1, 0]~, [0, 0, 1]~]
  ? Mat(%)
  %3 =
  [1 0 0]

  [0 1 0]

  [0 0 1]
  ? Col( [1,2; 3,4] )
  %4 = [[1, 2], [3, 4]]~
  ? Mat(%)
  %5 =
  [1 2]

  [3 4]
  ? Mat(Qfb(1,2,3))
  %6 =
  [1 1]

  [1 3]

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtomat>C<(GEN x = NULL)>X<gtomat>.

=head2 X<Mod>ModC<(x,y)>

X<Label se:Mod>
creates the PARI object
C<(x mod y)>, i.e.S< >an intmod or a polmod. C<y> must be an integer or a
polynomial. If C<y> is an integer, C<x> must be an integer, a rational
number, or a C<p>-adic number compatible with the modulus C<y>. If C<y> is a
polynomial, C<x> must be a scalar (which is not a polmod), a polynomial, a
rational function, or a power series.

This function is not the same as C<x> C<%> C<y>, the result of which is an
integer or a polynomial.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gmodulo>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gmodulo>.

=head2 X<Pol>PolC<(x,{v = x})>

X<Label se:Pol>
transforms the object C<x> into a polynomial with main variable C<v>. If C<x>
is a scalar, this gives a constant polynomial. If C<x> is a power series with
non-negative valuation or a rational function, the effect is similar to
C<truncate>, i.e.S< >we chop off the C<O(X^k)> or compute the Euclidean
quotient of the numerator by the denominator, then change the main variable
of the result to C<v>.

The main use of this function is when C<x> is a vector: it creates the
polynomial whose coefficients are given by C<x>, with C<x[1]> being the leading
coefficient (which can be zero). It is much faster to evaluate
C<Pol> on a vector of coefficients in this way, than the corresponding
formal expression C<a_n X^n +...+ a_0>, which is evaluated naively exactly
as written (linear versus quadratic time in C<n>). X<Polrev>C<Polrev> can be used if
one wants C<x[1]> to be the constant coefficient:

  ? Pol([1,2,3])
  %1 = x^2 + 2*x + 3
  ? Polrev([1,2,3])
  %2 = 3*x^2 + 2*x + 1

The reciprocal function of C<Pol> (resp.S< >C<Polrev>) is C<Vec> (resp.S< >
C<Vecrev>).

  ? Vec(Pol([1,2,3]))
  %1 = [1, 2, 3]
  ? Vecrev( Polrev([1,2,3]) )
  %2 = [1, 2, 3]

B<Warning> This is \emph{not} a substitution function. It will not
transform an object containing variables of higher priority thanS< >C<v>.

  ? Pol(x + y, y)
    ***   at top-level: Pol(x+y,y)
    ***                 ^----------
    *** Pol: variable must have higher priority in gtopoly.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtopoly>C<(GEN x, long v = -1)>X<gtopoly>, where C<v> is a variable number.

=head2 X<Polrev>PolrevC<(x,{v = x})>

X<Label se:Polrev>
transform the object C<x> into a polynomial
with main variable C<v>. If C<x> is a scalar, this gives a constant polynomial.
If C<x> is a power series, the effect is identical to C<truncate>, i.e.S< >it
chops off the C<O(X^k)>.

The main use of this function is when C<x> is a vector: it creates the
polynomial whose coefficients are given by C<x>, with C<x[1]> being the
constant term. X<Pol>C<Pol> can be used if one wants C<x[1]> to be the leading
coefficient:

  ? Polrev([1,2,3])
  %1 = 3*x^2 + 2*x + 1
  ? Pol([1,2,3])
  %2 = x^2 + 2*x + 3

The reciprocal function of C<Pol> (resp.S< >C<Polrev>) is C<Vec> (resp.S< >
C<Vecrev>).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtopolyrev>C<(GEN x, long v = -1)>X<gtopolyrev>, where C<v> is a variable number.

=head2 X<Qfb>QfbC<(a,b,c,{D = 0.})>

X<Label se:Qfb>creates the binary quadratic formX<binary quadratic form>
C<ax^2+bxy+cy^2>. If C<b^2-4ac E<gt> 0>, initialize X<Shanks>Shanks' distance
function to C<D>. Negative definite forms are not implemented,
use their positive definite counterpart instead.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<Qfb0>C<(GEN a, GEN b, GEN c, GEN D = NULL, long prec)>X<Qfb0>.
Also available are
C<GEN >B<qfi>C<(GEN a, GEN b, GEN c)>X<qfi> (assumes C<b^2-4ac E<lt> 0>) and
C<GEN >B<qfr>C<(GEN a, GEN b, GEN c, GEN D)>X<qfr> (assumes C<b^2-4ac E<gt> 0>).

=head2 X<Ser>SerC<(s,{v = x},{d = >I<seriesprecision>C<})>

X<Label se:Ser>transforms the object C<s> into a power series with main variable C<v>
(C<x> by default) and precision (number of significant terms) equal to
C<d> ( = the default C<seriesprecision> by default). If C<s> is a
scalar, this gives a constant power series with precision C<d>. If C<s>
is a polynomial, the precision is the maximum of C<d> and the degree of
the polynomial. If C<s> is a vector, the
coefficients of the vector are understood to be the coefficients of the power
series starting from the constant term (as in X<Polrev>C<Polrev>C<(x)>), and the
precision C<d> is ignored.

  ? Ser(x^2,, 5)
  %1 = x^2 + O(x^7)
  ? Ser([1,2,3], t)
  %2 = 1 + 2*t + 3*t^2 + O(t^3)

The warning given for C<Pol> also applies here: this is not a substitution
function.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtoser>C<(GEN s, long v = -1, long precdl)>X<gtoser>, where C<v> is a variable number.

=head2 X<Set>SetC<({x = []})>

X<Label se:Set>
converts C<x> into a set, i.e.S< >into a row
vector of character strings, with strictly increasing entries with respect to
lexicographic ordering. The components of C<x> are put in canonical form (type
C<t_STR>) so as to be easily sorted. To recover an ordinary C<GEN> from
such an element, you can apply X<eval>C<eval> to it.

Note that most set functions also accept ordinary vectors, provided their
components can be compared with C<E<lt> >. Sets as created by this function
are only useful when e.g. polynomial or vector entries are involved.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtoset>C<(GEN x = NULL)>X<gtoset>.

=head2 X<Str>StrC<({x}*)>

X<Label se:Str>
converts its argument list into a
single character string (type C<t_STR>, the empty string if C<x> is omitted).
To recover an ordinary C<GEN> from a string, apply C<eval> to it. The
arguments of C<Str> are evaluated in string context, see L<Label se:strings>.

  ? x2 = 0; i = 2; Str(x, i)
  %1 = "x2"
  ? eval(%)
  %2 = 0

This function is mostly useless in library mode. Use the pair
X<strtoGEN>C<strtoGEN>/X<GENtostr>C<GENtostr> to convert between C<GEN> and C<char*>.
The latter returns a malloced string, which should be freed after usage.

=head2 X<Strchr>StrchrC<(x)>

X<Label se:Strchr>
converts C<x> to a string, translating each integer
into a character.

  ? Strchr(97)
  %1 = "a"
  ? Vecsmall("hello world")
  %2 = Vecsmall([104, 101, 108, 108, 111, 32, 119, 111, 114, 108, 100])
  ? Strchr(%)
  %3 = "hello world"

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<Strchr>C<(GEN x)>X<Strchr>.

=head2 X<Strexpand>StrexpandC<({x}*)>

X<Label se:Strexpand>
converts its argument list into a
single character string (type C<t_STR>, the empty string if C<x> is omitted).
Then perform X<environment expansion>environment expansion, see L<Label se:envir>.
This feature can be used to read X<environment variable>environment variable values.

  ? Strexpand("$HOME/doc")
  %1 = "/home/pari/doc"

The individual arguments are read in string context, see L<Label se:strings>.

=head2 X<Strtex>StrtexC<({x}*)>

X<Label se:Strtex>
translates its arguments to TeX
format, and concatenates the results into a single character string (type
C<t_STR>, the empty string if C<x> is omitted).

The individual arguments are read in string context, see L<Label se:strings>.

=head2 X<Vec>VecC<({x = []})>

X<Label se:Vec>
transforms the object C<x> into a row vector.
That vector has a single component, except when C<x> is

\item a vector or a quadratic form (in which case the resulting vector
is simply the initial object considered as a row vector),

\item a matrix (the vector of columns comprising the matrix is return),

\item a character string (a vector of individual
characters is returned),

\item a polynomial or a power series. In the case of a polynomial, the
coefficients of the vector start with the leading coefficient of the
polynomial, while for power series only the significant coefficients are
taken into account, but this time by increasing order of degree.
In this last case, C<Vec> is the reciprocal function of C<Pol> and
C<Ser> respectively.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtovec>C<(GEN x = NULL)>X<gtovec>.

=head2 X<Vecrev>VecrevC<({x = []})>

X<Label se:Vecrev>
as C<Vec(x)>, then reverse the result. In particular
In this case, C<Vecrev> is the reciprocal function of C<Polrev>: the
coefficients of the vector start with the constant coefficient of the
polynomial and the others follow by increasing degree.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtovecrev>C<(GEN x = NULL)>X<gtovecrev>.

=head2 X<Vecsmall>VecsmallC<({x = []})>

X<Label se:Vecsmall>
transforms the object C<x> into a row
vector of type C<t_VECSMALL>. This acts as C<Vec>, but only on a
limited set of objects (the result must be representable as a vector of small
integers). In particular, polynomials and power series are forbidden.
If C<x> is a character string, a vector of individual characters in ASCII
encoding is returned (X<Strchr>C<Strchr> yields back the character string).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gtovecsmall>C<(GEN x = NULL)>X<gtovecsmall>.

=head2 X<binary>binaryC<(x)>

X<Label se:binary>
outputs the vector of the binary digits of C<|x|>.
Here C<x> can be an integer, a real number (in which case the result has two
components, one for the integer part, one for the fractional part) or a
vector/matrix.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<binaire>C<(GEN x)>X<binaire>.

=head2 X<bitand>bitandC<(x,y)>

X<Label se:bitand>
bitwise X<and>C<and>
X<bitwise and>of two integers C<x> and C<y>, that is the integer

S<  >F<sum>C<_i (x_iS< >andS< >y_i) 2^i>

Negative numbers behave C<2>-adically, i.e.S< >the result is the C<2>-adic limit
of C<bitand>C<(x_n,y_n)>, where C<x_n> and C<y_n> are non-negative integers
tending to C<x> and C<y> respectively. (The result is an ordinary integer,
possibly negative.)

  ? bitand(5, 3)
  %1 = 1
  ? bitand(-5, 3)
  %2 = 3
  ? bitand(-5, -3)
  %3 = -7

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gbitand>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gbitand>.
Also available is
C<GEN >B<ibitand>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<ibitand>, which returns the bitwise \emph{and}
of C<|x|> and C<|y|>, two integers.

=head2 X<bitneg>bitnegC<(x,{n = -1})>

X<Label se:bitneg>
X<bitwise negation>bitwise negation of an integer C<x>,
truncated to C<n> bits, that is the integer 

S<  >F<sum>C<_{i = 0}^{n-1} not(x_i)
2^i>

The special case C<n = -1> means no truncation: an infinite sequence of
leading C<1> is then represented as a negative number.

See L<Label se:bitand> for the behavior for negative arguments.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gbitneg>C<(GEN x, long n)>X<gbitneg>.

=head2 X<bitnegimply>bitnegimplyC<(x,y)>

X<Label se:bitnegimply>
bitwise negated imply of two integers C<x> and
C<y> (or C<not> C<(x ==E<gt> y)>), that is the integer 

S<  >F<sum>C<
(x_iS< >and not(y_i)) 2^i>

See L<Label se:bitand> for the behavior for negative arguments.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gbitnegimply>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gbitnegimply>.
Also available is
C<GEN >B<ibitnegimply>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<ibitnegimply>, which returns the bitwise negated
imply of C<|x|> and C<|y|>, two integers.

=head2 X<bitor>bitorC<(x,y)>

X<Label se:bitor>
X<bitwise inclusive or>bitwise (inclusive)
X<or>C<or> of two integers C<x> and C<y>, that is the integer 

S<  >F<sum>C<
(x_iS< >orS< >y_i) 2^i>

See L<Label se:bitand> for the behavior for negative arguments.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gbitor>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gbitor>.
Also available is
C<GEN >B<ibitor>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<ibitor>, which returns the bitwise \emph{ir}
of C<|x|> and C<|y|>, two integers.

=head2 X<bittest>bittestC<(x,n)>

X<Label se:bittest>
outputs the C<n^{th}> bit of C<x> starting
from the right (i.e.S< >the coefficient of C<2^n> in the binary expansion of C<x>).
The result is 0 or 1.

  ? bittest(7, 3)
  %1 = 1 \\ the 3rd bit is 1
  ? bittest(7, 4)
  %2 = 0 \\ the 4th bit is 0

See L<Label se:bitand> for the behavior at negative arguments.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gbittest>C<(GEN x, long n)>X<gbittest>.
For a C<t_INT> C<x>, the variant C<long >B<bittest>C<(GEN x, long n)>X<bittest> is
generally easier to use.

=head2 X<bitxor>bitxorC<(x,y)>

X<Label se:bitxor>
bitwise (exclusive) X<or>C<or>
X<bitwise exclusive or>of two integers C<x> and C<y>, that is the integer

S<  >F<sum>C< (x_iS< >xorS< >y_i) 2^i>

See L<Label se:bitand> for the behavior for negative arguments.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gbitxor>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<gbitxor>.
Also available is
C<GEN >B<ibitxor>C<(GEN x, GEN y)>X<ibitxor>, which returns the bitwise \emph{xor}
of C<|x|> and C<|y|>, two integers.

=head2 X<ceil>ceilC<(x)>

X<Label se:ceil>
ceiling of C<x>. When C<x> is in B<R>, the result is the
smallest integer greater than or equal to C<x>. Applied to a rational
function, C<ceil(x)> returns the Euclidean quotient of the numerator by
the denominator.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gceil>C<(GEN x)>X<gceil>.

=head2 X<centerlift>centerliftC<(x,{v})>

X<Label se:centerlift>
lifts an element C<x = a mod n> of B<Z>C</n>B<Z>
to C<a> in B<Z>, and similarly lifts a polmod to a polynomial. This is the
same as X<lift>C<lift> except that in the particular case of elements of
B<Z>C</n>B<Z>, the lift C<y> is such that C<-n/2 E<lt> y E<lt>= n/2>.  A C<t_PADIC> is lifted
as above if its valuation C<v> is non-negative; if not, returns the fraction
C<p^v> C<centerlift>C<(x^{-v})>; in particular, note that rational
reconstruction is not attempted.

If C<x> is of type fraction, complex, quadratic, polynomial, power series,
rational function, vector or matrix, the lift is done for each coefficient.
Reals are forbidden.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<centerlift0>C<(GEN x, long v = -1)>X<centerlift0>, where C<v> is a variable number.
Also available is C<GEN >B<centerlift>C<(GEN x)>X<centerlift> corresponding to
C<centerlift0(x,-1)>.

=head2 X<component>componentC<(x,n)>

X<Label se:component>extracts the C<n^{th}>-component of C<x>. This is to be understood
as follows: every PARI type has one or two initial X<code words>code words. The
components are counted, starting at 1, after these code words. In particular
if C<x> is a vector, this is indeed the C<n^{th}>-component of C<x>, if
C<x> is a matrix, the C<n^{th}> column, if C<x> is a polynomial, the
C<n^{th}> coefficient (i.e.S< >of degree C<n-1>), and for power series,
the C<n^{th}> significant coefficient.

For polynomials and power series, one should rather use X<polcoeff>C<polcoeff>, and
for vectors and matrices, the C<[]> operator. Namely, if C<x> is a
vector, then X<x[n]>C<x[n]> represents the C<n^{th}> component of C<x>. If
C<x> is a matrix, X<x[m,n]>C<x[m,n]> represents the coefficient of row C<m> and
column C<n> of the matrix, X<x[m,]>C<x[m,]> represents the C<m^{th}>
\emph{row} of C<x>, and X<x[,n]>C<x[,n]> represents the C<n^{th}>
\emph{column} of C<x>.

Using of this function requires detailed knowledge of the structure of the
different PARI types, and thus it should almost never be used directly.
Some useful exceptions:

      ? x = 3 + O(3^5);
      ? component(x, 2)
      %2 = 81   \\ p^(p-adic acurracy)
      ? component(x, 1)
      %3 = 3    \\ p
      ? q = Qfb(1,2,3);
      ? component(q, 1)
      %5 = 1

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<compo>C<(GEN x, long n)>X<compo>.

=head2 X<conj>conjC<(x)>

X<Label se:conj>
conjugate of C<x>. The meaning of this
is clear, except that for real quadratic numbers, it means conjugation in the
real quadratic field. This function has no effect on integers, reals,
intmods, fractions or C<p>-adics. The only forbidden type is polmod
(see C<conjvec> for this).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gconj>C<(GEN x)>X<gconj>.

=head2 X<conjvec>conjvecC<(z)>

X<Label se:conjvec>
conjugate vector representation of C<z>. If C<z> is a
polmod, equal to C<Mod>C<(a,T)>, this gives a vector of length
C<degree(T)> containing:

\item the complex embeddings of C<z> if C<T> has rational coefficients,
i.e.S< >the C<a(r[i])> where C<r = polroots(T)>;

\item the conjugates of C<z> if C<T> has some intmod coefficients;

if C<z> is a finite field element, the result is the vector of
conjugates C<[z,z^p,z^{p^2},...,z^{p^{n-1}}]> where C<n = degree(T)>.

If C<z> is an integer or a rational number, the result isS< >C<z>. If
C<z> is a (row or column) vector, the result is a matrix whose columns are
the conjugate vectors of the individual elements of C<z>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<conjvec>C<(GEN z, long prec)>X<conjvec>.

=head2 X<denominator>denominatorC<(x)>

X<Label se:denominator>
denominator of C<x>. The meaning of this
is clear when C<x> is a rational number or function. If C<x> is an integer
or a polynomial, it is treated as a rational number or function,
respectively, and the result is equal to C<1>. For polynomials, you
probably want to use

  denominator( content(x) )

instead. As for modular objects, C<t_INTMOD> and C<t_PADIC> have
denominator C<1>, and the denominator of a C<t_POLMOD> is the denominator
of its (minimal degree) polynomial representative.

If C<x> is a recursive structure, for instance a vector or matrix, the lcm
of the denominators of its components (a common denominator) is computed.
This also applies for C<t_COMPLEX>s and C<t_QUAD>s.

B<Warning> Multivariate objects are created according to variable
priorities, with possibly surprising side effects (C<x/y> is a polynomial, but
C<y/x> is a rational function). See L<Label se:priority>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<denom>C<(GEN x)>X<denom>.

=head2 X<floor>floorC<(x)>

X<Label se:floor>
floor of C<x>. When C<x> is in B<R>, the result is the
largest integer smaller than or equal to C<x>. Applied to a rational function,
C<floor(x)> returns the Euclidean quotient of the numerator by the
denominator.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gfloor>C<(GEN x)>X<gfloor>.

=head2 X<frac>fracC<(x)>

X<Label se:frac>
fractional part of C<x>. Identical to
C<x-floor(x)>. If C<x> is real, the result is in C<[0,1[>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gfrac>C<(GEN x)>X<gfrac>.

=head2 X<imag>imagC<(x)>

X<Label se:imag>imaginary part of C<x>. When C<x> is a quadratic number, this is the
coefficient of F<omega> in the ``canonical'' integral basis C<(1,>F<omega>C<)>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gimag>C<(GEN x)>X<gimag>.

=head2 X<length>lengthC<(x)>

X<Label se:length>length of C<x>; C<#>C<x> is a shortcut for C<length>C<(x)>.
This is mostly useful for

\item vectors: dimension (0 for empty vectors),

\item lists: number of entries (0 for empty lists),

\item matrices: number of columns,

\item character strings: number of actual characters (without
trailing C<\0>, should you expect it from C<C> C<char*>).

   ? #"a string"
   %1 = 8
   ? #[3,2,1]
   %2 = 3
   ? #[]
   %3 = 0
   ? #matrix(2,5)
   %4 = 5
   ? L = List([1,2,3,4]); #L
   %5 = 4

The routine is in fact defined for arbitrary GP types, but is awkward and
useless in other cases: it returns the number of non-code words in C<x>, e.g.
the effective length minus 2 for integers since the C<t_INT> type has two code
words.

The library syntax is C<long >B<glength>C<(GEN x)>X<glength>.

=head2 X<lift>liftC<(x,{v})>

X<Label se:lift>lifts an element C<x = a mod n> of B<Z>C</n>B<Z> to
C<a> in B<Z>, and similarly lifts a polmod to a polynomial if C<v> is omitted.
Otherwise, lifts only polmods whose modulus has main variable C<v> (if C<v>
does not occur in C<x>, lifts only intmods). If C<x> is of recursive (non
modular) type, the lift is done coefficientwise. For C<p>-adics, this routine
acts as X<truncate>C<truncate>. It is not allowed to have C<x> of type C<t_REAL>.

  ? lift(Mod(5,3))
  %1 = 2
  ? lift(3 + O(3^9))
  %2 = 3
  ? lift(Mod(x,x^2+1))
  %3 = x
  ? lift(x * Mod(1,3) + Mod(2,3))
  %4 = x + 2
  ? lift(x * Mod(y,y^2+1) + Mod(2,3))
  %5 = y*x + Mod(2, 3)   \\ do you understand this one ?
  ? lift(x * Mod(y,y^2+1) + Mod(2,3), x)
  %6 = Mod(y, y^2+1) * x + Mod(2, y^2+1)

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<lift0>C<(GEN x, long v = -1)>X<lift0>, where C<v> is a variable number.
Also available is C<GEN >B<lift>C<(GEN x)>X<lift> corresponding to
C<lift0(x,-1)>.

=head2 X<norm>normC<(x)>

X<Label se:norm>
algebraic norm of C<x>, i.e.S< >the product of C<x> with
its conjugate (no square roots are taken), or conjugates for polmods. For
vectors and matrices, the norm is taken componentwise and hence is not the
C<L^2>-norm (see C<norml2>). Note that the norm of an element of
B<R> is its square, so as to be compatible with the complex norm.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gnorm>C<(GEN x)>X<gnorm>.

=head2 X<norml2>norml2C<(x)>

X<Label se:norml2>
square of the C<L^2>-norm of C<x>. More precisely,
if C<x> is a scalar, C<norml2(x)> is defined to be C<x * conj(x)>.
If C<x> is a polynomial, a (row or column) vector or a matrix, C<norml2(x)> is
defined recursively as F<sum>C<_i norml2(x_i)>, where C<(x_i)> run through
the components of C<x>. In particular, this yields the usual F<sum>C< |x_i|^2>
(resp.S< >F<sum>C< |x_{i,j}|^2>) if C<x> is a polynomial or vector (resp.S< >matrix) with
complex components.

  ? norml2( [ 1, 2, 3 ] )      \\ vector
  %1 = 14
  ? norml2( [ 1, 2; 3, 4] )   \\ matrix
  %2 = 30
  ? norml2( 2*I + x )
  %3 = 5
  ? norml2( [ [1,2], [3,4], 5, 6 ] )   \\ recursively defined
  %4 = 91

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gnorml2>C<(GEN x)>X<gnorml2>.

=head2 X<numerator>numeratorC<(x)>

X<Label se:numerator>
numerator of C<x>. The meaning of this
is clear when C<x> is a rational number or function. If C<x> is an integer
or a polynomial, it is treated as a rational number or function,
respectively, and the result is C<x> itself. For polynomials, you
probably want to use

  numerator( content(x) )

instead.

In other cases, C<numerator(x)> is defined to be
C<denominator(x)*x>. This is the case when C<x> is a vector or a
matrix, but also for C<t_COMPLEX> or C<t_QUAD>. In particular since a
C<t_PADIC> or C<t_INTMOD> has  denominator C<1>, its numerator is
itself.

B<Warning> Multivariate objects are created according to variable
priorities, with possibly surprising side effects (C<x/y> is a polynomial, but
C<y/x> is a rational function). See L<Label se:priority>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<numer>C<(GEN x)>X<numer>.

=head2 X<numtoperm>numtopermC<(n,k)>

X<Label se:numtoperm>generates the C<k>-th permutation (as a row vector of length C<n>) of the
numbers C<1> to C<n>. The number C<k> is taken modulo C<n!>, i.e.S< >inverse
function of X<permtonum>C<permtonum>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<numtoperm>C<(long n, GEN k)>X<numtoperm>.

=head2 X<padicprec>padicprecC<(x,p)>

X<Label se:padicprec>absolute C<p>-adic precision of the object C<x>. This is the minimum
precision of the components of C<x>. The result is X<LONG_MAX>C<LONG_MAX>
(C<2^{31}-1> for 32-bit machines or C<2^{63}-1> for 64-bit machines) if C<x> is
an exact object.

The library syntax is C<long >B<padicprec>C<(GEN x, GEN p)>X<padicprec>.

=head2 X<permtonum>permtonumC<(x)>

X<Label se:permtonum>given a permutation C<x> on C<n> elements, gives the number C<k> such that
C<x = numtoperm(n,k)>, i.e.S< >inverse function of X<numtoperm>C<numtoperm>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<permtonum>C<(GEN x)>X<permtonum>.

=head2 X<precision>precisionC<(x,{n})>

X<Label se:precision>gives the precision in decimal digits of the PARI object C<x>. If C<x> is
an exact object, the largest single precision integer is returned.

  ? precision(exp(1e-100))
  %1 = 134                \\ 134 significant decimal digits
  ? precision(2 + x)
  %2 = 2147483647         \\ exact object
  ? precision(0.5 + O(x))
  %3 = 28                 \\ floating point accuracy, NOT series precision
  ? precision( [ exp(1e-100), 0.5 ] )
  %4 = 28                 \\ minimal accuracy among components

The return value for exact objects is meaningless since it is not even the
same on 32 and 64-bit machines. The proper way to test whether an object is
exact is

  ? isexact(x) = precision(x) == precision(0)

If C<n> is not omitted, creates a new object equal to C<x> with a new
``precision'' C<n>. (This never changes the type of the result. In particular
it is not possible to use it to obtain a polynomial from a power series; tor
that, see X<truncate>C<truncate>.) Now the meaning of precision is different from the
above (floating point accuracy), and depends on the type of C<x>:

For exact types, no change. For C<x> a vector or a matrix, the operation is
done componentwise.

For real C<x>, C<n> is the number of desired significant \emph{decimal}
digits. If C<n> is smaller than the precision of C<x>, C<x> is truncated,
otherwise C<x> is extended with zeros.

For C<x> a C<p>-adic or a power series, C<n> is the desired number of
\emph{significant} C<p>-adic or C<X>-adic digits, where C<X> is the main
variable of C<x>. (Note: yes, this is inconsistent.)
Note that the precision is a priori distinct from the exponent C<k> appearing
in C<O(*^k)>; it is indeed equal to C<k> if and only if C<x> is a C<p>-adic
or C<X>-adic \emph{unit}.

  ? precision(1 + O(x), 10)
  %1 = 1 + O(x^10)
  ? precision(x^2 + O(x^10), 3)
  %2 = x^2 + O(x^5)
  ? precision(7^2 + O(7^10), 3)
  %3 = 7^2 + O(7^5)

For the last two examples, note that C<x^2 + O(x^5) = x^2(1 + O(x^3))>
indeed has 3 significant coefficients

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<precision0>C<(GEN x, long n)>X<precision0>.
Also available are C<GEN >B<gprec>C<(GEN x, long n)>X<gprec> and
C<long >B<precision>C<(GEN x)>X<precision>. In both, the accuracy is expressed in
\emph{words} (32-bit or 64-bit depending on the architecture).

=head2 X<random>randomC<({N = 2^{>I<31>C<}})>

X<Label se:random>
returns a random element in various natural sets depending on the
argument C<N>.

\item C<t_INT>: returns an integer
uniformly distributed between C<0> and C<N-1>. Omitting the argument
is equivalent to C<random(2^31)>.

\item C<t_REAL>: returns a real number in C<[0,1[> with the same accuracy as
C<N> (whose mantissa has the same number of significant words).

\item C<t_INTMOD>: returns a random intmod for the same modulus.

\item C<t_FFELT>: returns a random element in the same finite field.

\item C<t_VEC> generated by C<ellinit> over a finite field C<k>
(coefficients are C<t_INTMOD>s modulo a prime or C<t_FFELT>s): returns a
random C<k>-rational \emph{affine} point on the curve. More precisely an
abscissa is drawn uniformly at random until X<ellordinate>C<ellordinate> succeeds.
In particular, the curves over B<F>C<_2> with a single point (at infinity!)
will trigger an infinite loop. Note that this is definitely not a uniform
distribution over C<E(k)>.

\item C<t_POL> return a random polynomial of degree at most the degree of C<N>.
The coefficients are drawn by applying C<random> to the leading
coefficient of C<N>.

  ? random(10)
  %1 = 9
  ? random(Mod(0,7))
  %2 = Mod(1, 7)
  ? a = ffgen(ffinit(3,7), 'a); random(a)
  %3 = a^6 + 2*a^5 + a^4 + a^3 + a^2 + 2*a
  ? E = ellinit([0,0,0,3,7]*Mod(1,109)); random(E)
  %4 = [Mod(103, 109), Mod(10, 109)]
  ? E = ellinit([0,0,0,1,7]*a^0); random(E)
  %5 = [a^6 + a^5 + 2*a^4 + 2*a^2, 2*a^6 + 2*a^4 + 2*a^3 + a^2 + 2*a]
  ? random(Mod(1,7)*x^4)
  %6 = Mod(5, 7)*x^4 + Mod(6, 7)*x^3 + Mod(2, 7)*x^2 + Mod(2, 7)*x + Mod(5, 7)

These variants all depend on a single internal generator, and are
independent from the system's random number generator.
A random seed may be obtained via X<getrand>C<getrand>, and reset
using X<setrand>C<setrand>: from a given seed, and given sequence of C<random>s,
the exact same values will be generated. The same seed is used at each
startup, reseed the generator yourself if this is a problem.

B<Technical note>
Up to
version 2.4 included, the internal generator produced pseudo-random numbers
by means of linear congruences, which were not well distributed in arithmetic
progressions. We now
use Brent's XORGEN algorithm, based on Feedback Shift Registers, see
L<http://wwwmaths.anu.edu.au/~brent/random.html>. The generator has period
C<2^{4096}-1>, passes the Crush battery of statistical tests of L'Ecuyer and
Simard, but is not suitable for cryptographic purposes: one can reconstruct
the state vector from a small sample of consecutive values, thus predicting
the entire sequence.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<genrand>C<(GEN N = NULL)>X<genrand>.

Also available: C<GEN >B<ellrandom>C<(GEN E)>X<ellrandom> and C<GEN >B<ffrandom>C<(GEN a)>X<ffrandom>.

=head2 X<real>realC<(x)>

X<Label se:real>real part of C<x>. In the case where C<x> is a quadratic number, this is the
coefficient of C<1> in the ``canonical'' integral basis C<(1,>F<omega>C<)>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<greal>C<(GEN x)>X<greal>.

=head2 X<round>roundC<(x,{&e})>

X<Label se:round>If C<x> is in B<R>, rounds C<x> to the nearest integer (rounding to
C<+ oo > in case of ties), then and sets C<e> to the number of error bits,
that is the binary exponent of the difference between the original and the
rounded value (the ``fractional part''). If the exponent of C<x> is too large
compared to its precision (i.e.S< >C<e E<gt> 0>), the result is undefined and an error
occurs if C<e> was not given.

B<Important remark> Contrary to the other truncation functions,
this function operates on every coefficient at every level of a PARI object.
For example

S<  >C<truncate((2.4*X^2-1.7)/(X)) = 2.4*X,>

whereas

S<  >C<round((2.4*X^2-1.7)/(X)) = (2*X^2-2)/(X).>

An important use of C<round> is to get exact results after an approximate
computation, when theory tells you that the coefficients must be integers.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<round0>C<(GEN x, GEN *e = NULL)>X<round0>.
Also available are C<GEN >B<grndtoi>C<(GEN x, long *e)>X<grndtoi> and
C<GEN >B<ground>C<(GEN x)>X<ground>.

=head2 X<simplify>simplifyC<(x)>

X<Label se:simplify>
this function simplifies C<x> as much as it can. Specifically, a complex or
quadratic number whose imaginary part is the integer 0 (i.e.S< >not C<Mod(0,2)>
or C<0.E-28>) is converted to its real part, and a polynomial of degree C<0>
is converted to its constant term. Simplifications occur recursively.

This function is especially useful before using arithmetic functions,
which expect integer arguments:

  ? x = 2 + y - y
  %1 = 2
  ? isprime(x)
    ***   at top-level: isprime(x)
    ***                 ^----------
    *** isprime: not an integer argument in an arithmetic function
  ? type(x)
  %2 = "t_POL"
  ? type(simplify(x))
  %3 = "t_INT"

Note that GP results are simplified as above before they are stored in the
history. (Unless you disable automatic simplification with C<\y>, that is.)
In particular

  ? type(%1)
  %4 = "t_INT"

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<simplify>C<(GEN x)>X<simplify>.

=head2 X<sizebyte>sizebyteC<(x)>

X<Label se:sizebyte>outputs the total number of bytes occupied by the tree representing the
PARI object C<x>.

The library syntax is C<long >B<gsizebyte>C<(GEN x)>X<gsizebyte>.
Also available is C<long >B<gsizeword>C<(GEN x)>X<gsizeword> returning a
number of \emph{words}.

=head2 X<sizedigit>sizedigitC<(x)>

X<Label se:sizedigit>
outputs a quick bound for the number of decimal
digits of (the components of) C<x>, off by at most C<1>. If you want the
exact value, you can use C<#Str(x)>, which is slower.

The library syntax is C<long >B<sizedigit>C<(GEN x)>X<sizedigit>.

=head2 X<truncate>truncateC<(x,{&e})>

X<Label se:truncate>truncates C<x> and sets C<e> to the number of
error bits. When C<x> is in B<R>, this means that the part after the decimal
point is chopped away, C<e> is the binary exponent of the difference between
the original and the truncated value (the ``fractional part''). If the
exponent of C<x> is too large compared to its precision (i.e.S< >C<e E<gt> 0>), the
result is undefined and an error occurs if C<e> was not given. The function
applies componentwise on vector / matrices; C<e> is then the maximal number of
error bits. If C<x> is a rational function, the result is the ``integer part''
(Euclidean quotient of numerator by denominator) and C<e> is not set.

Note a very special use of C<truncate>: when applied to a power series, it
transforms it into a polynomial or a rational function with denominator
a power of C<X>, by chopping away the C<O(X^k)>. Similarly, when applied to
a C<p>-adic number, it transforms it into an integer or a rational number
by chopping away the C<O(p^k)>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<trunc0>C<(GEN x, GEN *e = NULL)>X<trunc0>.
The following functions are also available: C<GEN >B<gtrunc>C<(GEN x)>X<gtrunc>
and C<GEN >B<gcvtoi>C<(GEN x, long *e)>X<gcvtoi>.

=head2 X<valuation>valuationC<(x,p)>

X<Label se:valuation>
computes the highest
exponent of C<p> dividing C<x>. If C<p> is of type integer, C<x> must be an
integer, an intmod whose modulus is divisible by C<p>, a fraction, a
C<q>-adic number with C<q = p>, or a polynomial or power series in which case the
valuation is the minimum of the valuation of the coefficients.

If C<p> is of type polynomial, C<x> must be of type polynomial or rational
function, and also a power series if C<x> is a monomial. Finally, the
valuation of a vector, complex or quadratic number is the minimum of the
component valuations.

If C<x = 0>, the result is X<LONG_MAX>C<LONG_MAX> (C<2^{31}-1> for 32-bit machines or
C<2^{63}-1> for 64-bit machines) if C<x> is an exact object. If C<x> is a
C<p>-adic numbers or power series, the result is the exponent of the zero.
Any other type combinations gives an error.

The library syntax is C<long >B<ggval>C<(GEN x, GEN p)>X<ggval>.

=head2 X<variable>variableC<({x})>

X<Label se:variable>
gives the main variable of the object C<x>, and
C<p> if C<x> is a C<p>-adic number. Gives an error if C<x> has no variable
associated to it. If C<x> is omitted, returns the list of user variables known
to the interpreter, by order of decreasing priority. (Highest priority is C<x>,
which always come first.)

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gpolvar>C<(GEN x = NULL)>X<gpolvar>.
However, in library mode, this function should not be used for C<x>
non-C<NULL>, since X<gvar>C<gvar> is more appropriate. Instead, for
C<x> a C<p>-adic (type C<t_PADIC>), C<p> is C<gel(x,2)>; otherwise, use
C<long >B<gvar>C<(GEN x)>X<gvar> which returns the variable number of C<x> if
it exists, C<NO_VARIABLE> otherwise, which satisfies the property
C<varncmp(NO_VARIABLE, v) E<gt> 0> for all valid variable number
C<v>, i.e. it has lower priority than any variable.

=head1 Transcendental functions

X<Label se:trans>
Since the values of transcendental functions cannot be exactly represented,
these functions will always return an inexact object: a real number,
a complex number, a C<p>-adic number or a power series.  All these objects
have a certain finite precision.

As a general rule, which of course in some cases may have exceptions,
transcendental functions operate in the following way:

\item If the argument is either a real number or an inexact complex number
(like C<1.0 + I> or C<Pi*I> but not C<2 - 3*I>), then the
computation is done with the precision of the argument.
In the example below, we see that changing the precision to C<50> digits does
not matter, because C<x> only had a precision of C<19> digits.

  ? \p 15
     realprecision = 19 significant digits (15 digits displayed)
  ? x = Pi/4
  %1 = 0.785398163397448
  ? \p 50
     realprecision = 57 significant digits (50 digits displayed)
  ? sin(x)
  %2 = 0.7071067811865475244

Note that even if the argument is real, the result may be complex
(e.g.S< >C<acos(2.0)> or C<acosh(0.0)>). See each individual
function help for the definition of the branch cuts and choice of principal
value.

\item If the argument is either an integer, a rational, an exact complex
number or a quadratic number, it is first converted to a real
or complex number using the current X<precision>precision held in the default
X<realprecision>C<realprecision>.  This precision (the number of decimal digits) can be
changed using C<\p> or C<default(realprecision,...)>).
After this conversion, the computation proceeds as above for real or complex
arguments.

In library mode, the C<realprecision> does not matter; instead the
precision is taken from the C<prec> parameter which every transcendental
function has.  As in C<gp>, this C<prec> is not used when the
argument to a function is already inexact.
Note that the argument I<prec> stands for the length in words of a real
number, including codewords. Hence we must have I<prec>C< E<gt>= 3>.

Some accuracies attainable on 32-bit machines cannot be attained
on 64-bit machines for parity reasons. For example the default C<gp> accuracy
is 28 decimal digits on 32-bit machines, corresponding to I<prec> having
the value 5, but this cannot be attained on 64-bit machines.

\item If the argument is a polmod (representing an algebraic number),
then the function is evaluated for every possible complex embedding of that
algebraic number.  A column vector of results is returned, with one component
for each complex embedding.  Therefore, the number of components equals
the degree of the C<t_POLMOD> modulus.

\item If the argument is an intmod or a C<p>-adic, at present only a
few functions like C<sqrt> (square root), C<sqr> (square), C<log>,
C<exp>, powering, C<teichmuller> (TeichmE<uuml>ller character) and
C<agm> (arithmetic-geometric mean) are implemented.

Note that in the case of a C<2>-adic number, C<sqr(x)> may not be
identical to C<x*x>: for example if C<x = 1+O(2^5)> and C<y = 1+O(2^5)> then
C<x*y = 1+O(2^5)> while C<sqr(x) = 1+O(2^6)>. Here, C<x * x> yields the
same result as C<sqr(x)> since the two operands are known to be
\emph{identical}. The same statement holds true for C<p>-adics raised to the
power C<n>, where C<v_p(n) E<gt> 0>.

B<Remark> If we wanted to be strictly consistent with
the PARI philosophy, we should have C<x*y = (4 mod 8)> and C<sqr(x) = 
(4 mod 32)> when both C<x> and C<y> are congruent to C<2> modulo C<4>.
However, since intmod is an exact object, PARI assumes that the modulus
must not change, and the result is hence C<(0 mod 4)> in both cases. On
the other hand, C<p>-adics are not exact objects, hence are treated
differently.

\item If the argument is a polynomial, a power series or a rational function,
it is, if necessary, first converted to a power series using the current
series precision, held in the default X<seriesprecision>C<seriesprecision>. This precision
(the number of significant terms) can be changed using C<\ps> or
C<default(seriesprecision,...)>. Then the Taylor series expansion of the
function around C<X = 0> (where C<X> is the main variable) is computed to a
number of terms depending on the number of terms of the argument and the
function being computed.

Under C<gp> this again is transparent to the user. When programming in
library mode, however, it is \emph{strongly} advised to perform an explicit
conversion to a power series first, as in C<x = gtoser(x, seriesprec)>,
where the number of significant terms C<seriesprec> can be specified
explicitly. If you do not do this, a global variable C<precdl> is used
instead, to convert polynomials and rational functions to a power series with
a reasonable number of terms; tampering with the value of this global
variable is \emph{deprecated} and strongly discouraged.

\item If the argument is a vector or a matrix, the result is the
componentwise evaluation of the function. In particular, transcendental
functions on square matrices, which are not implemented in the present
version B<2.2.0>, will have a different name if they are implemented some day.

=head2 ^

If C<y> is not of type integer, C<x^y> has the same
effect as C<exp(y*log(x))>. It can be applied to C<p>-adic numbers as well
as to the more usual types.X<powering>

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gpow>C<(GEN x, GEN n, long prec)>X<gpow>
for C<x^n>.

=head2 Euler

X<Label se:Euler>Euler's constant F<gamma>C< = 0.57721...>. Note that
C<Euler> is one of the few special reserved names which cannot be used for
variables (the others are C<I> and C<Pi>, as well as all function
names).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<mpeuler>C<(long prec)>X<mpeuler>.

=head2 I

X<Label se:I>the complex number  F<sqrt> C<{-1}>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gen_I>C<()>X<gen_I>.

=head2 Pi

X<Label se:Pi>the constant F<Pi> (C<3.14159...>).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<mppi>C<(long prec)>X<mppi>.

=head2 X<abs>absC<(x)>

X<Label se:abs>absolute value of C<x> (modulus if C<x> is complex).
Rational functions are not allowed. Contrary to most transcendental
functions, an exact argument is \emph{not} converted to a real number before
applying C<abs> and an exact result is returned if possible.

  ? abs(-1)
  %1 = 1
  ? abs(3/7 + 4/7*I)
  %2 = 5/7
  ? abs(1 + I)
  %3 = 1.414213562373095048801688724

If C<x> is a polynomial, returns C<-x> if the leading coefficient is
real and negative else returns C<x>. For a power series, the constant
coefficient is considered instead.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gabs>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gabs>.

=head2 X<acos>acosC<(x)>

X<Label se:acos>principal branch of C<cos^{-1}(x) = -i > F<log> C< (x + i> F<sqrt> C<{1-x^2})>.
In particular, C<Re(acos(x)) belongs to [0,>F<Pi>C<]> and if C<x belongs to >B<R> and C<|x| E<gt> 1>,
then C<acos(x)> is complex. The branch cut is in two pieces:
C<]- oo ,-1]> , continuous with quadrant II, and C<[1,+ oo [>, continuous
with quadrant IV. We have C<acos(x) = >F<Pi>C</2 - asin(x)> for all
C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gacos>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gacos>.

=head2 X<acosh>acoshC<(x)>

X<Label se:acosh>principal branch of C<cosh^{-1}(x) = 2
> F<log> C<(> F<sqrt> C<{(x+1)/2} + > F<sqrt> C<{(x-1)/2})>. In particular,
C<Re(acosh(x)) E<gt>= 0> and
C<In(acosh(x)) belongs to ]->F<Pi>C<,>F<Pi>C<]0>; if C<x belongs to >B<R> and C<x E<lt> 1>, then
C<acosh(x)> is complex.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gach>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gach>.

=head2 X<agm>agmC<(x,y)>

X<Label se:agm>arithmetic-geometric mean of C<x> and C<y>. In the
case of complex or negative numbers, the principal square root is always
chosen. C<p>-adic or power series arguments are also allowed. Note that
a C<p>-adic agm exists only if C<x/y> is congruent to 1 modulo C<p> (modulo
16 for C<p = 2>). C<x> and C<y> cannot both be vectors or matrices.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<agm>C<(GEN x, GEN y, long prec)>X<agm>.

=head2 X<arg>argC<(x)>

X<Label se:arg>argument of the complex number C<x>, such that C<->F<Pi>C< E<lt> arg(x) E<lt>= >F<Pi>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<garg>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<garg>.

=head2 X<asin>asinC<(x)>

X<Label se:asin>principal branch of C<sin^{-1}(x) = -i > F<log> C<(ix + > F<sqrt> C<{1 - x^2})>.
In particular, C<Re(asin(x)) belongs to [->F<Pi>C</2,>F<Pi>C</2]> and if C<x belongs to >B<R> and
C<|x| E<gt> 1> then C<asin(x)> is complex. The branch cut is in two pieces:
C<]- oo ,-1]>, continuous with quadrant II, and C<[1,+ oo [> continuous
with quadrant IV. The function satisfies C<i asin(x) = 
asinh(ix)>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gasin>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gasin>.

=head2 X<asinh>asinhC<(x)>

X<Label se:asinh>principal branch of C<sinh^{-1}(x) = > F<log> C<(x + > F<sqrt> C<{1+x^2})>. In
particular C<Im(asinh(x)) belongs to [->F<Pi>C</2,>F<Pi>C</2]>.
The branch cut is in two pieces: [-i oo ,-i],  continuous with quadrant III
and [i,+i oo [ continuous with quadrant I.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gash>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gash>.

=head2 X<atan>atanC<(x)>

X<Label se:atan>principal branch of C<tan^{-1}(x) = > F<log> C< ((1+ix)/(1-ix)) /
2i>. In particular the real part of C<atan(x))> belongs to
C<]->F<Pi>C</2,>F<Pi>C</2[>.
The branch cut is in two pieces:
C<]-i oo ,-i[>, continuous with quadrant IV, and C<]i,+i  oo [> continuous
with quadrant II. The function satisfies C<i atan(x) = 
-iatanh(ix)> for all C<x ! = F<+-> i>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gatan>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gatan>.

=head2 X<atanh>atanhC<(x)>

X<Label se:atanh>principal branch of C<tanh^{-1}(x) = log ((1+x)/(1-x)) / 2>. In
particular the imaginary part of C<atanh(x)> belongs to
C<[->F<Pi>C</2,>F<Pi>C</2]>; if C<x belongs to >B<R> and C<|x| E<gt> 1> then C<atanh(x)> is complex.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gath>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gath>.

=head2 X<bernfrac>bernfracC<(x)>

X<Label se:bernfrac>Bernoulli numberX<Bernoulli numbers> C<B_x>,
where C<B_0 = 1>, C<B_1 = -1/2>, C<B_2 = 1/6>,..., expressed as a rational number.
The argument C<x> should be of type integer.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bernfrac>C<(long x)>X<bernfrac>.

=head2 X<bernreal>bernrealC<(x)>

X<Label se:bernreal>Bernoulli numberX<Bernoulli numbers>
C<B_x>, as C<bernfrac>, but C<B_x> is returned as a real number
(with the current precision).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bernreal>C<(long x, long prec)>X<bernreal>.

=head2 X<bernvec>bernvecC<(x)>

X<Label se:bernvec>creates a vector containing, as rational numbers,
the X<Bernoulli numbers>Bernoulli numbers C<B_0>, C<B_2>,..., C<B_{2x}>.
This routine is obsolete. Use C<bernfrac> instead each time you need a
Bernoulli number in exact form.

B<Note> This routine is implemented using repeated independent
calls to C<bernfrac>, which is faster than the standard recursion in exact
arithmetic. It is only kept for backward compatibility: it is not faster than
individual calls to C<bernfrac>, its output uses a lot of memory space,
and coping with the index shift is awkward.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bernvec>C<(long x)>X<bernvec>.

=head2 X<besselh1>besselh1C<(>I<nu>C<,x)>

X<Label se:besselh1>C<H^1>-Bessel function of index I<nu> and argument C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<hbessel1>C<(GEN nu, GEN x, long prec)>X<hbessel1>.

=head2 X<besselh2>besselh2C<(>I<nu>C<,x)>

X<Label se:besselh2>C<H^2>-Bessel function of index I<nu> and argument C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<hbessel2>C<(GEN nu, GEN x, long prec)>X<hbessel2>.

=head2 X<besseli>besseliC<(>I<nu>C<,x)>

X<Label se:besseli>C<I>-Bessel function of index I<nu> and
argument C<x>. If C<x> converts to a power series, the initial factor
C<(x/2)^>F<nu>C</>F<Gamma>C<(>F<nu>C<+1)> is omitted (since it cannot be represented in PARI
when F<nu> is not integral).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ibessel>C<(GEN nu, GEN x, long prec)>X<ibessel>.

=head2 X<besselj>besseljC<(>I<nu>C<,x)>

X<Label se:besselj>C<J>-Bessel function of index I<nu> and
argument C<x>. If C<x> converts to a power series, the initial factor
C<(x/2)^>F<nu>C</>F<Gamma>C<(>F<nu>C<+1)> is omitted (since it cannot be represented in PARI
when F<nu> is not integral).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<jbessel>C<(GEN nu, GEN x, long prec)>X<jbessel>.

=head2 X<besseljh>besseljhC<(n,x)>

X<Label se:besseljh>C<J>-Bessel function of half integral index.
More precisely, C<besseljh(n,x)> computes C<J_{n+1/2}(x)> where C<n>
must be of type integer, and C<x> is any element of B<C>. In the
present version B<2.2.0>, this function is not very accurate when C<x> is small.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<jbesselh>C<(GEN n, GEN x, long prec)>X<jbesselh>.

=head2 X<besselk>besselkC<(>I<nu>C<,x)>

X<Label se:besselk>C<K>-Bessel function of index I<nu> and argument C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<kbessel>C<(GEN nu, GEN x, long prec)>X<kbessel>.

=head2 X<besseln>besselnC<(>I<nu>C<,x)>

X<Label se:besseln>C<N>-Bessel function of index I<nu> and argument C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<nbessel>C<(GEN nu, GEN x, long prec)>X<nbessel>.

=head2 X<cos>cosC<(x)>

X<Label se:cos>cosine of C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gcos>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gcos>.

=head2 X<cosh>coshC<(x)>

X<Label se:cosh>hyperbolic cosine of C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gch>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gch>.

=head2 X<cotan>cotanC<(x)>

X<Label se:cotan>cotangent of C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gcotan>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gcotan>.

=head2 X<dilog>dilogC<(x)>

X<Label se:dilog>principal branch of the dilogarithm of C<x>,
i.e.S< >analytic continuation of the power series  F<log> C<_2(x) = >F<sum>C<_{n E<gt>= 1}x^n/n^2>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<dilog>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<dilog>.

=head2 X<eint1>eint1C<(x,{n})>

X<Label se:eint1>exponential integral F<int>C<_x^ oo (e^{-t})/(t)dt> (C<x belongs to >B<R>)

If C<n> is present, outputs the C<n>-dimensional vector
C<[eint1(x),...,eint1(nx)]> (C<x E<gt>= 0>). This is faster than
repeatedly calling C<eint1(i * x)>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<veceint1>C<(GEN x, GEN n = NULL, long prec)>X<veceint1>.
Also available is C<GEN >B<eint1>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<eint1>.

=head2 X<erfc>erfcC<(x)>

X<Label se:erfc>complementary error function, analytic continuation of
C<(2/> F<sqrt> F<Pi>C<)>F<int>C<_x^ oo e^{-t^2}dt> (C<x belongs to >B<R>).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gerfc>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gerfc>.

=head2 X<eta>etaC<(z,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:eta>Variants of X<Dedekind>Dedekind's F<eta> function.
If I<flag>C< = 0>, return F<prod>C<_{n = 1}^ oo (1-q^n)>, where C<q> depends on C<x>
in the following way:

\item C<q = e^{2i>F<Pi>C< x}> if C<x> is a \emph{complex number} (which must then
have positive imaginary part); notice that the factor C<q^{1/24}> is
missing!

\item C<q = x> if C<x> is a C<t_PADIC>, or can be converted to a
\emph{power series} (which must then have positive valuation).

If I<flag> is non-zero, C<x> is converted to a complex number and we return the
true F<eta> function, C<q^{1/24}>F<prod>C<_{n = 1}^ oo (1-q^n)>,
where C<q = e^{2i>F<Pi>C< x}>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<eta0>C<(GEN z, long flag, long prec)>X<eta0>.

Also available is C<GEN >B<trueeta>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<trueeta> (I<flag>C< = 1>).

=head2 X<exp>expC<(x)>

X<Label se:exp>exponential of C<x>.
C<p>-adic arguments with positive valuation are accepted.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gexp>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gexp>.
For a C<t_PADIC> C<x>, the function
C<GEN >B<Qp_exp>C<(GEN x)>X<Qp_exp> is also available.

=head2 X<gamma>gammaC<(s)>

X<Label se:gamma>For C<s> a complex number, evaluates Euler's gamma
function X<gamma-function>

S<  >F<Gamma>C<(s) = >F<int>C<_0^ oo t^{s-1}> F<exp> C<(-t)dt.>

Error if C<s> is a non-positive integer, where F<Gamma> has a pole.

For C<s> a C<p>-adic number, evaluates the Morita gamma function at C<s>, that
is the unique continuous C<p>-adic function on the C<p>-adic integers
extending F<Gamma>C<_p(k) = (-1)^k >F<prod>C<_{j E<lt> k}'j>, where the prime means that C<p>
does not divide C<j>.

  ? gamma(1/4 + O(5^10))
  %1= 1 + 4*5 + 3*5^4 + 5^6 + 5^7 + 4*5^9 + O(5^10)
  ? algdep(%,4)
  %2 = x^4 + 4*x^2 + 5

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ggamma>C<(GEN s, long prec)>X<ggamma>.
For a C<t_PADIC> C<x>, the function
C<GEN >B<Qp_gamma>C<(GEN x)>X<Qp_gamma> is also available.

=head2 X<gammah>gammahC<(x)>

X<Label se:gammah>gamma function evaluated at the argument C<x+1/2>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ggamd>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<ggamd>.

=head2 X<hyperu>hyperuC<(a,b,x)>

X<Label se:hyperu>C<U>-confluent hypergeometric function with
parameters C<a> and C<b>. The parameters C<a> and C<b> can be complex but
the present implementation requires C<x> to be positive.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<hyperu>C<(GEN a, GEN b, GEN x, long prec)>X<hyperu>.

=head2 X<incgam>incgamC<(s,x,{y})>

X<Label se:incgam>incomplete gamma function. The argument C<x>
and C<s> are complex numbers (C<x> must be a positive real number if C<s = 0>).
The result returned is F<int>C<_x^ oo e^{-t}t^{s-1}dt>. When C<y> is given,
assume (of course without checking!) that C<y = >F<Gamma>C<(s)>. For small C<x>, this
will speed up the computation.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<incgam0>C<(GEN s, GEN x, GEN y = NULL, long prec)>X<incgam0>.
Also available is C<GEN >B<incgam>C<(GEN s, GEN x, long prec)>X<incgam>.

=head2 X<incgamc>incgamcC<(s,x)>

X<Label se:incgamc>complementary incomplete gamma function.
The arguments C<x> and C<s> are complex numbers such that C<s> is not a pole of
F<Gamma> and C<|x|/(|s|+1)> is not much larger than 1 (otherwise the
convergence is very slow). The result returned is F<int>C<_0^x
e^{-t}t^{s-1}dt>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<incgamc>C<(GEN s, GEN x, long prec)>X<incgamc>.

=head2 X<lngamma>lngammaC<(x)>

X<Label se:lngamma>principal branch of the logarithm of the gamma
function of C<x>. This function is analytic on the complex plane with
non-positive integers removed. Can have much larger arguments than C<gamma>
itself. The C<p>-adic C<lngamma> function is not implemented.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<glngamma>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<glngamma>.

=head2 X<log>logC<(x)>

X<Label se:log>principal branch of the natural logarithm of
C<x belongs to >B<C>C<^*>, i.e.S< >such that C<Im(log(x)) belongs to  ]->F<Pi>C<,>F<Pi>C<]>.
The branch cut lies
along the negative real axis, continuous with quadrant 2, i.e.S< >such that
 F<lim> C<_{b\to 0^+} > F<log> C< (a+bi) = > F<log> C< a> for C<a belongs to >B<R>C<^*>. The result is complex
(with imaginary part equal to F<Pi>) if C<x belongs to >B<R> and C<x E<lt> 0>. In general,
the algorithm uses the formula

S<  > F<log> C<(x)  ~  (>F<Pi>C<)/(2agm(1, 4/s)) - m > F<log> C< 2, >

if C<s = x 2^m> is large enough. (The result is exact to C<B> bits provided
C<s E<gt> 2^{B/2}>.) At low accuracies, the series expansion near C<1> is used.

C<p>-adic arguments are also accepted for C<x>, with the convention that
 F<log> C<(p) = 0>. Hence in particular  F<exp> C<(> F<log> C<(x))/x> is not in general equal to
1 but to a C<(p-1)>-th root of unity (or C<F<+->1> if C<p = 2>) times a power of C<p>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<glog>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<glog>.
For a C<t_PADIC> C<x>, the function
C<GEN >B<Qp_log>C<(GEN x)>X<Qp_log> is also available.

=head2 X<polylog>polylogC<(m,x,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:polylog>one of the different polylogarithms, depending on I<flag>:

If I<flag>C< = 0> or is omitted: C<m^th> polylogarithm of C<x>, i.e.S< >analytic
continuation of the power series C<Li_m(x) = >F<sum>C<_{n E<gt>= 1}x^n/n^m>
(C<x E<lt> 1>). Uses the functional equation linking the values at C<x> and C<1/x>
to restrict to the case C<|x| E<lt>= 1>, then the power series when
C<|x|^2 E<lt>= 1/2>, and the power series expansion in  F<log> C<(x)> otherwise.

Using I<flag>, computes a modified C<m^th> polylogarithm of C<x>.
We use Zagier's notations; let  F<Re> C<_m> denote  F<Re>  or  F<Im>  depending
on whether C<m> is odd or even:

If I<flag>C< = 1>: compute C<~ D_m(x)>, defined for C<|x| E<lt>= 1> by

S<  > F<Re> C<_m(>F<sum>C<_{k = 0}^{m-1} ((-> F<log> C<|x|)^k)/(k!)Li_{m-k}(x)
+((-> F<log> C<|x|)^{m-1})/(m!)> F<log> C<|1-x|).>

If I<flag>C< = 2>: compute C<D_m(x)>, defined for C<|x| E<lt>= 1> by

S<  > F<Re> C<_m(>F<sum>C<_{k = 0}^{m-1}((-> F<log> C<|x|)^k)/(k!)Li_{m-k}(x)
-(1)/(2)((-> F<log> C<|x|)^m)/(m!)).>

If I<flag>C< = 3>: compute C<P_m(x)>, defined for C<|x| E<lt>= 1> by

S<  > F<Re> C<_m(>F<sum>C<_{k = 0}^{m-1}(2^kB_k)/(k!)(> F<log> C<|x|)^kLi_{m-k}(x)
-(2^{m-1}B_m)/(m!)(> F<log> C<|x|)^m).>

These three functions satisfy the functional equation
C<f_m(1/x) = (-1)^{m-1}f_m(x)>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<polylog0>C<(long m, GEN x, long flag, long prec)>X<polylog0>.
Also available is
C<GEN >B<gpolylog>C<(long m, GEN x, long prec)>X<gpolylog> (I<flag> = 0).

=head2 X<psi>psiC<(x)>

X<Label se:psi>the F<psi>-function of C<x>, i.e.S< >the
logarithmic derivative F<Gamma>C<'(x)/>F<Gamma>C<(x)>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gpsi>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gpsi>.

=head2 X<sin>sinC<(x)>

X<Label se:sin>sine of C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gsin>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gsin>.

=head2 X<sinh>sinhC<(x)>

X<Label se:sinh>hyperbolic sine of C<x>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gsh>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gsh>.

=head2 X<sqr>sqrC<(x)>

X<Label se:sqr>square of C<x>. This operation is not completely
straightforward, i.e.S< >identical to C<x * x>, since it can usually be
computed more efficiently (roughly one-half of the elementary
multiplications can be saved). Also, squaring a C<2>-adic number increases
its precision. For example,

  ? (1 + O(2^4))^2
  %1 = 1 + O(2^5)
  ? (1 + O(2^4)) * (1 + O(2^4))
  %2 = 1 + O(2^4)

Note that this function is also called whenever one multiplies two objects
which are known to be \emph{identical}, e.g.S< >they are the value of the same
variable, or we are computing a power.

  ? x = (1 + O(2^4)); x * x
  %3 = 1 + O(2^5)
  ? (1 + O(2^4))^4
  %4 = 1 + O(2^6)

(note the difference between C<%2> and C<%3> above).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gsqr>C<(GEN x)>X<gsqr>.

=head2 X<sqrt>sqrtC<(x)>

X<Label se:sqrt>principal branch of the square root of C<x>, defined as  F<sqrt> C<{x} = 
> F<exp> C<(> F<log> C< x / 2)>. In particular, we have
C<Arg(sqrt(x)) belongs to  ]->F<Pi>C</2, >F<Pi>C</2]>, and if C<x belongs to >B<R> and C<x E<lt> 0>,
then the result is complex with positive imaginary part.

Intmod a prime C<p> and C<p>-adics are allowed as arguments. In that case,
the square root (if it exists) which is returned is the one whose
first C<p>-adic digit is in the interval C<[0,p/2]>. When the argument is an
intmod a non-prime (or a non-prime-adic), the result is undefined.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gsqrt>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<gsqrt>.
For a C<t_PADIC> C<x>, the function
C<GEN >B<Qp_sqrt>C<(GEN x)>X<Qp_sqrt> is also available.

=head2 X<sqrtn>sqrtnC<(x,n,{&z})>

X<Label se:sqrtn>principal branch of the C<n>th root of C<x>,
i.e.S< >such that C<Arg(sqrt(x)) belongs to  ]->F<Pi>C</n, >F<Pi>C</n]>. Intmod
a prime and C<p>-adics are allowed as arguments.

If C<z> is present, it is set to a suitable root of unity allowing to
recover all the other roots. If it was not possible, z is
