to work for every C<n> and not just those prime to the conductor, C<E> must
be a minimal Weierstrass equation. If this is not the case, use the
function C<ellminimalmodel> before using C<ellak>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<akell>C<(GEN E, GEN n)>X<akell>.

=head2 X<ellan>ellanC<(E,n)>

X<Label se:ellan>
computes the vector of the first C<n> C<a_k>
corresponding to the elliptic curve C<E>. All comments in C<ellak>
description remain valid.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<anell>C<(GEN E, long n)>X<anell>.

=head2 X<ellanalyticrank>ellanalyticrankC<(e, {>I<eps>C<})>

X<Label se:ellanalyticrank>returns the order of vanishing at C<s = 1> of the C<L>-function of the
elliptic curve C<e> and the value of the first non-zero derivative. To
determine this order, it is assumed that any value less than C<eps> is
zero. If no value of C<eps> is given, a value of half the current
precision is used.

  ? e = ellinit("11a1"); \\ rank 0
  ? ellanalyticrank(e)
  %2 = [0, 0.2538418608559106843377589233]
  ? e = ellinit("37a1"); \\ rank 1
  ? ellanalyticrank(e)
  %4 = [1, 0.3059997738340523018204836835]
  ? e = ellinit("389a1"); \\ rank 2
  ? ellanalyticrank(e)
  %6 = [2, 1.518633000576853540460385214]
  ? e = ellinit("5077a1"); \\ rank 3
  ? ellanalyticrank(e)
  %8 = [3, 10.39109940071580413875185035]

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellanalyticrank>C<(GEN e, GEN eps = NULL, long prec)>X<ellanalyticrank>.

=head2 X<ellap>ellapC<(E,{p})>

X<Label se:ellap>computes the trace of Frobenius C<a_p> for the elliptic curve C<E> and the
prime number C<p>. This is defined by the equation C<#E(>B<F>C<_p) = p+1 - a_p>,
where C<#E(>B<F>C<_p)> stands for the number of points of the curve C<E> over the
finite field B<F>C<_p>.

No checking is done that C<p> is indeed prime. C<E> must be a I<smallell> as
output by C<ellinit>, defined over B<Q>, B<Q>C<_p>, or B<F>C<_p>. The prime C<p>
may be omitted if the curve was defined over B<F>C<_p> (C<t_INTMOD>
coefficients) or B<Q>C<_p> (C<t_PADIC> coefficients). Otherwise the curve must
be defined over B<Q>, and C<p> must be explicitly given. Over B<Q> or
B<Q>C<_p>, the equation is assumed to be minimal at C<p>.

  ? E = ellinit([0,0,0,0,1]);  \\ defined over Q
  ? ellap(E, 3)  \\ 3 necessary here
  %2 = 0    \\ #E(F_3) = 3+1 - 0 = 4
  ? ellap(E, 7)
  %3 = -4   \\ #E(F_7) = 12

  ? E = ellinit([0,0,0,0,1] * Mod(1,11));  \\ defined over F_11
  ? ellap(E)       \\ no need to repeat 11
  %5 = 0
  ? ellap(E, 11)   \\ ... but it also works
  %6 = 0
  ? ellgroup(E, 13) \\ ouch, inconsistent input !
     ***   at top-level: ellap(E,13)
     ***                 ^-----------
     *** ellap: inconsistent moduli in Rg_to_Fp: 11, 13.

B<Algorithms used> If C<E/>B<F>C<_p> has CM by a principal imaginary
quadratic order we use an explicit formula (involving essentially Kronecker
symbols and Cornacchia's algorithm, hence very fast: C<O(> F<log> C< p)^2>).
Otherwise, we use Shanks-Mestre's baby-step/giant-step method, which runs in
time C<O(p^{1/4})> using C<O(p^{1/4})> storage, hence becomes unreasonable when
C<p> has about 30S< >digits. If the X<seadata>C<seadata> package is installed, the
X<SEA>C<SEA> algorithm becomes available and primes of the order of 200S< >digits
become feasible.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellap>C<(GEN E, GEN p = NULL)>X<ellap>.

=head2 X<ellbil>ellbilC<(E,>I<z1>C<,>I<z2>C<)>

X<Label se:ellbil>
if C<z1> and C<z2> are points on the elliptic
curve C<E>, assumed to be integral given by a minimal model, this function
computes the value of the canonical bilinear form on C<z1>, C<z2>:

S<  >C< ( h(E,z1+z2) - h(E,z1) - h(E,z2) ) / 2 >

where C<+> denotes of course addition on C<E>. In addition, C<z1> or C<z2>
(but not both) can be vectors or matrices.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bilhell>C<(GEN E, GEN z1, GEN z2, long prec)>X<bilhell>.

=head2 X<ellchangecurve>ellchangecurveC<(E,v)>

X<Label se:ellchangecurve>
changes the data for the elliptic curve C<E>
by changing the coordinates using the vector C<v = [u,r,s,t]>, i.e.S< >if C<x'>
and C<y'> are the new coordinates, then C<x = u^2x'+r>, C<y = u^3y'+su^2x'+t>.
C<E> must be a I<smallell> as output by C<ellinit>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellchangecurve>C<(GEN E, GEN v)>X<ellchangecurve>.

=head2 X<ellchangepoint>ellchangepointC<(x,v)>

X<Label se:ellchangepoint>
changes the coordinates of the point or
vector of points C<x> using the vector C<v = [u,r,s,t]>, i.e.S< >if C<x'> and
C<y'> are the new coordinates, then C<x = u^2x'+r>, C<y = u^3y'+su^2x'+t> (see also
C<ellchangecurve>).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellchangepoint>C<(GEN x, GEN v)>X<ellchangepoint>.
The reciprocal function C<GEN >B<ellchangepointinv>C<(GEN x, GEN ch)>X<ellchangepointinv>
inverts the coordinate change.

=head2 X<ellconvertname>ellconvertnameC<(>I<name>C<)>

X<Label se:ellconvertname>
converts an elliptic curve name, as found in the X<elldata>C<elldata> database,
from a string to a triplet C<[>I<conductor>C<, >I<isogeny class>C<,
>I<index>C<]>. It will also convert a triplet back to a curve name.
Examples:

  ? ellconvertname("123b1")
  %1 = [123, 1, 1]
  ? ellconvertname(%)
  %2 = "123b1"

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellconvertname>C<(GEN name)>X<ellconvertname>.

=head2 X<elldivpol>elldivpolC<(E,n,{v = 'x})>

X<Label se:elldivpol>C<n>-division polynomial for the curve C<E> in the
variable C<v>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<elldivpol>C<(GEN E, long n, long v = -1)>X<elldivpol>, where C<v> is a variable number.

=head2 X<elleisnum>elleisnumC<(E,k,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:elleisnum>
C<E> being an elliptic curve as
output by C<ellinit> (or, alternatively, given by a 2-component vector
C<[>F<omega>C<_1,>F<omega>C<_2]> representing its periods), and C<k> being an even
positive integer, computes the numerical value of the Eisenstein series of
weight C<k> at C<E>, namely

S<  >C<
(2i >F<Pi>C</>F<omega>C<_2)^k
\Big(1 + 2/>F<zeta>C<(1-k) >F<sum>C<_{n E<gt>= 0} n^{k-1}q^n / (1-q^n)\Big),
>

where C<q = > F<exp> C<(2i>F<Pi> F<tau>C<)> and F<tau>C< := >F<omega>C<_1/>F<omega>C<_2> belongs to the
complex upper half-plane.

When I<flag> is non-zero and C<k = 4> or 6, returns the elliptic invariants C<g_2>
or C<g_3>, such that

S<  >C<y^2 = 4x^3 - g_2 x - g_3>

is a Weierstrass equation for C<E>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<elleisnum>C<(GEN E, long k, long flag, long prec)>X<elleisnum>.

=head2 X<elleta>elletaC<(>I<om>C<)>

X<Label se:elleta>
returns the quasi-periods C<[>F<eta>C<_1,>F<eta>C<_2]>
associated to the lattice basis I<om>C< = [>F<omega>C<_1, >F<omega>C<_2]>.
Alternatively, I<om> can be an elliptic curve C<E> as output by
C<ellinit>, in which case, the quasi periods associated to the period
lattice basis C<E.omega> (namely, C<E.eta>) are returned.

  ? elleta([1, I])
  %1 = [3.141592653589793238462643383, 9.424777960769379715387930149*I]

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<elleta>C<(GEN om, long prec)>X<elleta>.

=head2 X<ellgenerators>ellgeneratorsC<(E)>

X<Label se:ellgenerators>
returns a B<Z>-basis of the free part of the
X<Mordell-Weil group>Mordell-Weil group associated to C<E>.  This function depends on the
X<elldata>C<elldata> database being installed and referencing the curve, and so
is only available for curves over B<Z> of small conductors.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellgenerators>C<(GEN E)>X<ellgenerators>.

=head2 X<ellglobalred>ellglobalredC<(E)>

X<Label se:ellglobalred>
calculates the arithmetic conductor, the global
minimal model of C<E> and the global X<Tamagawa number>Tamagawa number C<c>.
C<E> must be a I<smallell> as output by C<ellinit>, \emph{and is supposed
to have all its coefficients C<a_i> in} B<Q>. The result is a 3 component
vector C<[N,v,c]>. C<N> is the arithmetic conductor of the curve. C<v> gives the
coordinate change for C<E> over B<Q> to the minimal integral model (see
X<ellminimalmodel>C<ellminimalmodel>). Finally C<c> is the product of the local Tamagawa
numbers C<c_p>, a quantity which enters in the 
X<Birch and Swinnerton-Dyer conjecture>Birch and Swinnerton-Dyer
conjecture.X<minimal model>

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellglobalred>C<(GEN E)>X<ellglobalred>.

=head2 X<ellgroup>ellgroupC<(E,{p})>

X<Label se:ellgroup>computes the structure of the group C<E(>B<F>C<_p)  ~  >B<Z>C</d_1>B<Z>C<  x 
>B<Z>C</d_2>B<Z>, with C<d_2 | d_1>. The prime C<p> may be omitted if the curve
was defined over B<F>C<_p> (C<t_INTMOD> coefficients) or B<Q>C<_p> (C<t_PADIC>
coefficients). Otherwise the curve must be defined over B<Q>, and C<p> must
be explicitly given. Over B<Q> or B<Q>C<_p>, the equation is assumed to be
minimal at C<p>.

  ? E = ellinit([0,0,0,0,1]);  \\ defined over Q
  ? ellgroup(E, 3)  \\ 3 necessary here
  %2 = [4]    \\ cyclic
  ? ellgroup(E, 7)
  %3 = [6, 2] \\ non-cyclic

  ? E = ellinit([0,0,0,0,1] * Mod(1,11));  \\ defined over F_11
  ? ellgroup(E)   \\ no need to repeat 11
  %5 = [12]
  ? ellgroup(E, 11)   \\ ... but it also works
  %6 = [12]
  ? ellgroup(E, 13) \\ ouch, inconsistent input !
     ***   at top-level: ellgroup(E,13)
     ***                 ^--------------
     *** ellgroup: inconsistent moduli in Rg_to_Fp: 11, 13.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellgroup>C<(GEN E, GEN p = NULL)>X<ellgroup>.

=head2 X<ellheight>ellheightC<(E,x,{>I<flag>C< = 2})>

X<Label se:ellheight>global X<NE<eacute>ron-Tate height>NE<eacute>ron-Tate height of the point C<z> on the elliptic curve
C<E> (defined over B<Q>), given by a standard minimal integral model. C<E>
must be an C<ell> as output by C<ellinit>. I<flag> selects the algorithm
used to compute the Archimedean local height. If I<flag>C< = 0>, this computation
is done using sigma and theta-functions and a trick due to J.S< >Silverman. If
I<flag>C< = 1>, use Tate's C<4^n> algorithm. If I<flag>C< = 2>, use Mestre's AGM algorithm.
The latter is much faster than the other two, both in theory (converges
quadratically) and in practice.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellheight0>C<(GEN E, GEN x, long flag, long prec)>X<ellheight0>.
Also available is C<GEN >B<ghell>C<(GEN E, GEN x, long prec)>X<ghell>
(I<flag>C< = 2>).

=head2 X<ellheightmatrix>ellheightmatrixC<(E,x)>

X<Label se:ellheightmatrix>
C<x> being a vector of points, this
function outputs the Gram matrix of C<x> with respect to the NE<eacute>ron-Tate
height, in other words, the C<(i,j)> component of the matrix is equal to
C<ellbil(E,x[i],x[j])>. The rank of this matrix, at least in some
approximate sense, gives the rank of the set of points, and if C<x> is a
basis of the X<Mordell-Weil group>Mordell-Weil group of C<E>, its determinant is equal to
the regulator of C<E>. Note that this matrix should be divided by 2 to be in
accordance with certain normalizations. C<E> is assumed to be integral,
given by a minimal model.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<mathell>C<(GEN E, GEN x, long prec)>X<mathell>.

=head2 X<ellidentify>ellidentifyC<(E)>

X<Label se:ellidentify>
look up the elliptic curve C<E> (over B<Z>)
in the X<elldata>C<elldata> database and return C<[[N, M, G], C]>  where C<N>
is the name of the curve in the J.S< >E.S< >Cremona database, C<M> the minimal
model, C<G> a B<Z>-basis of the free part of the X<Mordell-Weil group>Mordell-Weil group
of C<E> and C<C> the coordinates change (see C<ellchangecurve>).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellidentify>C<(GEN E)>X<ellidentify>.

=head2 X<ellinit>ellinitC<(x,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:ellinit>
initialize an X<ell>C<ell> structure,
associated to the elliptic curve C<E>. C<E> is either a C<5>-component
vector C<[a_1,a_2,a_3,a_4,a_6]> defining the elliptic curve with Weierstrass
equation

S<  >C< Y^2 + a_1 XY + a_3 Y = X^3 + a_2 X^2 + a_4 X + a_6 >

or a string, in this case the coefficients of the curve with matching name
are looked in the X<elldata>C<elldata> database if available.

  ? E = ellinit([0,0,0,0,1]); \\ y^2 = x^3 + 1
  ? E = ellinit("36a1");      \\ the same curve, using Cremona's notations

For the time being, only curves over a prime field B<F>C<_p> and over the
C<p>-adic or real numbers (including rational numbers) are fully supported.
Other domains are only supported for very basic operations such as point
addition.

The result of X<ellinit>C<ellinit> is an X<ell>I<ell> structure by default, and
a shorter X<sell>I<sell> if I<flag>C< = 1>. Both contain the following information in
their components:

S<  >C< a_1,a_2,a_3,a_4,a_6,b_2,b_4,b_6,b_8,c_4,c_6,>F<Delta>C<,j.>

All are accessible via member functions. In particular, the discriminant is
C<E.disc>, and the C<j>-invariant is C<E.j>.

The other six components are only present if I<flag> is C<0> or omitted, in
which case the computation will be 10 (C<p>-adic) to 200 (complex) times
slower. Their content depends on whether the curve is defined over B<R> or
not:

\item When C<E> is defined over B<R>, C<E.roots> is a vector whose
three components contain the roots of the right hand side of the associated
Weierstrass equation.

S<  >C< (y + a_1x/2 + a_3/2)^2 = g(x) >

If the roots are all real, they are ordered by decreasing value. If only
one is real, it is the first component.

Then F<omega>C<_1 = >C<E.omega[1]> is the real period of C<E> (integral of
C<dx/(2y+a_1x+a_3)> over the connected component of the identity element of
the real points of the curve), and F<omega>C<_2 = >C<E.omega[2]> is a
complex period. C<E.omega> forms a basis of the
complex lattice defining C<E>, such that
F<tau>C< = (>F<omega>C<_1)/(>F<omega>C<_2)> has positive imaginary part.

C<E.eta> is a row vector containing the quasi-periods F<eta>C<_1> and
F<eta>C<_2> such that F<eta>C<_i = 2>F<zeta>C<(>F<omega>C<_i/2)>, where F<zeta> is the
Weierstrass zeta function associated to the period lattice (see
X<ellzeta>C<ellzeta>). In particular, the Legendre relation holds:
F<eta>C<_2>F<omega>C<_1 - >F<eta>C<_1>F<omega>C<_2 = 2i>F<Pi>.

Finally, C<E.area> is the volume of the complex lattice defining
C<E>.

\item When C<E> is defined over B<Q>C<_p>, the C<p>-adic valuation of C<j>
must be negative. Then C<E.roots> is the vector with a single component
equal to the C<p>-adic root of the associated Weierstrass equation
corresponding to C<-1> under the Tate parametrization.

C<E.tate> yields the three-component vector C<[u^2,u,q]>, in the
notations of Tate. If the C<u>-component does not belong to B<Q>C<_p>, it is set
to zero.

C<E.w> is Mestre's C<w> (this is technical).

For all other base fields or rings, the last six components are
arbitrarily set to zero. See also the description of member functions
related to elliptic curves at the beginning of this section.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellinit0>C<(GEN x, long flag, long prec)>X<ellinit0>.
Also available are C<GEN >B<ellinit>C<(GEN E, long prec)>X<ellinit> (I<flag>C< = 0>) and
C<GEN >B<smallellinit>C<(GEN E, long prec)>X<smallellinit> (I<flag>C< = 1>).

=head2 X<ellisoncurve>ellisoncurveC<(E,z)>

X<Label se:ellisoncurve>gives 1 (i.e.S< >true) if the point C<z> is on the elliptic curve C<E>, 0
otherwise. If C<E> or C<z> have imprecise coefficients, an attempt is made to
take this into account, i.e.S< >an imprecise equality is checked, not a precise
one. It is allowed for C<z> to be a vector of points in which case a vector
(of the same type) is returned.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellisoncurve>C<(GEN E, GEN z)>X<ellisoncurve>.
Also available is C<int >B<oncurve>C<(GEN E, GEN z)>X<oncurve> which does not
accept vectors of points.

=head2 X<ellj>elljC<(x)>

X<Label se:ellj>
elliptic C<j>-invariant. C<x> must be a complex number
with positive imaginary part, or convertible into a power series or a
C<p>-adic number with positive valuation.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<jell>C<(GEN x, long prec)>X<jell>.

=head2 X<elllocalred>elllocalredC<(E,p)>

X<Label se:elllocalred>
calculates the X<Kodaira>Kodaira type of the
local fiber of the elliptic curve C<E> at the prime C<p>.
C<E> must be a I<smallell> as output by C<ellinit>, and is assumed to have
all its coefficients C<a_i> in B<Z>. The result is a 4-component vector
C<[f,kod,v,c]>. Here C<f> is the exponent of C<p> in the arithmetic conductor of
C<E>, and C<kod> is the Kodaira type which is coded as follows:

1 means good reduction (type IC<_0>), 2, 3 and 4 mean types II, III and IV
respectively, C<4+>F<nu> with F<nu>C< E<gt> 0> means type IC<_>F<nu>;
finally the opposite values C<-1>, C<-2>, etc.S< >refer to the starred types
IC<_0^*>, IIC<^*>, etc. The third component C<v> is itself a vector C<[u,r,s,t]>
giving the coordinate changes done during the local reduction. Normally, this
has no use if C<u> is 1, that is, if the given equation was already minimal.
Finally, the last component C<c> is the local X<Tamagawa number>Tamagawa number C<c_p>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<elllocalred>C<(GEN E, GEN p)>X<elllocalred>.

=head2 X<elllog>elllogC<(E,P,G,{o})>

X<Label se:elllog>discrete logarithm of the point C<P> of the elliptic curve C<E> in base C<G>.
See X<znlog>C<znlog> for the limitations of the underlying discrete log algorithms.
If present, C<o> represents the order of C<G>, see L<Label se:DLfun>;
the preferred format for this parameter is C<[N, factor(N)]>, where C<N>
is  the order of C<G>.

If no C<o> is given, assume that C<G> generates the curve.
The function also assumes that C<P> is a multiple of C<G>.

  ? a = ffgen(ffinit(2,8),'a);
  ? E = ellinit([a,1,0,0,1]);  \\ over F_{2^8}
  ? x = a^3; y = ellordinate(E,x)[1];
  ? P = [x,y]; G = ellpow(E, P, 113);
  ? ord = [242, factor(242)]; \\ P generates a group of order 242. Initialize.
  ? ellorder(E, G, ord)
  %4 = 242
  ? e = elllog(E, P, G, ord)
  %5 = 15
  ? ellpow(E,G,e) == P
  %6 = 1

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<elllog>C<(GEN E, GEN P, GEN G, GEN o = NULL)>X<elllog>.

=head2 X<elllseries>elllseriesC<(E,s,{A = 1})>

X<Label se:elllseries>
C<E> being an I<sell> as output by
C<ellinit>, this computes the value of the L-series of C<E> at C<s>. It is
assumed that C<E> is defined over B<Q>, not necessarily minimal. The optional
parameter C<A> is a cutoff point for the integral, which must be chosen close
to 1 for best speed. The result must be independent of C<A>, so this allows
some internal checking of the function.

Note that if the conductor of the curve is large, say greater than C<10^{12}>,
this function will take an unreasonable amount of time since it uses an
C<O(N^{1/2})> algorithm.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<elllseries>C<(GEN E, GEN s, GEN A = NULL, long prec)>X<elllseries>.

=head2 X<ellminimalmodel>ellminimalmodelC<(E,{&v})>

X<Label se:ellminimalmodel>return the standard minimal integral model of the rational elliptic
curve C<E>. If present, sets C<v> to the corresponding change of variables,
which is a vector C<[u,r,s,t]> with rational components. The return value is
identical to that of C<ellchangecurve(E, v)>.

The resulting model has integral coefficients, is everywhere minimal, C<a_1>
is 0 or 1, C<a_2> is 0, 1 or C<-1> and C<a_3> is 0 or 1. Such a model is
unique, and the vector C<v> is unique if we specify that C<u> is positive,
which we do. X<minimal model>

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellminimalmodel>C<(GEN E, GEN *v = NULL)>X<ellminimalmodel>.

=head2 X<ellmodulareqn>ellmodulareqnC<(l,{x},{y})>

X<Label se:ellmodulareqn>return a vector [C<eqn>,C<t>] where C<eqn> is a modular equation of
level C<l>, for C<l E<lt> 500>, C<l> prime. This requires the package seadata to be
installed. The equation is either of canonical type (C<t = 0>) or of Atkin type
(C<t = 1>).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellmodulareqn>C<(long l, long x = -1, long y = -1)>X<ellmodulareqn>, where C<x>, C<y> are variable numbers.

=head2 X<ellorder>ellorderC<(E,z,{o})>

X<Label se:ellorder>
gives the order of the point C<z> on the elliptic
curve C<E>. If the curve is defined over B<Q>, return zero if the point has
infinite order. The parameter C<o>, if present, represents a non-zero
multiple of the order of C<z>, see L<Label se:DLfun>; the preferred format for
this parameter is C<[ord, factor(ord)]>, where C<ord> is the
cardinality of the curve.

For a curve defined over B<F>C<_p>, it is very important to supply C<o> since
its computation is very expensive and should only be done once, using

  ? N = p+1-ellap(E,p); o = [N, factor(N)];

possibly using the X<seadata>C<seadata> package; for a curve defined
over a non-prime finite field,
giving the order is \emph{mandatory} since no function is available yet to
compute the cardinality or trace of Frobenius in that case.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellorder>C<(GEN E, GEN z, GEN o = NULL)>X<ellorder>.
The obsolete form C<GEN >B<orderell>C<(GEN e, GEN z)>X<orderell> should no longer be
used.

=head2 X<ellordinate>ellordinateC<(E,x)>

X<Label se:ellordinate>
gives a 0, 1 or 2-component vector containing
the C<y>-coordinates of the points of the curve C<E> having C<x> as
C<x>-coordinate.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellordinate>C<(GEN E, GEN x, long prec)>X<ellordinate>.

=head2 X<ellpointtoz>ellpointtozC<(E,P)>

X<Label se:ellpointtoz>
if C<E> is an elliptic curve with coefficients
in B<R>, this computes a complex number C<t> (modulo the lattice defining
C<E>) corresponding to the point C<z>, i.e.S< >such that, in the standard
Weierstrass model,  F<wp> C<(t) = z[1],> F<wp> C<'(t) = z[2]>. In other words, this is the
inverse function of C<ellztopoint>. More precisely, if C<(w1,w2)> are the
real and complex periods of C<E>, C<t> is such that C<0 E<lt>= > F<Re> C<(t) E<lt> w1>
and C<0 E<lt>= > F<Im> C<(t) E<lt> > F<Im> C<(w2)>.

If C<E> has coefficients in B<Q>C<_p>, then either Tate's C<u> is in B<Q>C<_p>, in
which case the output is a C<p>-adic number C<t> corresponding to the point C<z>
under the Tate parametrization, or only its square is, in which case the
output is C<t+1/t>. C<E> must be an I<ell> as output by C<ellinit>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<zell>C<(GEN E, GEN P, long prec)>X<zell>.

=head2 X<ellpow>ellpowC<(E,z,n)>

X<Label se:ellpow>
computes C<[n]z>, where C<z> is a point on the elliptic curve C<E>. The
exponent C<n> is in B<Z>, or may be a complex quadratic integer if the curve C<E>
has complex multiplication by C<n> (if not, an error message is issued).

  ? Ei = ellinit([0,0,0,1,0]); z = [0,0];
  ? ellpow(Ei, z, 10)
  %2 = [0]     \\ unsurprising: z has order 2
  ? ellpow(Ei, z, I)
  %3 = [0, 0]  \\ Ei has complex multiplication by Z[i]
  ? ellpow(Ei, z, quadgen(-4))
  %4 = [0, 0]  \\ an alternative syntax for the same query
  ? Ej  = ellinit([0,0,0,0,1]); z = [-1,0];
  ? ellpow(Ej, z, I)
    ***   at top-level: ellpow(Ej,z,I)
    ***                 ^--------------
    *** ellpow: not a complex multiplication in powell.
  ? ellpow(Ej, z, 1+quadgen(-3))
  %6 = [1 - w, 0]

The simple-minded algorithm for the CM case assumes that we are in
characteristic C<0>, and that the quadratic order to which C<n> belongs has
small discriminant.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<powell>C<(GEN E, GEN z, GEN n)>X<powell>.

=head2 X<ellrootno>ellrootnoC<(E,{p = 1})>

X<Label se:ellrootno>
C<E> being a I<smallell> as output by
C<ellinit>, this computes the local (if C<p ! = 1>) or global (if C<p = 1>)
root number of the L-series of the elliptic curve C<E>. Note that the global
root number is the sign of the functional equation and conjecturally is the
parity of the rank of the X<Mordell-Weil group>Mordell-Weil group. The equation for C<E> must
have coefficients in B<Q> but need \emph{not} be minimal.

The library syntax is C<long >B<ellrootno>C<(GEN E, GEN p = NULL)>X<ellrootno>.

=head2 X<ellsearch>ellsearchC<(N)>

X<Label se:ellsearch>if C<N> is an integer, it is taken as a conductor else if C<N> is a
string, it can be a curve name ("11a1"), an isogeny class ("11a") or a
conductor "11". This function finds all curves in the X<elldata>C<elldata> database
with the given property.

If C<N> is a full curve name, the output format is C<[N, [a_1,a_2,a_3,a_4,a_6],
G]> where C<[a_1,a_2,a_3,a_4,a_6]> are the coefficients of the Weierstrass
equation of the curve and C<G> is a B<Z>-basis of the free part of the
X<Mordell-Weil group>Mordell-Weil group associated to the curve.

If C<N> is not a full-curve name, the output is a vector of all matching
curves in the above format.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellsearch>C<(GEN N)>X<ellsearch>.
Also available is C<GEN >B<ellsearchcurve>C<(GEN N)>X<ellsearchcurve> that only
accepts complete curve names.

=head2 X<ellsigma>ellsigmaC<(E,z,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:ellsigma>
C<E> being given by C<ellinit>,
returns the value at C<z> of the Weierstrass F<sigma> function of the period
lattice C<L> of C<E>:

S<  > F<sigma>C<(z, L) = z >F<prod>C<_{>F<omega>C< belongs to L^*} (1 -
(z)/(>F<omega>C<))e^{(z)/(>F<omega>C<) + (z^2)/(2>F<omega>C<^2)}>

Alternatively, one can input a lattice basis C<[>F<omega>C<_1,>F<omega>C<_2]> directly
instead of C<E>.

If I<flag>C< = 1>, computes an (arbitrary) determination of  F<log> C<(>F<sigma>C<(z))>.

If I<flag>C< = 2,3>, same using the product expansion instead of theta series.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellsigma>C<(GEN E, GEN z, long flag, long prec)>X<ellsigma>.

=head2 X<ellsub>ellsubC<(E,>I<z1>C<,>I<z2>C<)>

X<Label se:ellsub>
difference of the points C<z1> and C<z2> on the
elliptic curve corresponding to C<E>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<subell>C<(GEN E, GEN z1, GEN z2)>X<subell>.

=head2 X<elltaniyama>elltaniyamaC<(E, {d = >I<seriesprecision>C<})>

X<Label se:elltaniyama>
computes the modular parametrization of the
elliptic curve C<E>, where C<E> is a I<smallell> as output by C<ellinit>,
in the form of a two-component vector C<[u,v]> of power series, given to C<d>
significant terms (X<seriesprecision>C<seriesprecision> by default). This vector is
characterized by the
following two properties. First the point C<(x,y) = (u,v)> satisfies the
equation of the elliptic curve. Second, the differential C<du/(2v+a_1u+a_3)>
is equal to C<f(z)dz>, a differential form on C<H/>F<Gamma>C<_0(N)> where C<N> is the
conductor of the curve. The variable used in the power series for C<u> and C<v>
is C<x>, which is implicitly understood to be equal to  F<exp> C<(2i>F<Pi>C< z)>. It is
assumed that the curve is a \emph{strong} X<Weil curve>Weil curve, and that the
Manin constant is equal to 1. The equation of the curve C<E> must be minimal
(use C<ellminimalmodel> to get a minimal equation).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<elltaniyama>C<(GEN E, long precdl)>X<elltaniyama>.

=head2 X<elltatepairing>elltatepairingC<(E, P, Q, m)>

X<Label se:elltatepairing>Computes the Tate pairing of the two points C<P> and C<Q> on the elliptic
curve C<E>. The point C<P> must be of C<m>-torsion.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<elltatepairing>C<(GEN E, GEN P, GEN Q, GEN m)>X<elltatepairing>.

=head2 X<elltors>elltorsC<(E,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:elltors>
if C<E> is an elliptic curve \emph{defined
over B<Q>}, outputs the torsion subgroup of C<E> as a 3-component vector
C<[t,v1,v2]>, where C<t> is the order of the torsion group, C<v1>
gives the structure of the torsion group as a product of cyclic groups
(sorted by decreasing order), and C<v2> gives generators for these cyclic
groups. C<E> must be an I<ell> as output by C<ellinit>.

  ?  E = ellinit([0,0,0,-1,0]);
  ?  elltors(E)
  %1 = [4, [2, 2], [[0, 0], [1, 0]]]

Here, the torsion subgroup is isomorphic to B<Z>C</2>B<Z>C<  x >B<Z>C</2>B<Z>, with
generators C<[0,0]> and C<[1,0]>.

If I<flag>C< = 0>, use Doud's algorithm: bound torsion by computing C<#E(>B<F>C<_p)>
for small primes of good reduction, then look for torsion points using
Weierstrass parametrization (and Mazur's classification).

If I<flag>C< = 1>, use Lutz-Nagell (\emph{much} slower), C<E> is allowed to be a
I<smallell>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<elltors0>C<(GEN E, long flag)>X<elltors0>.
Also available is C<GEN >B<elltors>C<(GEN E)>X<elltors> for C<elltors(E, 0)>.

=head2 X<ellweilpairing>ellweilpairingC<(E, P, Q, m)>

X<Label se:ellweilpairing>Computes the Weil pairing of the two points of C<m>-torsion C<P> and C<Q>
on the elliptic curve C<E>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellweilpairing>C<(GEN E, GEN P, GEN Q, GEN m)>X<ellweilpairing>.

=head2 X<ellwp>ellwpC<(E,{z = x},{>I<flag>C< = 0},{d = >I<seriesprecision>C<})>

X<Label se:ellwp>Computes the value at C<z> of the Weierstrass  F<wp>  function attached to
the elliptic curve C<E> as given by C<ellinit> (alternatively, C<E> can be
given as a lattice C<[>F<omega>C<_1,>F<omega>C<_2]>).

If C<z> is omitted or is a simple variable, computes the \emph{power series}
expansion in C<z> (starting C<z^{-2}+O(z^2)>). The series is given to C<d>
significant terms (X<seriesprecision>C<seriesprecision> by default).

Optional I<flag> is (for now) only taken into account when C<z> is numeric, and
means 0: compute only  F<wp> C<(z)>, 1: compute C<[> F<wp> C<(z),> F<wp> C<'(z)]>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellwp0>C<(GEN E, GEN z = NULL, long flag, long precdl, long prec)>X<ellwp0>.
Also available is C<GEN >B<weipell>C<(GEN E, long precdl)>X<weipell> for the power
series.

=head2 X<ellzeta>ellzetaC<(E,z)>

X<Label se:ellzeta>
C<E> being given by C<ellinit>, returns the
value at C<z> of the Weierstrass F<zeta> function of the period lattice C<L>
of C<E>:

S<  > F<zeta>C<(z, L) = (1)/(z) + z^2>F<sum>C<_{>F<omega>C< belongs to L^*}
(1)/(>F<omega>C<^2(z->F<omega>C<)).>

Alternatively, one can input a lattice basis C<[>F<omega>C<_1,>F<omega>C<_2]> directly
instead of C<E>.

  ? e = ellinit([0,0,0,1,0]);
  ? ellzeta(e, e.omega[1]/2)
  %2 = 0.8472130847939790866064991234 + 4.417621070 E-29*I
  ? 2*ellzeta([1,I], 1/2)
  %3 = 3.141592653589793238462643384 + 0.E-37*I

The quasi-periods of F<zeta>, such that

S<  >F<zeta>C<(z + a>F<omega>C<_1 + b>F<omega>C<_2) = >F<zeta>C<(z) + a>F<eta>C<_1 + b>F<eta>C<_2 >

for integers C<a> and C<b> are obtained directly as F<eta>C<_i = 
2>F<zeta>C<(>F<omega>C<_i/2)> or using X<elleta>C<elleta>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<ellzeta>C<(GEN E, GEN z, long prec)>X<ellzeta>.

=head2 X<ellztopoint>ellztopointC<(E,z)>

X<Label se:ellztopoint>
C<E> being an I<ell> as output by
C<ellinit>, computes the coordinates C<[x,y]> on the curve C<E>
corresponding to the complex number C<z>. Hence this is the inverse function
of C<ellpointtoz>. In other words, if the curve is put in Weierstrass
form, C<[x,y]> represents the X<Weierstrass > F<wp> X<-function>Weierstrass  F<wp> -function and its
derivative. If C<z> is in the lattice defining C<E> over B<C>, the result is
the point at infinity C<[0]>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<pointell>C<(GEN E, GEN z, long prec)>X<pointell>.

=head1 Functions related to general number fields

In this section can be found functions which are used almost exclusively for
working in general number fields. Other less specific functions can be found
in the next section on polynomials. Functions related to quadratic number
fields are found in section L<Label se:arithmetic> (Arithmetic functions).

=head2 Number field structures

Let C<K = >B<Q>C<[X] / (T)> a number field, B<Z>C<_K> its ring of integers, C<T belongs to >B<Z>C<[X]>
is monic. Three basic number field structures can be associated to C<K> in
GP:

\item X<nf>I<nf> denotes a number field, i.e.S< >a data structure output by
X<nfinit>C<nfinit>. This contains the basic arithmetic data associated to the
number field: signature, maximal order (given by a basis C<nf.zk>),
discriminant, defining polynomial C<T>, etc.

\item X<bnf>I<bnf> denotes a ``Buchmann's number field'', i.e.S< >a
data structure output by X<bnfinit>C<bnfinit>. This contains
I<nf> and the deeper invariants of the field: units C<U(K)>, class group
C<\Cl(K)>, as well as technical data required to solve the two associated
discrete logarithm problems.

\item X<bnr>I<bnr> denotes a ``ray number field'', i.e.S< >a data structure
output by C<bnrinit>, corresponding to the ray class group structure of
the field, for some modulus C<f>. It contains a I<bnf>, the modulus
C<f>, the ray class group C<\Cl_f(K)> and data associated to
the discrete logarithm problem therein.

=head2 Algebraic numbers and ideals

An X<algebraic number>I<algebraic number> belonging to C<K = >B<Q>C<[X]/(T)> is given as

\item a C<t_INT>, C<t_FRAC> or C<t_POL> (implicitly modulo C<T>), or

\item a C<t_POLMOD> (modulo C<T>), or

\item a C<t_COL>S< >C<v> of dimension C<N = [K:>B<Q>C<]>, representing
the element in terms of the computed integral basis, as
C<sum(i = 1, N,S< >v[i] * nf.zk[i])>. Note that a C<t_VEC>
will not be recognized.

An X<ideal>I<ideal> is given in any of the following ways:

\item an algebraic number in one of the above forms, defining a principal ideal.

\item a prime ideal, i.e.S< >a 5-component vector in the format output by
C<idealprimedec> or C<idealfactor>.

\item a C<t_MAT>, square and in Hermite Normal Form (or at least
upper triangular with non-negative coefficients), whose columns represent a
B<Z>-basis of the ideal.

One may use C<idealhnf> to convert any ideal to the last (preferred) format.

\item an \emph{extended ideal} X<ideal (extended)> is a 2-component
vector C<[I, t]>, where C<I> is an ideal as above and C<t> is an algebraic
number, representing the ideal C<(t)I>. This is useful whenever X<idealred>C<idealred>
is involved, implicitly working in the ideal class group, while keeping track
of principal ideals. Ideal operations suitably update the principal part
when it makes sense (in a multiplicative context), e.g.S< >using C<idealmul>
on C<[I,t]>, C<[J,u]>, we obtain C<[IJ, tu]>. When it does not make sense, the
extended part is silently discarded, e.g.S< >using C<idealadd> with the above
input produces C<I+J>.

The ``principal part'' C<t> in an extended ideal may be
represented in any of the above forms, and \emph{also} as a factorization
matrix (in terms of number field elements, not ideals!), possibly the empty
matrix C<[;]> representing C<1>. In the latter case, elements stay in
factored form, or X<famat>I<famat> for \emph{fa}ctorization \emph{mat}rix, which
is a convenient way to avoid coefficient explosion. To recover the
conventional expanded form, try X<nffactorback>C<nffactorback>; but many functions
already accept I<famat>s as input, for instance X<ideallog>C<ideallog>, so
expanding huge elements should never be necessary.

=head2 Finite abelian groups

A finite abelian group C<G> in user-readable format is given by its Smith
Normal Form as a pair C<[h,d]> or triple C<[h,d,g]>.
Here C<h> is the cardinality of C<G>, C<(d_i)> is the vector of elementary
divisors, and C<(g_i)> is a vector of generators. In short,
C<G = > F<oplus> C<_{i E<lt>= n} (>B<Z>C</d_i>B<Z>C<) g_i>, with C<d_n | ... | d_2 | d_1>
and F<prod>C< d_i = h>. This information can also be retrieved as
C<G.no>, C<G.cyc> and C<G.gen>.

\item a X<character>I<character> on the abelian group
 F<oplus> C< (>B<Z>C</d_i>B<Z>C<) g_i>
is given by a row vector F<chi>C< = [a_1,...,a_n]> such that
F<chi>C<(>F<prod>C< g_i^{n_i}) = > F<exp> C<(2i>F<Pi>F<sum>C< a_i n_i / d_i)>.

\item given such a structure, a X<subgroup>I<subgroup> C<H> is input as a square
matrix in HNF, whose columns express generators of C<H> on the given generators
C<g_i>. Note that the determinant of that matrix is equal to the index C<(G:H)>.

=head2 Relative extensions

We now have a look at data structures associated to relative extensions
of number fields C<L/K>, and to projective B<Z>C<_K>-modules. When defining a
relative extension C<L/K>, the I<nf> associated to the base field C<K>
must be defined by a variable having a lower priority (see
L<Label se:priority>) than the variable defining the extension. For example,
you may use the variable name C<y> to define the base field C<K>, and C<x> to
define the relative extension C<L/K>.

=item Basic definitions\label{se

ZKmodules}

\item X<rnf>I<rnf> denotes a relative number field, i.e.S< >a data structure
output by C<rnfinit>, associated to the extension C<L/K>. The I<nf>
associated to be base field C<K> is C<rnf.nf>.

\item A \emph{relative matrix} is an C<m x n> matrix whose entries are
elements of C<K>, in any form. Its C<m> columns C<A_j> represent elements
in C<K^n>.

\item An X<ideal list>I<ideal list> is a row vector of fractional ideals of the number
field I<nf>.

\item A X<pseudo-matrix>I<pseudo-matrix> is a 2-component row vector C<(A,I)> where C<A>
is a relative C<m x n> matrix and C<I> an ideal list of length C<n>. If C<I = 
{{>B<a>C<}_1,..., {>B<a>C<}_n}> and the columns of C<A> are C<(A_1,...,
A_n)>, this data defines the torsion-free (projective) B<Z>C<_K>-module C<{>B<a>C<}_1 A_1> F<oplus> C< {>B<a>C<}_n A_n>.

\item An X<integral pseudo-matrix>I<integral pseudo-matrix> is a 3-component row vector wC<(A,I,J)>
where C<A = (a_{i,j})> is an C<m x n> relative matrix and C<I = ({>B<b>C<}_1,..., {>B<b>C<}_m)>, C<J = ({>B<a>C<}_1,..., {>B<a>C<}_n)> are ideal
lists, such that C<a_{i,j} belongs to {>B<b>C<}_i {>B<a>C<}_j^{-1}> for all C<i,j>. This
data defines two abstract projective B<Z>C<_K>-modules C<N = {>B<a>C<_1}>F<omega>C<_1> F<oplus> C<...> F<oplus> C< {>B<a>C<_n}>F<omega>C<_n > in C<K^n>, C<P = {>B<b>C<_1}>F<eta>C<_1> F<oplus> C<...> F<oplus> C< {>B<b>C<_m}>F<eta>C<_m> in C<K^m>, and a B<Z>C<_K>-linear
map C<f:N\to P> given by

S<  >C< f(>F<sum> F<alpha>C<_j>F<omega>C<_j) = >F<sum>C<_i \Big(a_{i,j}>F<alpha>C<_j\Big) >F<eta>C<_i.>

This data defines the B<Z>C<_K>-module C<M = P/f(N)>.

\item Any \emph{projective} B<Z>C<_K>-module\varsidx{projective module} C<M>
of finite type in C<K^m> can be given by a pseudo matrix C<(A,I)>.

\item An arbitrary B<Z>C<_K> modules of finite type in C<K^m>, with non-trivial
torsion, is given by an integral pseudo-matrix C<(A,I,J)>

=item Pseudo-bases, determinant

\item The pair C<(A,I)> is a X<pseudo-basis>I<pseudo-basis> of the module it
generates if the C<{>B<a>C<_j}> are non-zero, and the C<A_j> are C<K>-linearly
independent. We call C<n> the \emph{size} of the pseudo-basis. If C<A> is a
relative matrix, the latter condition means it is square with non-zero
determinant; we say that it is in Hermite Normal Form
X<Hermite normal form> (HNF) if it is upper triangular and all the elements of the diagonal
are equal to 1.

\item For instance, the relative integer basis C<rnf.zk> is a pseudo-basis
C<(A,I)> of B<Z>C<_L>, where C<A = rnf.zk[1]> is a vector of elements of C<L>,
which are C<K>-linearly independent. Most I<rnf> routines return and handle
B<Z>C<_K>-modules contained in C<L> (e.g.S< >B<Z>C<_L>-ideals) via a pseudo-basis
C<(A',I')>, where C<A'> is a relative matrix representing a vector of elements of
C<L> in terms of the fixed basis C<rnf.zk[1]>

\item The \emph{determinant} of a pseudo-basis C<(A,I)> is the ideal
equal to the product of the determinant of C<A> by all the ideals of C<I>. The
determinant of a pseudo-matrix is the determinant of any pseudo-basis of the
module it generates.

=head2 Class field theory

X<Label se:CFT>
A X<modulus>I<modulus>, in the sense of class field theory, is a divisor supported
on the non-complex places of C<K>. In PARI terms, this means either an
ordinary ideal C<I> as above (no Archimedean component), or a pair C<[I,a]>,
where C<a> is a vector with C<r_1> C<{0,1}>-components, corresponding to the
infinite part of the divisor. More precisely, the C<i>-th component of C<a>
corresponds to the real embedding associated to the C<i>-th real root of
C<K.roots>. (That ordering is not canonical, but well defined once a
defining polynomial for C<K> is chosen.) For instance, C<[1, [1,1]]> is a
modulus for a real quadratic field, allowing ramification at any of the two
places at infinity, and nowhere else.

A X<bid>I<bid> or ``big ideal'' is a structure output by C<idealstar>
needed to compute in C<(>B<Z>C<_K/I)^*>, where C<I> is a modulus in the above sense.
It is a finite abelian group as described above, supplemented by
technical data needed to solve discrete log problems.

Finally we explain how to input ray number fields (or I<bnr>), using class
field theory. These are defined by a triple C<A>, C<B>, C<C>, where the
defining set C<[A,B,C]> can have any of the following forms: C<[>I<bnr>C<]>,
C<[>I<bnr>C<,>I<subgroup>C<]>, C<[>I<bnf>C<,>I<mod>C<]>,
C<[>I<bnf>C<,>I<mod>C<,>I<subgroup>C<]>. The last two forms are kept for
backward compatibility, but no longer serve any real purpose (see example
below); no newly written function will accept them.

\item I<bnf> is as output by C<bnfinit>, where units are mandatory
unless the modulus is trivial; I<bnr> is as output by C<bnrinit>. This
is the ground field C<K>.

\item \emph{mod} is a modulus C<\goth{f}>, as described above.

\item \emph{subgroup} a subgroup of the ray class group modulo C<\goth{f}> of
C<K>. As described above, this is input as a square matrix expressing
generators of a subgroup of the ray class group I<bnr>C<.clgp> on the
given generators.

The corresponding I<bnr> is the subfield of the ray class field of C<K>
modulo C<\goth{f}>, fixed by the given subgroup.

    ? K = bnfinit(y^2+1);
    ? bnr = bnrinit(K, 13)
    ? %.clgp
    %3 = [36, [12, 3]]
    ? bnrdisc(bnr); \\ discriminant of the full ray class field
    ? bnrdisc(bnr, [3,1;0,1]); \\ discriminant of cyclic cubic extension of K

We could have written directly

    ? bnrdisc(K, 13);
    ? bnrdisc(K, 13, [3,1;0,1]);

avoiding one X<bnrinit>C<bnrinit>, but this would actually be slower since the
C<bnrinit> is called internally anyway. And now twice!

=head2 General use

All the functions which are specific to relative extensions, number fields,
Buchmann's number fields, Buchmann's number rays, share the prefix C<rnf>,
C<nf>, C<bnf>, C<bnr> respectively. They take as first argument a
number field of that precise type, respectively output by C<rnfinit>,
C<nfinit>, C<bnfinit>, and C<bnrinit>.

However, and even though it may not be specified in the descriptions of the
functions below, it is permissible, if the function expects a I<nf>, to
use a I<bnf> instead, which contains much more information. On the other
hand, if the function requires a C<bnf>, it will \emph{not} launch
C<bnfinit> for you, which is a costly operation. Instead, it will give you
a specific error message. In short, the types

S<  >C< nf E<lt>= bnf E<lt>= bnr>

are ordered, each function requires a minimal type to work properly, but you
may always substitute a larger type.

The data types corresponding to the structures described above are rather
complicated. Thus, as we already have seen it with elliptic curves, GP
provides ``member functions'' to retrieve data from these structures (once
they have been initialized of course). The relevant types of number fields
are indicated between parentheses: 

X<member functions>
S< >X<bid>C<bid>     (I<bnr> ) :   bid ideal structure.

S< >X<bnf>C<bnf>     (I<bnr>,  I<bnf> ) :   Buchmann's number field.

S< >X<clgp>C<clgp>   (I<bnr>,  I<bnf> ) :   classgroup. This one admits the
following three subclasses:

S< >        X<cyc>C<cyc>  :     cyclic decomposition
(SNF)X<Smith normal form>.

S< >        C<gen>X<gen (member function)>  :  
generators.

S< >        X<no>C<no>   :     number of elements.

S< >X<diff>C<diff>   (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   the different ideal.

S< >X<codiff>C<codiff> (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   the codifferent
(inverse of the different in the ideal group).

S< >X<disc>C<disc>  (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   discriminant.

S< >X<fu>C<fu>    (I<bnr>,  I<bnf> ) :   X<fundamental units>fundamental units.

S< >X<index>C<index>    (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :  
X<index>index of the power order in the ring of integers.

S< >X<mod>C<mod>    (I<bnr> ) :   modulus.

S< >X<nf>C<nf>    (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   number field.

S< >X<pol>C<pol>    (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   defining polynomial.

S< >X<r1>C<r1>  (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   the number
of real embeddings.

S< >X<r2>C<r2>  (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   the number
of pairs of complex embeddings.

S< >X<reg>C<reg>   (I<bnr>,  I<bnf> ) :   regulator.

S< >X<roots>C<roots> (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   roots of the
polynomial generating the field.

S< >X<sign>C<sign>  (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   signature C<[r1,r2]>.

S< >X<t2>C<t2>    (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   the C<T_2> matrix (see
C<nfinit>).

S< >X<tu>C<tu>    (I<bnr>,  I<bnf> ) :   a generator for the torsion
units.

S< >X<zk>C<zk>    (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>,  I<nf> ) :   integral basis, i.e.S< >a
B<Z>-basis of the maximal order.

S< >X<zkst>C<zkst>    (I<bnr> ) :   structure of C<(>B<Z>C<_K/m)^*>.

B<Deprecated> The following member functions are still available, but deprecated and should not be used in new scripts :
S< >X<futu>C<futu>  (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>, ) :  
C<[u_1,...,u_r,w]>, C<(u_i)> is a vector of fundamental units,
S< >  C<w> generates the torsion units.

S< >X<tufu>C<tufu>  (I<bnr>,  I<bnf>, ) :  
C<[w,u_1,...,u_r]>, C<(u_i)> is a vector of fundamental units,
S< >  C<w> generates the torsion units.

For instance, assume that I<bnf>C< = bnfinit(>I<pol>C<)>, for some
polynomial. Then I<bnf>C<.clgp> retrieves the class group, and
I<bnf>C<.clgp.no> the class number. If we had set I<bnf>C< = 
nfinit(>I<pol>C<)>, both would have output an error message. All these
functions are completely recursive, thus for instance
I<bnr>C<.bnf.nf.zk> will yield the maximal order of I<bnr>, which
you could get directly with a simple I<bnr>C<.zk>.

=head2 Class group, units, and the GRH

X<Label se:GRHbnf>
Some of the functions starting with C<bnf> are implementations of the
sub-exponential algorithms for finding class and unit groups under X<GRH>GRH,
due to Hafner-McCurley, X<Buchmann>Buchmann and Cohen-Diaz-Olivier. The general
call to the functions concerning class groups of general number fields
(i.e.S< >excluding C<quadclassunit>) involves a polynomial C<P> and a
technical vector

S<  >I<tech>C< = [c_1, c_2, >I<nrpid>C< ],>

where the parameters are to be understood as follows:

C<P> is the defining polynomial for the number field, which must be in
B<Z>C<[X]>, irreducible and monic. In fact, if you supply a non-monic polynomial
at this point, C<gp> issues a warning, then \emph{transforms your
polynomial} so that it becomes monic. The C<nfinit> routine
will return a different result in this case: instead of C<res>, you get a
vector C<[res,Mod(a,Q)]>, where C<Mod(a,Q) = Mod(X,P)> gives the change
of variables. In all other routines, the variable change is simply lost.

The I<tech> interface is obsolete and you should not tamper with
these parameters. Indeed, from version 2.4.0 on,

\item the results are always rigorous under X<GRH>GRH (before that version,
they relied on a heuristic strengthening, hence the need for overrides).

\item the influence of these parameters on execution time and stack size is
marginal. They \emph{can} be useful to fine-tune and experiment with the
C<bnfinit> code, but you will be better off modifying all tuning
parameters in the C code (there are many more than just those three).
We nevertheless describe it for completeness.

The numbers C<c_1 E<lt>= c_2> are positive real numbers. For C<i = 1,2>, let C<B_i
= c_i(> F<log> C< |d_K|)^2>, and denote by C<S(B)> the set of maximal ideals of C<K>
whose norm is less than C<B>. We want C<S(B_1)> to generate C<\Cl(K)> and hope
that C<S(B_2)> can be \emph{proven} to generate C<\Cl(K)>.

More precisely, C<S(B_1)> is a factorbase used to compute a tentative
C<\Cl(K)> by generators and relations. We then check explicitly, using
essentially C<bnfisprincipal>, that the elements of C<S(B_2)> belong to the
span of C<S(B_1)>. Under the assumption that C<S(B_2)> generates C<\Cl(K)>, we
are done. User-supplied C<c_i> are only used to compute initial guesses for
the bounds C<B_i>, and the algorithm increases them until one can \emph{prove}
under GRH that C<S(B_2)> generates C<\Cl(K)>. A uniform result of Bach says
that C<c_2 = 12> is always suitable, but this bound is very pessimistic and a
direct algorithm due to Belabas-Diaz-Friedman is used to check the condition,
assuming GRH. The default values are C<c_1 = c_2 = 0.3>.

I<nrpid> is the maximal number of small norm relations associated to each
ideal in the factor base. Set it to C<0> to disable the search for small norm
relations. Otherwise, reasonable values are between 4 and 20. The default is
4.

B<Warning> Make sure you understand the above! By default, most of
the C<bnf> routines depend on the correctness of the GRH. In particular,
any of the class number, class group structure, class group generators,
regulator and fundamental units may be wrong, independently of each other.
Any result computed from such a C<bnf> may be wrong. The only guarantee is
that the units given generate a subgroup of finite index in the full unit
group. You must use C<bnfcertify> to certify the computations
unconditionally.

B<Remarks>

You do not need to supply the technical parameters (under the library you
still need to send at least an empty vector, coded as C<NULL>). However,
should you choose to set some of them, they \emph{must} be given in the
requested order. For example, if you want to specify a given value of
I<nrpid>, you must give some values as well for C<c_1> and C<c_2>, and provide
a vector C<[c_1,c_2,>I<nrpid>C<]>.

Note also that you can use an I<nf> instead of C<P>, which avoids
recomputing the integral basis and analogous quantities.

=head2 X<bnfcertify>bnfcertifyC<(>I<bnf>C<,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:bnfcertify>I<bnf> being as output by
C<bnfinit>, checks whether the result is correct, i.e.S< >whether it is
possible to remove the assumption of the Generalized Riemann
HypothesisX<GRH>. It is correct if and only if the answer is 1. If it is
incorrect, the program may output some error message, or loop indefinitely.
You can check its progress by increasing the debug level.

If flag is present, only certify that the class group is a quotient of the
one computed in bnf (much simpler in general).

The library syntax is C<long >B<bnfcertify0>C<(GEN bnf, long flag )>X<bnfcertify0>.
Also available is  C<GEN >B<bnfcertify>C<(GEN bnf)>X<bnfcertify> (I<flag>C< = 0>).

=head2 X<bnfcompress>bnfcompressC<(>I<bnf>C<)>

X<Label se:bnfcompress>computes a compressed version of I<bnf> (from X<bnfinit>C<bnfinit>), a
``small Buchmann's number field'' (or I<sbnf> for short) which contains
enough information to recover a full I<bnf> vector very rapidly, but
which is much smaller and hence easy to store and print. Calling
C<bnfinit> on the result recovers a true C<bnf>, in general different
from the original. Note that an X<snbf>I<snbf> is useless for almost all
purposes besides storage, and must be converted back to X<bnf>I<bnf> form
before use; for instance, no C<nf*>, C<bnf*> or member function
accepts them.

An I<sbnf> is a 12 component vector C<v>, as follows. Let C<bnf> be
the result of a full C<bnfinit>, complete with units. Then C<v[1]> is
C<bnf.pol>, C<v[2]> is the number of real embeddings C<bnf.sign[1]>,
C<v[3]> is C<bnf.disc>, C<v[4]> is C<bnf.zk>, C<v[5]> is the list of roots
C<bnf.roots>, C<v[7]> is the matrix C<W = bnf[1]>,
C<v[8]> is the matrix C<matalpha = bnf[2]>,
C<v[9]> is the prime ideal factor base C<bnf[5]> coded in a compact way,
and ordered according to the permutation C<bnf[6]>, C<v[10]> is the
2-component vector giving the number of roots of unity and a generator,
expressed on the integral basis, C<v[11]> is the list of fundamental units,
expressed on the integral basis, C<v[12]> is a vector containing the algebraic
numbers alpha corresponding to the columns of the matrix C<matalpha>,
expressed on the integral basis.

All the components are exact (integral or rational), except for the roots in
C<v[5]>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnfcompress>C<(GEN bnf)>X<bnfcompress>.

=head2 X<bnfdecodemodule>bnfdecodemoduleC<(>I<nf>C<,m)>

X<Label se:bnfdecodemodule>if C<m> is a module as output in the
first component of an extension given by C<bnrdisclist>, outputs the
true module.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<decodemodule>C<(GEN nf, GEN m)>X<decodemodule>.

=head2 X<bnfinit>bnfinitC<(P,{>I<flag>C< = 0},{>I<tech>C< = []})>

X<Label se:bnfinit>initializes a
I<bnf> structure. Used in programs such as C<bnfisprincipal>,
C<bnfisunit> or C<bnfnarrow>. By default, the results are conditional
on the GRH, see X<se:GRHbnf>se:GRHbnf. The result is a
10-component vector I<bnf>.

This implements X<Buchmann>Buchmann's sub-exponential algorithm for computing the
class group, the regulator and a system of X<fundamental units>fundamental units of the
general algebraic number field C<K> defined by the irreducible polynomial C<P>
with integer coefficients.

If the precision becomes insufficient, C<gp> does not strive to compute
the units by default (I<flag>C< = 0>).

When I<flag>C< = 1>, we insist on finding the fundamental units exactly. Be
warned that this can take a very long time when the coefficients of the
fundamental units on the integral basis are very large. If the fundamental
units are simply too large to be represented in this form, an error message
is issued. They could be obtained using the so-called compact representation
of algebraic numbers as a formal product of algebraic integers. The latter is
implemented internally but not publicly accessible yet.

I<tech> is a technical vector (empty by default, see X<se:GRHbnf>se:GRHbnf).
Careful use of this parameter may speed up your computations,
but it is mostly obsolete and you should leave it alone.

The components of a I<bnf> or I<sbnf> are technical and never used by
the casual user. In fact: \emph{never access a component directly, always use
a proper member function.} However, for the sake of completeness and internal
documentation, their description is as follows. We use the notations
explained in the book by H. Cohen, \emph{A Course in Computational Algebraic
Number Theory}, Graduate Texts in Maths B<138>, Springer-Verlag, 1993,
Section 6.5, and subsection 6.5.5 in particular.

I<bnf>C<[1]> contains the matrix C<W>, i.e.S< >the matrix in Hermite normal
form giving relations for the class group on prime ideal generators
C<(\goth{p}_i)_{1 E<lt>= i E<lt>= r}>.

I<bnf>C<[2]> contains the matrix C<B>, i.e.S< >the matrix containing the
expressions of the prime ideal factorbase in terms of the C<\goth{p}_i>.
It is an C<r x c> matrix.

I<bnf>C<[3]> contains the complex logarithmic embeddings of the system of
fundamental units which has been found. It is an C<(r_1+r_2) x (r_1+r_2-1)>
matrix.

I<bnf>C<[4]> contains the matrix C<M''_C> of Archimedean components of the
relations of the matrix C<(W|B)>.

I<bnf>C<[5]> contains the prime factor base, i.e.S< >the list of prime
ideals used in finding the relations.

I<bnf>C<[6]> used to contain a permutation of the prime factor base, but
has been obsoleted. It contains a dummy C<0>.

I<bnf>C<[7]> or I<bnf>C<.nf> is equal to the number field data
I<nf> as would be given by C<nfinit>.

I<bnf>C<[8]> is a vector containing the classgroup I<bnf>C<.clgp>
as a finite abelian group, the regulator I<bnf>C<.reg>, a C<1> (used to
contain an obsolete ``check number''), the number of roots of unity and a
generator I<bnf>C<.tu>, the fundamental units I<bnf>C<.fu>.

I<bnf>C<[9]> is a 3-element row vector used in X<bnfisprincipal>C<bnfisprincipal> only
and obtained as follows. Let C<D = U W V> obtained by applying the
X<Smith normal form>Smith normal form algorithm to the matrix C<W> ( = I<bnf>C<[1]>) and
let C<U_r> be the reduction of C<U> modulo C<D>. The first elements of the
factorbase are given (in terms of C<bnf.gen>) by the columns of C<U_r>,
with Archimedean component C<g_a>; let also C<GD_a> be the Archimedean
components of the generators of the (principal) ideals defined by the
C<bnf.gen[i]^bnf.cyc[i]>. Then I<bnf>C<[9] = [U_r, g_a, GD_a]>.

I<bnf>C<[10]> is by default unused and set equal to 0. This field is used
to store further information about the field as it becomes available, which
is rarely needed, hence would be too expensive to compute during the initial
C<bnfinit> call. For instance, the generators of the principal ideals
C<bnf.gen[i]^bnf.cyc[i]> (during a call to X<bnrisprincipal>C<bnrisprincipal>), or
those corresponding to the relations in C<W> and C<B> (when the C<bnf>
internal precision needs to be increased).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnfinit0>C<(GEN P, long flag, GEN tech = NULL, long prec)>X<bnfinit0>.

Also available is C<GEN >B<Buchall>C<(GEN P, long flag, long prec)>X<Buchall>,
corresponding to C<tech = NULL>, where
C<flag> is either C<0> (default) or X<nf_FORCE>C<nf_FORCE> (insist on finding
fundamental units). The function
C<GEN >B<Buchall_param>C<(GEN P, double c1, double c2, long nrpid, long
flag, long prec)>X<Buchall_param> gives direct access to the technical parameters.

=head2 X<bnfisintnorm>bnfisintnormC<(>I<bnf>C<,x)>

X<Label se:bnfisintnorm>computes a complete system of
solutions (modulo units of positive norm) of the absolute norm equation
C<\Norm(a) = x>,
where C<a> is an integer in I<bnf>. If I<bnf> has not been certified,
the correctness of the result depends on the validity of X<GRH>GRH.

See also X<bnfisnorm>C<bnfisnorm>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnfisintnorm>C<(GEN bnf, GEN x)>X<bnfisintnorm>.

=head2 X<bnfisnorm>bnfisnormC<(>I<bnf>C<,x,{>I<flag>C< = 1})>

X<Label se:bnfisnorm>tries to tell whether the
rational number C<x> is the norm of some element y in I<bnf>. Returns a
vector C<[a,b]> where C<x = Norm(a)*b>. Looks for a solution which is an C<S>-unit,
with C<S> a certain set of prime ideals containing (among others) all primes
dividing C<x>. If I<bnf> is known to be X<Galois>Galois, set I<flag>C< = 0> (in
this case, C<x> is a norm iff C<b = 1>). If I<flag> is non zero the program adds to
C<S> the following prime ideals, depending on the sign of I<flag>. If I<flag>C< E<gt> 0>,
the ideals of norm less than I<flag>. And if I<flag>C< E<lt> 0> the ideals dividing I<flag>.

Assuming X<GRH>GRH, the answer is guaranteed (i.e.S< >C<x> is a norm iff C<b = 1>),
if C<S> contains all primes less than C<12> F<log> C<(\disc(>I<Bnf>C<))^2>, where
I<Bnf> is the Galois closure of I<bnf>.

See also X<bnfisintnorm>C<bnfisintnorm>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnfisnorm>C<(GEN bnf, GEN x, long flag)>X<bnfisnorm>.

=head2 X<bnfisprincipal>bnfisprincipalC<(>I<bnf>C<,x,{>I<flag>C< = 1})>

X<Label se:bnfisprincipal>I<bnf> being the X<principal ideal>
number field data output by C<bnfinit>, and C<x> being an ideal, this
function tests whether the ideal is principal or not. The result is more
complete than a simple true/false answer and solves general discrete
logarithm problem. Assume the class group is  F<oplus> C< (>B<Z>C</d_i>B<Z>C<)g_i>
(where the generators C<g_i> and their orders C<d_i> are respectively given by
C<bnf.gen> and C<bnf.cyc>). The routine returns a row vector C<[e,t]>,
where C<e> is a vector of exponents C<0 E<lt>= e_i E<lt> d_i>, and C<t> is a number
field element such that

S<  >C< x = (t) >F<prod>C<_i  g_i^{e_i}.>

For \emph{given} C<g_i> (i.e. for a given C<bnf>), the C<e_i> are unique,
and C<t> is unique modulo units.

In particular, C<x> is principal if and only if C<e> is the zero vector. Note
that the empty vector, which is returned when the class number is C<1>, is
considered to be a zero vector (of dimension C<0>).

  ? K = bnfinit(y^2+23);
  ? K.cyc
  %2 = [3]
  ? K.gen
  %3 = [[2, 0; 0, 1]]          \\ a prime ideal above 2
  ? P = idealprimedec(K,3)[1]; \\ a prime ideal above 3
  ? v = bnfisprincipal(K, P)
  %5 = [[2]~, [3/4, 1/4]~]
  ? idealmul(K, v[2], idealfactorback(K, K.gen, v[1]))
  %6 =
  [3 0]

  [0 1]
  ? % == idealhnf(K, P)
  %7 = 1

The binary digits of I<flag> mean:

\item C<1>: If set, outputs C<[e,t]> as explained above, otherwise returns
only C<e>, which is much easier to compute. The following idiom only tests
whether an ideal is principal:

    is_principal(bnf, x) = !bnfisprincipal(bnf,x,0);

\item C<2>: It may not be possible to recover C<t>, given the initial accuracy
to which C<bnf> was computed. In that case, a warning is printed and C<t> is
set equal to the empty vector C<[]~>. If this bit is set,
increase the precision and recompute needed quantities until C<t> can be
computed. Warning: setting this may induce \emph{very} lengthy computations.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnfisprincipal0>C<(GEN bnf, GEN x, long flag)>X<bnfisprincipal0>.
Instead of the above hardcoded numerical flags, one should
rather use an or-ed combination of the symbolic flags X<nf_GEN>C<nf_GEN> (include
generators, possibly a place holder if too difficult) and X<nf_FORCE>C<nf_FORCE>
(insist on finding the generators).

=head2 X<bnfissunit>bnfissunitC<(>I<bnf>C<,>I<sfu>C<,x)>

X<Label se:bnfissunit>I<bnf> being output by
C<bnfinit>, I<sfu> by C<bnfsunit>, gives the column vector of
exponents of C<x> on the fundamental C<S>-units and the roots of unity.
If C<x> is not a unit, outputs an empty vector.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnfissunit>C<(GEN bnf, GEN sfu, GEN x)>X<bnfissunit>.

=head2 X<bnfisunit>bnfisunitC<(>I<bnf>C<,x)>

X<Label se:bnfisunit>I<bnf> being the number field data
output by C<bnfinit> and C<x> being an algebraic number (type integer,
rational or polmod), this outputs the decomposition of C<x> on the fundamental
units and the roots of unity if C<x> is a unit, the empty vector otherwise.
More precisely, if C<u_1>,...,C<u_r> are the fundamental units, and F<zeta>
is the generator of the group of roots of unity (C<bnf.tu>), the output is
a vector C<[x_1,...,x_r,x_{r+1}]> such that C<x = u_1^{x_1}...
u_r^{x_r}.>F<zeta>C<^{x_{r+1}}>. The C<x_i> are integers for C<i E<lt>= r> and is an
integer modulo the order of F<zeta> for C<i = r+1>.

Note that I<bnf> need not contain the fundamental unit explicitly:

  ? setrand(1); bnf = bnfinit(x^2-x-100000);
  ? bnf.fu
    ***   at top-level: bnf.fu
    ***                     ^--
    *** _.fu: missing units in .fu.
  ? u = [119836165644250789990462835950022871665178127611316131167, \
         379554884019013781006303254896369154068336082609238336]~;
  ? bnfisunit(bnf, u)
  %3 = [-1, Mod(0, 2)]~

The given C<u> is the inverse of the fundamental unit
implicitly stored in I<bnf>. In this case, the fundamental unit was not
computed and stored in algebraic form since the default accuracy was too
low. (Re-run the command at \g1 or higher to see such diagnostics.)

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnfisunit>C<(GEN bnf, GEN x)>X<bnfisunit>.

=head2 X<bnfnarrow>bnfnarrowC<(>I<bnf>C<)>

X<Label se:bnfnarrow>I<bnf> being as output by
C<bnfinit>, computes the narrow class group of I<bnf>. The output is
a 3-component row vector C<v> analogous to the corresponding class group
component I<bnf>C<.clgp> (I<bnf>C<[8][1]>): the first component
is the narrow class number C<v.no>, the second component is a vector
containing the SNFX<Smith normal form> cyclic components C<v.cyc> of
the narrow class group, and the third is a vector giving the generators of
the corresponding C<v.gen> cyclic groups. Note that this function is a
special case of C<bnrinit>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<buchnarrow>C<(GEN bnf)>X<buchnarrow>.

=head2 X<bnfsignunit>bnfsignunitC<(>I<bnf>C<)>

X<Label se:bnfsignunit>I<bnf> being as output by
C<bnfinit>, this computes an C<r_1 x (r_1+r_2-1)> matrix having C<F<+->1>
components, giving the signs of the real embeddings of the fundamental units.
The following functions compute generators for the totally positive units:

  /* exponents of totally positive units generators on bnf.tufu */
  tpuexpo(bnf)=
  { my(S,d,K);

    S = bnfsignunit(bnf); d = matsize(S);
    S = matrix(d[1],d[2], i,j, if (S[i,j] < 0, 1,0));
    S = concat(vectorv(d[1],i,1), S);   \\ add sign(-1)
    K = lift(matker(S * Mod(1,2)));
    if (K, mathnfmodid(K, 2), 2*matid(d[1]))
  }

  /* totally positive units */
  tpu(bnf)=
  { my(vu = bnf.tufu, ex = tpuexpo(bnf));

    vector(#ex-1, i, factorback(vu, ex[,i+1]))  \\ ex[,1] is 1
  }

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<signunits>C<(GEN bnf)>X<signunits>.

=head2 X<bnfsunit>bnfsunitC<(>I<bnf>C<,S)>

X<Label se:bnfsunit>computes the fundamental C<S>-units of the
number field I<bnf> (output by C<bnfinit>), where C<S> is a list of
prime ideals (output by C<idealprimedec>). The output is a vector C<v> with
6 components.

C<v[1]> gives a minimal system of (integral) generators of the C<S>-unit group
modulo the unit group.

C<v[2]> contains technical data needed by C<bnfissunit>.

C<v[3]> is an empty vector (used to give the logarithmic embeddings of the
generators in C<v[1]> in version 2.0.16).

C<v[4]> is the C<S>-regulator (this is the product of the regulator, the
determinant of C<v[2]> and the natural logarithms of the norms of the ideals
in C<S>).

C<v[5]> gives the C<S>-class group structure, in the usual format
(a row vector whose three components give in order the C<S>-class number,
the cyclic components and the generators).

C<v[6]> is a copy of C<S>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnfsunit>C<(GEN bnf, GEN S, long prec)>X<bnfsunit>.

=head2 X<bnrL1>bnrL1C<(>I<bnr>C<, {>I<subgrp>C<}, {>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:bnrL1>I<bnr> being
the number field data which is output by C<bnrinit(,,1)> and
I<subgrp> being a square matrix defining a congruence subgroup of the
ray class group corresponding to I<bnr> (the trivial congruence subgroup
if omitted), returns for each X<character>character F<chi> of the ray class group
which is trivial on this subgroup, the value at C<s = 1> (or C<s = 0>) of the
abelian C<L>-function associated to F<chi>. For the value at C<s = 0>, the
function returns in fact for each character F<chi> a vector C<[r_>F<chi>C< ,
c_>F<chi>C<]> where C<r_>F<chi> is the order of C<L(s, >F<chi>C<)> at C<s = 0> and C<c_>F<chi>
the first non-zero term in the expansion of C<L(s, >F<chi>C<)> at C<s = 0>; in other
words

S<  >C<L(s, >F<chi>C<) = c_>F<chi>C<.s^{r_>F<chi>C<} + O(s^{r_>F<chi>C< + 1})>

near C<0>. I<flag> is optional, default value is 0; its binary digits
mean 1: compute at C<s = 1> if set to 1 or C<s = 0> if set to 0, 2: compute the
primitive C<L>-functions associated to F<chi> if set to 0 or the C<L>-function
with Euler factors at prime ideals dividing the modulus of I<bnr> removed
if set to 1 (this is the so-called C<L_S(s, >F<chi>C<)> function where C<S> is the
set of infinite places of the number field together with the finite prime
ideals dividing the modulus of I<bnr>, see the example below), 3: returns
also the character. Example:

  bnf = bnfinit(x^2 - 229);
  bnr = bnrinit(bnf,1,1);
  bnrL1(bnr)

returns the order and the first non-zero term of the abelian
C<L>-functions C<L(s, >F<chi>C<)> at C<s = 0> where F<chi> runs through the
characters of the class group of B<Q>C<(> F<sqrt> C<{229})>. Then

  bnr2 = bnrinit(bnf,2,1);
  bnrL1(bnr2,,2)

returns the order and the first non-zero terms of the abelian
C<L>-functions C<L_S(s, >F<chi>C<)> at C<s = 0> where F<chi> runs through the
characters of the class group of B<Q>C<(> F<sqrt> C<{229})> and C<S> is the set
of infinite places of B<Q>C<(> F<sqrt> C<{229})> together with the finite prime
C<2>. Note that the ray class group modulo C<2> is in fact the class
group, so C<bnrL1(bnr2,0)> returns exactly the same answer as
C<bnrL1(bnr,0)>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrL1>C<(GEN bnr, GEN subgrp = NULL, long flag, long prec)>X<bnrL1>.

=head2 X<bnrclassno>bnrclassnoC<(>I<bnf>C<,I)>

X<Label se:bnrclassno>I<bnf> being as output by
C<bnfinit> (units are mandatory unless the ideal is trivial), and C<I>
being a modulus, computes the ray class number of the number field for the
modulus C<I>. One can input the associated I<bid> for C<I> instead of the
module itself, saving some time.

This function is faster than C<bnrinit> and should be used if only the
ray class number is desired. See X<bnrclassnolist>C<bnrclassnolist> if you need ray class
numbers for all moduli less than some bound.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrclassno>C<(GEN bnf, GEN I)>X<bnrclassno>.

=head2 X<bnrclassnolist>bnrclassnolistC<(>I<bnf>C<,>I<list>C<)>

X<Label se:bnrclassnolist>I<bnf> being as
output by C<bnfinit>, and I<list> being a list of moduli (with units) as
output by C<ideallist> or C<ideallistarch>, outputs the list of the
class numbers of the corresponding ray class groups. To compute a single
class number, X<bnrclassno>C<bnrclassno> is more efficient.

  ? bnf = bnfinit(x^2 - 2);
  ? L = ideallist(bnf, 100, 2);
  ? H = bnrclassnolist(bnf, L);
  ? H[98]
  %4 = [1, 3, 1]
  ? l = L[1][98]; ids = vector(#l, i, l[i].mod[1])
  %5 = [[98, 88; 0, 1], [14, 0; 0, 7], [98, 10; 0, 1]]

The weird C<l[i].mod[1]>, is the first component of C<l[i].mod>, i.e.
the finite part of the conductor. (This is cosmetic: since by construction
the Archimedean part is trivial, I do not want to see it). This tells us that
the ray class groups modulo the ideals of norm 98 (printed as C<%5>) have
respectively order C<1>, C<3> and C<1>. Indeed, we may check directly :

  ? bnrclassno(bnf, ids[2])
  %6 = 3

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrclassnolist>C<(GEN bnf, GEN list)>X<bnrclassnolist>.

=head2 X<bnrconductor>bnrconductorC<(A,{B},{C},{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:bnrconductor>conductor C<f> of the subfield of a ray class field as defined by C<[A,B,C]>
(of type C<[>I<bnr>C<]>,
C<[>I<bnr>C<, >I<subgroup>C<]>,
C<[>I<bnf>C<, >I<modulus>C<]> or
C<[>I<bnf>C<, >I<modulus>C<, >I<subgroup>C<]>,
L<Label se:CFT>)

If I<flag>C< = 0>, returns C<f>.

If I<flag>C< = 1>, returns C<[f, Cl_f, H]>, where C<Cl_f> is the ray class group
modulo C<f>, as a finite abelian group; finally C<H> is the subgroup of C<Cl_f>
defining the extension.

If I<flag>C< = 2>, returns C<[f, >I<bnr>C<(f), H]>, as above except C<Cl_f> is
replaced by a C<bnr> structure, as output by X<bnrinit>C<bnrinit(,f,1)>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrconductor0>C<(GEN A, GEN B = NULL, GEN C = NULL, long flag)>X<bnrconductor0>.

Also available is C<GEN >B<bnrconductor>C<(GEN bnr, GEN H, long flag)>X<bnrconductor>

=head2 X<bnrconductorofchar>bnrconductorofcharC<(>I<bnr>C<,>I<chi>C<)>

X<Label se:bnrconductorofchar>I<bnr> being a big
ray number field as output by C<bnrinit>, and I<chi> being a row vector
representing a X<character>character as expressed on the generators of the ray
class group, gives the conductor of this character as a modulus.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrconductorofchar>C<(GEN bnr, GEN chi)>X<bnrconductorofchar>.

=head2 X<bnrdisc>bnrdiscC<(A,{B},{C},{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:bnrdisc>C<A>, C<B>, C<C> defining a class field C<L> over a ground field C<K>
(of type C<[>I<bnr>C<]>,
C<[>I<bnr>C<, >I<subgroup>C<]>,
C<[>I<bnf>C<, >I<modulus>C<]> or
C<[>I<bnf>C<, >I<modulus>C<, >I<subgroup>C<]>,
L<Label se:CFT>), outputs data C<[N,r_1,D]> giving the discriminant and
signature of C<L>, depending on the binary digits of I<flag>:

\item 1: if this bit is unset, output absolute data related to C<L/>B<Q>:
C<N> is the absolute degree C<[L:>B<Q>C<]>, C<r_1> the number of real places of C<L>,
and C<D> the discriminant of C<L/>B<Q>. Otherwise, output relative data for C<L/K>:
C<N> is the relative degree C<[L:K]>, C<r_1> is the number of real places of C<K>
unramified in C<L> (so that the number of real places of C<L> is equal to C<r_1>
times C<N>), and C<D> is the relative discriminant ideal of C<L/K>.

\item 2: if this bit is set and if the modulus is not the conductor of C<L>,
only return 0.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrdisc0>C<(GEN A, GEN B = NULL, GEN C = NULL, long flag)>X<bnrdisc0>.

=head2 X<bnrdisclist>bnrdisclistC<(>I<bnf>C<,>I<bound>C<,{>I<arch>C<})>

X<Label se:bnrdisclist>I<bnf> being as output by C<bnfinit> (with units), computes a
list of discriminants of Abelian extensions of the number field by increasing
modulus norm up to bound I<bound>. The ramified Archimedean places are
given by I<arch>; all possible values are taken if I<arch> is omitted.

The alternative syntax C<bnrdisclist(>I<bnf>C<,>I<list>C<)> is
supported, where I<list> is as output by C<ideallist> or
C<ideallistarch> (with units), in which case I<arch> is disregarded.

The output C<v> is a vector of vectors, where C<v[i][j]> is understood to be in
fact C<V[2^{15}(i-1)+j]> of a unique big vector C<V>. (This awkward scheme
allows for larger vectors than could be otherwise represented.)

C<V[k]> is itself a vector C<W>, whose length is the number of ideals of norm
C<k>. We consider first the case where I<arch> was specified. Each
component of C<W> corresponds to an ideal C<m> of norm C<k>, and
gives invariants associated to the ray class field C<L> of I<bnf> of
conductor C<[m, >I<arch>C<]>. Namely, each contains a vector C<[m,d,r,D]> with
the following meaning: C<m> is the prime ideal factorization of the modulus,
C<d = [L:>B<Q>C<]> is the absolute degree of C<L>, C<r> is the number of real places
of C<L>, and C<D> is the factorization of its absolute discriminant. We set C<d
= r = D = 0> if C<m> is not the finite part of a conductor.

If I<arch> was omitted, all C<t = 2^{r_1}> possible values are taken and a
component of C<W> has the form C<[m, [[d_1,r_1,D_1],..., [d_t,r_t,D_t]]]>,
where C<m> is the finite part of the conductor as above, and
C<[d_i,r_i,D_i]> are the invariants of the ray class field of conductor
C<[m,v_i]>, where C<v_i> is the C<i>-th Archimedean component, ordered by
inverse lexicographic order; so C<v_1 = [0,...,0]>, C<v_2 = [1,0...,0]>,
etc. Again, we set C<d_i = r_i = D_i = 0> if C<[m,v_i]> is not a conductor.

Finally, each prime ideal C<pr = [p,>F<alpha>C<,e,f,>F<beta>C<]> in the prime
factorization C<m> is coded as the integer C<p.n^2+(f-1).n+(j-1)>,
where C<n> is the degree of the base field and C<j> is such that

C<pr = idealprimedec(>I<nf>C<,p)[j]>.

C<m> can be decoded using X<bnfdecodemodule>C<bnfdecodemodule>.

Note that to compute such data for a single field, either X<bnrclassno>C<bnrclassno>
or X<bnrdisc>C<bnrdisc> is more efficient.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrdisclist0>C<(GEN bnf, GEN bound, GEN arch = NULL)>X<bnrdisclist0>.

=head2 X<bnrinit>bnrinitC<(>I<bnf>C<,f,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:bnrinit>I<bnf> is as
output by C<bnfinit>, C<f> is a modulus, initializes data linked to
the ray class group structure corresponding to this module, a so-called
I<bnr> structure. The following member functions are available
on the result: C<.bnf> is the underlying I<bnf>,
C<.mod> the modulus, C<.bid> the I<bid> structure associated to the
modulus; finally, C<.clgp>, C<.no>, C<.cyc>, C<.gen> refer to the
ray class group (as a finite abelian group), its cardinality, its elementary
divisors, its generators.

The last group of functions are different from the members of the underlying
I<bnf>, which refer to the class group; use I<bnr>C<.bnf.>I<xxx>
to access these, e.g.S< >I<bnr>C<.bnf.cyc> to get the cyclic decomposition
of the class group.

They are also different from the members of the underlying I<bid>, which
refer to C<(>B<Z>C<_K/f)^*>; use I<bnr>C<.bid.>I<xxx> to access these,
e.g.S< >I<bnr>C<.bid.no> to get F<phi>C<(f)>.

If I<flag>C< = 0> (default), the generators of the ray class group are not computed,
which saves time. Hence I<bnr>C<.gen> would produce an error.

If I<flag>C< = 1>, as the default, except that generators are computed.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrinit0>C<(GEN bnf, GEN f, long flag)>X<bnrinit0>.
Instead the above  hardcoded  numerical flags,  one should rather use
C<GEN >B<Buchray>C<(GEN bnf, GEN module, long flag)>X<Buchray>
where flag is an or-ed combination of C<nf_GEN> (include generators)
and C<nf_INIT> (if omitted, return just the cardinal of the ray class group
and its structure), possibly 0.

=head2 X<bnrisconductor>bnrisconductorC<(A,{B},{C})>

X<Label se:bnrisconductor>C<A>, C<B>, C<C> represent
an extension of the base field, given by class field theory
(seeS< >L<Label se:CFT>). Outputs 1 if this modulus is the conductor, and 0
otherwise. This is slightly faster than C<bnrconductor>.

The library syntax is C<long >B<bnrisconductor0>C<(GEN A, GEN B = NULL, GEN C = NULL)>X<bnrisconductor0>.

=head2 X<bnrisprincipal>bnrisprincipalC<(>I<bnr>C<,x,{>I<flag>C< = 1})>

X<Label se:bnrisprincipal>I<bnr> being the
number field data which is output by C<bnrinit>C<(,,1)> and C<x> being an
ideal in any form, outputs the components of C<x> on the ray class group
generators in a way similar to C<bnfisprincipal>. That is a 2-component
vector C<v> where C<v[1]> is the vector of components of C<x> on the ray class
group generators, C<v[2]> gives on the integral basis an element F<alpha> such
that C<x = >F<alpha>F<prod>C<_ig_i^{x_i}>.

If I<flag>C< = 0>, outputs only C<v_1>. In that case, I<bnr> need not contain the
ray class group generators, i.e.S< >it may be created with C<bnrinit>C<(,,0)>
If C<x> is not coprime to the modulus of I<bnr> the result is undefined.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrisprincipal>C<(GEN bnr, GEN x, long flag)>X<bnrisprincipal>.
Instead of hardcoded  numerical flags,  one should rather
use
C<GEN >B<isprincipalray>C<(GEN bnr, GEN x)>X<isprincipalray> for C<flag = 0>, and if you
want generators:

    bnrisprincipal(bnr, x, nf_GEN)

=head2 X<bnrrootnumber>bnrrootnumberC<(>I<bnr>C<,>I<chi>C<,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:bnrrootnumber>if F<chi>C< = >I<chi> is a
X<character>character over I<bnr>, not necessarily primitive, let
C<L(s,>F<chi>C<) = >F<sum>C<_{id} >F<chi>C<(id) N(id)^{-s}> be the associated
X<Artin L-function>Artin L-function. Returns the so-called X<Artin root number>Artin root number, i.e.S< >the
complex number C<W(>F<chi>C<)> of modulus 1 such that

S<  >F<Lambda>C<(1-s,>F<chi>C<) = W(>F<chi>C<) >F<Lambda>C<(s,\overline{>F<chi>C<})>

where F<Lambda>C<(s,>F<chi>C<) = A(>F<chi>C<)^{s/2}>F<gamma>C<_>F<chi>C<(s) L(s,>F<chi>C<)> is
the enlarged L-function associated to C<L>.

The generators of the ray class group are needed, and you can set I<flag>C< = 1> if
the character is known to be primitive. Example:

  bnf = bnfinit(x^2 - x - 57);
  bnr = bnrinit(bnf, [7,[1,1]], 1);
  bnrrootnumber(bnr, [2,1])

returns the root number of the character F<chi> of
C<\Cl_{7 oo _1 oo _2}(>B<Q>C<(> F<sqrt> C<{229}))> defined by F<chi>C<(g_1^ag_2^b)
= >F<zeta>C<_1^{2a}>F<zeta>C<_2^b>. Here C<g_1, g_2> are the generators of the
ray-class group given by C<bnr.gen> and F<zeta>C<_1 = e^{2i>F<Pi>C</N_1},
>F<zeta>C<_2 = e^{2i>F<Pi>C</N_2}> where C<N_1, N_2> are the orders of C<g_1> and
C<g_2> respectively (C<N_1 = 6> and C<N_2 = 3> as C<bnr.cyc> readily tells us).

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<bnrrootnumber>C<(GEN bnr, GEN chi, long flag, long prec)>X<bnrrootnumber>.

=head2 X<bnrstark>bnrstarkC<(>I<bnr>C<,{>I<subgroup>C<})>

X<Label se:bnrstark>I<bnr> being as output by C<bnrinit(,,1)>, finds a relative equation
for the class field corresponding to the modulus in I<bnr> and the given
congruence subgroup (as usual, omit I<subgroup> if you want the whole ray
class group).

The main variable of I<bnr> must not be C<x>, and the ground field and the
class field must be totally real. When the base field is B<Q>, the vastly
simpler X<galoissubcyclo>C<galoissubcyclo> is used instead. Here is an example:

  bnf = bnfinit(y^2 - 3);
  bnr = bnrinit(bnf, 5, 1);
  bnrstark(bnr)

returns the ray class field of B<Q>C<(> F<sqrt> C<{3})> modulo C<5>. Usually, one wants
to apply to the result one of

  rnfpolredabs(bnf, pol, 16)     \\ compute a reduced relative polynomial
  rnfpolredabs(bnf, pol, 16 + 2) \\ compute a reduced absolute polynomial

The routine uses X<Stark units>Stark units and needs to find a suitable auxiliary
conductor, which may not exist when the class field is not cyclic over the
base. In this case C<bnrstark> is allowed to return a vector of
polynomials defining \emph{independent} relative extensions, whose compositum
is the requested class field. It was decided that it was more useful
to keep the extra information thus made available, hence the user has to take
the compositum herself.

Even if it exists, the auxiliary conductor may be so large that later
computations become unfeasible. (And of course, Stark's conjecture may simply
be wrong.) In case of difficulties, try X<rnfkummer>C<rnfkummer>:

  ? bnr = bnrinit(bnfinit(y^8-12*y^6+36*y^4-36*y^2+9,1), 2, 1);
  ? bnrstark(bnr)
