window, and Ilya Zakharevich who rewrote the graphic code from scratch
implementing many new primitives (splines, clipping). Nils Skoruppa and Bill
Allombert wrote the X<Qt>C<Qt> and X<fltk>C<fltk> graphic drivers respectively. E<gt>E<gt>E<gt>
suggested ideas or submitted patches for this section of the code. There are
three types of graphic functions.

=head2 High-level plotting functions

(all the functions starting with
C<ploth>) in which the user has little to do but explain what type of plot
he wants, and whose syntax is similar to the one used in the preceding
section.

=head2 Low-level plotting functions

(called I<rectplot> functions,
sharing the prefix C<plot>), where every drawing primitive (point, line,
box, etc.) is specified by the user. These low-level functions work as
follows. You have at your disposal 16 virtual windows which are filled
independently, and can then be physically ORed on a single window at
user-defined positions. These windows are numbered from 0 to 15, and must be
initialized before being used by the function C<plotinit>, which specifies
the height and width of the virtual window (called a I<rectwindow> in the
sequel). At all times, a virtual cursor (initialized at C<[0,0]>) is associated
to the window, and its current value can be obtained using the function
C<plotcursor>.

A number of primitive graphic objects (called I<rect> objects) can then
be drawn in these windows, using a default color associated to that window
(which can be changed using the C<plotcolor> function) and only the part
of the object which is inside the window will be drawn, with the exception of
polygons and strings which are drawn entirely. The ones sharing the prefix
C<plotr> draw relatively to the current position of the virtual cursor,
the others use absolute coordinates. Those having the prefix C<plotrecth>
put in the rectwindow a large batch of rect objects corresponding to the
output of the related C<ploth> function.

Finally, the actual physical drawing is done using C<plotdraw>. The
rectwindows are preserved so that further drawings using the same windows at
different positions or different windows can be done without extra work. To
erase a window, use C<plotkill>. It is not possible to partially erase a
window: erase it completely, initialize it again, then fill it with the
graphic objects that you want to keep.

In addition to initializing the window, you may use a scaled window to
avoid unnecessary conversions. For this, use C<plotscale>. As long as this
function is not called, the scaling is simply the number of pixels, the
origin being at the upper left and the C<y>-coordinates going downwards.

Plotting functions are platform independent, but a number of graphical
drivers are available for screen output: X11-windows (hence also for GUI's
based on X11 such as Openwindows and Motif), and the Qt and FLTK graphical
libraries. The physical window opened by C<plotdraw> or any of the
C<ploth*> functions is completely separated from C<gp> (technically, a
C<fork> is done, and the non-graphical memory is immediately freed in the
child process), which means you can go on working in the current C<gp>
session, without having to kill the window first. This window can be closed,
enlarged or reduced using the standard window manager functions. No zooming
procedure is implemented though (yet).

=head2 Functions for PostScript output:

in the same way that C<printtex> allows you to have a TeX output
corresponding to printed results, the functions starting with C<ps> allow
you to have X<PostScript>C<PostScript> output of the plots. This will not be identical
with the screen output, but sufficiently close. Note that you can use
PostScript output even if you do not have the plotting routines enabled. The
PostScript output is written in a file whose name is derived from the
X<psfile>C<psfile> default (C<./pari.ps> if you did not tamper with it). Each
time a new PostScript output is asked for, the PostScript output is appended
to that file. Hence you probably want to remove this file, or change the
value of C<psfile>, in between plots. On the other hand, in this manner,
as many plots as desired can be kept in a single file. 

=head2 And library mode ?

\emph{None of the graphic functions are available
within the PARI library, you must be under C<gp> to use them}. The reason
for that is that you really should not use PARI for heavy-duty graphical work,
there are better specialized alternatives around. This whole set of routines
was only meant as a convenient, but simple-minded, visual aid. If you really
insist on using these in your program (we warned you), the source
(C<plot*.c>) should be readable enough for you to achieve something.

=head2 X<plot>plotC<(X = a,b,>I<expr>C<,{>I<Ymin>C<},{>I<Ymax>C<})>

X<Label se:plot>crude ASCII plot of the function represented by expression I<expr>
from C<a> to C<b>, with I<Y> ranging from I<Ymin> to I<Ymax>. If
I<Ymin> (resp. I<Ymax>) is not given, the minima (resp. the maxima) of
the computed values of the expression is used instead.

=head2 X<plotbox>plotboxC<(w,>I<x2>C<,>I<y2>C<)>

X<Label se:plotbox>let C<(x1,y1)> be the current position of the virtual cursor. Draw in the
rectwindow C<w> the outline of the rectangle which is such that the points
C<(x1,y1)> and C<(x2,y2)> are opposite corners. Only the part of the rectangle
which is in C<w> is drawn. The virtual cursor does \emph{not} move.

=head2 X<plotclip>plotclipC<(w)>

X<Label se:plotclip>`clips' the content of rectwindow C<w>, i.e remove all parts of the
drawing that would not be visible on the screen. Together with
X<plotcopy>C<plotcopy> this function enables you to draw on a scratchpad before
committing the part you're interested in to the final picture.

=head2 X<plotcolor>plotcolorC<(w,c)>

X<Label se:plotcolor>set default color to C<c> in rectwindow C<w>.
This is only implemented for the X-windows, fltk and Qt graphing engines.
Possible values for C<c> are given by the X<graphcolormap>C<graphcolormap> default,
factory setting are

1 = black, 2 = blue, 3 = violetred, 4 = red, 5 = green, 6 = grey, 7 = gainsborough.

but this can be considerably extended.

=head2 X<plotcopy>plotcopyC<(>I<sourcew>C<,>I<destw>C<,>I<dx>C<,>I<dy>C<,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:plotcopy>copy the contents of rectwindow I<sourcew> to rectwindow I<destw>
with offset (dx,dy). If flag's bit 1 is set, dx and dy express fractions of
the size of the current output device, otherwise dx and dy are in pixels. dx
and dy are relative positions of northwest corners if other bits of flag
vanish, otherwise of: 2: southwest, 4: southeast, 6: northeast corners

=head2 X<plotcursor>plotcursorC<(w)>

X<Label se:plotcursor>give as a 2-component vector the current
(scaled) position of the virtual cursor corresponding to the rectwindow C<w>.

=head2 X<plotdraw>plotdrawC<(>I<list>C<, {>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:plotdraw>physically draw the rectwindows given in C<list>
which must be a vector whose number of components is divisible by 3. If
C<list = [w1,x1,y1,w2,x2,y2,...]>, the windows C<w1>, C<w2>, etc.S< >are
physically placed with their upper left corner at physical position
C<(x1,y1)>, C<(x2,y2)>,...respectively, and are then drawn together.
Overlapping regions will thus be drawn twice, and the windows are considered
transparent. Then display the whole drawing in a special window on your
screen. If I<flag>C<  ! = 0>, x1, y1 etc. express fractions of the size of the
current output device

=head2 X<ploth>plothC<(X = a,b,>I<expr>C<,{>I<flags>C< = 0},{n = 0})>

X<Label se:ploth>high precision plot of the function C<y = f(x)> represented by the expression
I<expr>, C<x> going from C<a> to C<b>. This opens a specific window (which is
killed whenever you click on it), and returns a four-component vector giving
the coordinates of the bounding box in the form
C<[>I<xmin>C<,>I<xmax>C<,>I<ymin>C<,>I<ymax>C<]>.

B<Important note>: C<ploth> may evaluate C<expr> thousands of
times; given the relatively low resolution of plotting devices, few
significant digits of the result will be meaningful. Hence you should keep
the current precision to a minimum (e.g.S< >9) before calling this function.

C<n> specifies the number of reference point on the graph, where a value of 0
means we use the hardwired default values (1000 for general plot, 1500 for
parametric plot, and 15 for recursive plot).

If no I<flag> is given, I<expr> is either a scalar expression C<f(X)>, in which
case the plane curve C<y = f(X)> will be drawn, or a vector
C<[f_1(X),...,f_k(X)]>, and then all the curves C<y = f_i(X)> will be drawn in
the same window.

The binary digits of I<flag> mean:

\item C<1 = Parametric>: X<parametric plot>I<parametric plot>. Here I<expr> must
be a vector with an even number of components. Successive pairs are then
understood as the parametric coordinates of a plane curve. Each of these are
then drawn.

For instance:

  ploth(X=0,2*Pi,[sin(X),cos(X)], "Parametric")
  ploth(X=0,2*Pi,[sin(X),cos(X)])
  ploth(X=0,2*Pi,[X,X,sin(X),cos(X)], "Parametric")

draw successively a circle, two entwined sinusoidal curves
and a circle cut by the line C<y = x>.

\item C<2 = Recursive>: X<recursive plot>I<recursive plot>. If this flag is set,
only \emph{one} curve can be drawn at a time, i.e.S< >I<expr> must be either a
two-component vector (for a single parametric curve, and the parametric flag
\emph{has} to be set), or a scalar function. The idea is to choose pairs of
successive reference points, and if their middle point is not too far away
from the segment joining them, draw this as a local approximation to the
curve. Otherwise, add the middle point to the reference points. This is
fast, and usually more precise than usual plot. Compare the results of

  ploth(X=-1,1, sin(1/X), "Recursive")
  ploth(X=-1,1, sin(1/X))

for instance. But beware that if you are extremely unlucky, or choose too few
reference points, you may draw some nice polygon bearing little resemblance
to the original curve. For instance you should \emph{never} plot recursively
an odd function in a symmetric interval around 0. Try

  ploth(x = -20, 20, sin(x), "Recursive")

to see why. Hence, it's usually a good idea to try and plot the same curve
with slightly different parameters.

The other values toggle various display options:

\item C<4 = no_Rescale>: do not rescale plot according to the
computed extrema. This is used in conjunction with X<plotscale>C<plotscale> when
graphing multiple functions on a rectwindow (as a X<plotrecth>C<plotrecth> call):

    s = plothsizes();
    plotinit(0, s[2]-1, s[2]-1);
    plotscale(0, -1,1, -1,1);
    plotrecth(0, t=0,2*Pi, [cos(t),sin(t)], "Parametric|no_Rescale")
    plotdraw([0, -1,1]);

This way we get a proper circle instead of the distorted ellipse produced by

    ploth(t=0,2*Pi, [cos(t),sin(t)], "Parametric")

\item C<8 = no_X_axis>: do not print the C<x>-axis.

\item C<16 = no_Y_axis>: do not print the C<y>-axis.

\item C<32 = no_Frame>: do not print frame.

\item C<64 = no_Lines>: only plot reference points, do not join them.

\item C<128 = Points_too>: plot both lines and points.

\item C<256 = Splines>: use splines to interpolate the points.

\item C<512 = no_X_ticks>: plot no C<x>-ticks.

\item C<1024 = no_Y_ticks>: plot no C<y>-ticks.

\item C<2048 = Same_ticks>: plot all ticks with the same length.

\item C<4096 = Complex>: is a parametric plot but where each member of
C<expr> is considered a complex number encoding the two coordinates of a
point. For instance:

  ploth(X=0,2*Pi,exp(I*X), "Complex")
  ploth(X=0,2*Pi,[(1+I)*X,exp(I*X)], "Complex")

will draw respectively a circle and a circle cut by the line
C<y = x>.

=head2 X<plothraw>plothrawC<(>I<listx>C<,>I<listy>C<,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:plothraw>given I<listx> and I<listy> two vectors of equal length, plots (in
high precision) the points whose C<(x,y)>-coordinates are given in
I<listx> and I<listy>. Automatic positioning and scaling is done, but
with the same scaling factor on C<x> and C<y>. If I<flag> is 1, join points,
other non-0 flags toggle display options and should be combinations of bits
C<2^k>, C<k E<gt>= 3> as in C<ploth>.

=head2 X<plothsizes>plothsizesC<({>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:plothsizes>return data corresponding to the output window
in the form of a 6-component vector: window width and height, sizes for ticks
in horizontal and vertical directions (this is intended for the C<gnuplot>
interface and is currently not significant), width and height of characters.

If I<flag>C< = 0>, sizes of ticks and characters are in
pixels, otherwise are fractions of the screen size

=head2 X<plotinit>plotinitC<(w,{x},{y},{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:plotinit>initialize the rectwindow C<w>,
destroying any rect objects you may have already drawn in C<w>. The virtual
cursor is set to C<(0,0)>. The rectwindow size is set to width C<x> and height
C<y>; omitting either C<x> or C<y> means we use the full size of the device
in that direction.
If I<flag>C< = 0>, C<x> and C<y> represent pixel units. Otherwise, C<x> and C<y>
are understood as fractions of the size of the current output device (hence
must be between C<0> and C<1>) and internally converted to pixels.

The plotting device imposes an upper bound for C<x> and C<y>, for instance the
number of pixels for screen output. These bounds are available through the
X<plothsizes>C<plothsizes> function. The following sequence initializes in a portable
way (i.e independent of the output device) a window of maximal size, accessed
through coordinates in the C<[0,1000]  x [0,1000]> range:

  s = plothsizes();
  plotinit(0, s[1]-1, s[2]-1);
  plotscale(0, 0,1000, 0,1000);

=head2 X<plotkill>plotkillC<(w)>

X<Label se:plotkill>erase rectwindow C<w> and free the corresponding memory. Note that if you
want to use the rectwindow C<w> again, you have to use C<plotinit> first
to specify the new size. So it's better in this case to use C<plotinit>
directly as this throws away any previous work in the given rectwindow.

=head2 X<plotlines>plotlinesC<(w,X,Y,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:plotlines>draw on the rectwindow C<w>
the polygon such that the (x,y)-coordinates of the vertices are in the
vectors of equal length C<X> and C<Y>. For simplicity, the whole
polygon is drawn, not only the part of the polygon which is inside the
rectwindow. If I<flag> is non-zero, close the polygon. In any case, the
virtual cursor does not move.

C<X> and C<Y> are allowed to be scalars (in this case, both have to).
There, a single segment will be drawn, between the virtual cursor current
position and the point C<(X,Y)>. And only the part thereof which
actually lies within the boundary of C<w>. Then \emph{move} the virtual cursor
to C<(X,Y)>, even if it is outside the window. If you want to draw a
line from C<(x1,y1)> to C<(x2,y2)> where C<(x1,y1)> is not necessarily the
position of the virtual cursor, use C<plotmove(w,x1,y1)> before using this
function.

=head2 X<plotlinetype>plotlinetypeC<(w,>I<type>C<)>

X<Label se:plotlinetype>change the type of lines subsequently plotted in rectwindow C<w>.
I<type> C<-2> corresponds to frames, C<-1> to axes, larger values may
correspond to something else. C<w = -1> changes highlevel plotting. This is
only taken into account by the C<gnuplot> interface.

=head2 X<plotmove>plotmoveC<(w,x,y)>

X<Label se:plotmove>move the virtual cursor of the rectwindow C<w> to position C<(x,y)>.

=head2 X<plotpoints>plotpointsC<(w,X,Y)>

X<Label se:plotpoints>draw on the rectwindow C<w> the
points whose C<(x,y)>-coordinates are in the vectors of equal length C<X> and
C<Y> and which are inside C<w>. The virtual cursor does \emph{not} move. This
is basically the same function as C<plothraw>, but either with no scaling
factor or with a scale chosen using the function C<plotscale>.

As was the case with the C<plotlines> function, C<X> and C<Y> are allowed to
be (simultaneously) scalar. In this case, draw the single point C<(X,Y)> on
the rectwindow C<w> (if it is actually inside C<w>), and in any case
\emph{move} the virtual cursor to position C<(x,y)>.

=head2 X<plotpointsize>plotpointsizeC<(w,>I<size>C<)>

X<Label se:plotpointsize>changes the ``size'' of following points in rectwindow C<w>. If C<w = -1>,
change it in all rectwindows. This only works in the C<gnuplot> interface.

=head2 X<plotpointtype>plotpointtypeC<(w,>I<type>C<)>

X<Label se:plotpointtype>change the type of points subsequently plotted in rectwindow C<w>.
I<type>C< = -1> corresponds to a dot, larger values may correspond to
something else. C<w = -1> changes highlevel plotting. This is only taken into
account by the C<gnuplot> interface.

=head2 X<plotrbox>plotrboxC<(w,>I<dx>C<,>I<dy>C<)>

X<Label se:plotrbox>draw in the rectwindow C<w> the outline of the rectangle which is such
that the points C<(x1,y1)> and C<(x1+dx,y1+dy)> are opposite corners, where
C<(x1,y1)> is the current position of the cursor. Only the part of the
rectangle which is in C<w> is drawn. The virtual cursor does \emph{not} move.

=head2 X<plotrecth>plotrecthC<(w,X = a,b,>I<expr>C<,{>I<flag>C< = 0},{n = 0})>

X<Label se:plotrecth>writes to rectwindow C<w> the curve output of
C<ploth>C<(w,X = a,b,>I<expr>C<,>I<flag>C<,n)>. Returns a vector for the bounding box.

=head2 X<plotrecthraw>plotrecthrawC<(w,>I<data>C<,{>I<flags>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:plotrecthraw>plot graph(s) for
I<data> in rectwindow C<w>. I<flag> has the same significance here as in
C<ploth>, though recursive plot is no more significant.

I<data> is a vector of vectors, each corresponding to a list a coordinates.
If parametric plot is set, there must be an even number of vectors, each
successive pair corresponding to a curve. Otherwise, the first one contains
the C<x> coordinates, and the other ones contain the C<y>-coordinates
of curves to plot.

=head2 X<plotrline>plotrlineC<(w,>I<dx>C<,>I<dy>C<)>

X<Label se:plotrline>draw in the rectwindow C<w> the part of the segment
C<(x1,y1)-(x1+dx,y1+dy)> which is inside C<w>, where C<(x1,y1)> is the current
position of the virtual cursor, and move the virtual cursor to
C<(x1+dx,y1+dy)> (even if it is outside the window).

=head2 X<plotrmove>plotrmoveC<(w,>I<dx>C<,>I<dy>C<)>

X<Label se:plotrmove>move the virtual cursor of the rectwindow C<w> to position
C<(x1+dx,y1+dy)>, where C<(x1,y1)> is the initial position of the cursor
(i.e.S< >to position C<(dx,dy)> relative to the initial cursor).

=head2 X<plotrpoint>plotrpointC<(w,>I<dx>C<,>I<dy>C<)>

X<Label se:plotrpoint>draw the point C<(x1+dx,y1+dy)> on the rectwindow C<w> (if it is inside
C<w>), where C<(x1,y1)> is the current position of the cursor, and in any case
move the virtual cursor to position C<(x1+dx,y1+dy)>.

=head2 X<plotscale>plotscaleC<(w,>I<x1>C<,>I<x2>C<,>I<y1>C<,>I<y2>C<)>

X<Label se:plotscale>scale the local coordinates of the rectwindow C<w> so that C<x> goes from
C<x1> to C<x2> and C<y> goes from C<y1> to C<y2> (C<x2 E<lt> x1> and C<y2 E<lt> y1> being
allowed). Initially, after the initialization of the rectwindow C<w> using
the function C<plotinit>, the default scaling is the graphic pixel count,
and in particular the C<y> axis is oriented downwards since the origin is at
the upper left. The function C<plotscale> allows to change all these
defaults and should be used whenever functions are graphed.

=head2 X<plotstring>plotstringC<(w,x,{>I<flags>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:plotstring>draw on the rectwindow C<w> the String C<x> (see L<Label se:strings>), at
the current position of the cursor.

I<flag> is used for justification: bits 1 and 2 regulate horizontal alignment:
left if 0, right if 2, center if 1. Bits 4 and 8 regulate vertical
alignment: bottom if 0, top if 8, v-center if 4. Can insert additional small
gap between point and string: horizontal if bit 16 is set, vertical if bit
32 is set (see the tutorial for an example).

=head2 X<psdraw>psdrawC<(>I<list>C<, {>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:psdraw>same as C<plotdraw>, except that the output is a PostScript program
appended to the C<psfile>, and flag! = 0 scales the plot from size of the
current output device to the standard PostScript plotting size

=head2 X<psploth>psplothC<(X = a,b,>I<expr>C<,{>I<flags>C< = 0},{n = 0})>

X<Label se:psploth>same as C<ploth>, except that the output is a PostScript program
appended to the C<psfile>.

=head2 X<psplothraw>psplothrawC<(>I<listx>C<,>I<listy>C<,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:psplothraw>same as C<plothraw>, except that the output is a PostScript program
appended to the C<psfile>.

=head1 Programming in GP: control statements

X<programming>X<Label se:programming>
A number of control statements are available in GP. They are simpler and
have a syntax slightly different from their C counterparts, but are quite
powerful enough to write any kind of program. Some of them are specific to
GP, since they are made for number theorists. As usual, C<X> will denote any
simple variable name, and I<seq> will always denote a sequence of
expressions, including the empty sequence.

B<Caveat> In constructs like

      for (X = a,b, seq)

the variable C<X> is lexically scoped to the loop, leading to possibly
unexpected behavior:

      n = 5;
      for (n = 1, 10,
        if (something_nice(), break);
      );
      \\  at this point n is 5 !

If the sequence C<seq> modifies the loop index, then the loop
is modified accordingly:

      ? for (n = 1, 10, n += 2; print(n))
      3
      6
      9
      12

=head2 X<break>breakC<({n = 1})>

X<Label se:break>interrupts execution of current I<seq>, and
immediately exits from the C<n> innermost enclosing loops, within the
current function call (or the top level loop); the integer C<n> must be
positive. If C<n> is greater than the number of enclosing loops, all
enclosing loops are exited.

=head2 X<for>forC<(X = a,b,>I<seq>C<)>

X<Label se:for>evaluates I<seq>, where
the formal variable C<X> goes from C<a> to C<b>. Nothing is done if C<a E<gt> b>.
C<a> and C<b> must be in B<R>.

=head2 X<fordiv>fordivC<(n,X,>I<seq>C<)>

X<Label se:fordiv>evaluates I<seq>, where
the formal variable C<X> ranges through the divisors of C<n>
(see X<divisors>C<divisors>, which is used as a subroutine). It is assumed that
C<factor> can handle C<n>, without negative exponents. Instead of C<n>,
it is possible to input a factorization matrix, i.e. the output of
C<factor(n)>.

This routine uses C<divisors> as a subroutine, then loops over the
divisors. In particular, if C<n> is an integer, divisors are sorted by
increasing size.

To avoid storing all divisors, possibly using a lot of memory, the following
(much slower) routine loops over the divisors using essentially constant
space:

  FORDIV(N)=
  { my(P, E);

    P = factor(N); E = P[,2]; P = P[,1];
    forvec( v = vector(#E, i, [0,E[i]]),
    X = factorback(P, v)
    \\ ...
  );
  }
  ? for(i=1,10^5, FORDIV(i))
  time = 3,445 ms.
  ? for(i=1,10^5, fordiv(i, d, ))
  time = 490 ms.

=head2 X<forell>forellC<(E,a,b,>I<seq>C<)>

X<Label se:forell>evaluates I<seq>, where the formal variable C<E> ranges through all
elliptic curves of conductors from C<a> to C<b>. Th X<elldata>C<elldata> database must
be installed and contain data for the specified conductors.

X<forell>The library syntax is B<forell>C<(void *data, long (*call)(void*,GEN), GEN a, GEN b)>.

=head2 X<forprime>forprimeC<(X = a,b,>I<seq>C<)>

X<Label se:forprime>evaluates I<seq>,
where the formal variable C<X> ranges over the prime numbers between C<a> to
C<b> (including C<a> and C<b> if they are prime). More precisely, the value of
C<X> is incremented to the smallest prime strictly larger than C<X> at the end
of each iteration. Nothing is done if C<a E<gt> b>. Note that C<a> and C<b> must be in
B<R>.

  f(N) =
  {
    forprime(p = 2, N,
      print(p);
      if (p == 3, p = 6);
    )
  }
  ? f(12)
  2
  3
  7
  11

=head2 X<forstep>forstepC<(X = a,b,s,>I<seq>C<)>

X<Label se:forstep>evaluates I<seq>,
where the formal variable C<X> goes from C<a> to C<b>, in increments of C<s>.
Nothing is done if C<s E<gt> 0> and C<a E<gt> b> or if C<s E<lt> 0> and C<a E<lt> b>. C<s> must be in
B<R>C<^*> or a vector of steps C<[s_1,...,s_n]>. In the latter case, the
successive steps are used in the order they appear in C<s>.

  ? forstep(x=5, 20, [2,4], print(x))
  5
  7
  11
  13
  17
  19

=head2 X<forsubgroup>forsubgroupC<(H = G,{>I<bound>C<},>I<seq>C<)>

X<Label se:forsubgroup>evaluates I<seq> for
each subgroup C<H> of the \emph{abelian} group C<G> (given in
SNFX<Smith normal form> form or as a vector of elementary divisors),
whose index is bounded by C<B>. The subgroups are not ordered in any
obvious way, unless C<G> is a C<p>-group in which case Birkhoff's algorithm
produces them by decreasing index. A X<subgroup>subgroup is given as a matrix
whose columns give its generators on the implicit generators of C<G>. For
example, the following prints all subgroups of index less than 2 in C<G = 
>B<Z>C</2>B<Z>C< g_1  x >B<Z>C</2>B<Z>C< g_2>:

  ? G = [2,2]; forsubgroup(H=G, 2, print(H))
  [1; 1]
  [1; 2]
  [2; 1]
  [1, 0; 1, 1]

The last one, for instance is generated by C<(g_1, g_1 + g_2)>. This
routine is intended to treat huge groups, when X<subgrouplist>C<subgrouplist> is not an
option due to the sheer size of the output.

For maximal speed the subgroups have been left as produced by the algorithm.
To print them in canonical form (as left divisors of C<G> in HNF form), one
can for instance use

  ? G = matdiagonal([2,2]); forsubgroup(H=G, 2, print(mathnf(concat(G,H))))
  [2, 1; 0, 1]
  [1, 0; 0, 2]
  [2, 0; 0, 1]
  [1, 0; 0, 1]

Note that in this last representation, the index C<[G:H]> is given by the
determinant. See X<galoissubcyclo>C<galoissubcyclo> and X<galoisfixedfield>C<galoisfixedfield> for
applications to X<Galois>Galois theory.

X<forsubgroup>The library syntax is B<forsubgroup>C<(void *data, long (*call)(void*,GEN), GEN G, GEN bound)>.

=head2 X<forvec>forvecC<(X = v,>I<seq>C<,{>I<flag>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:forvec>Let C<v> be an C<n>-component
vector (where C<n> is arbitrary) of two-component vectors C<[a_i,b_i]>
for C<1 E<lt>= i E<lt>= n>. This routine evaluates I<seq>, where the formal
variables C<X[1],..., X[n]> go from C<a_1> to C<b_1>,..., from C<a_n> to
C<b_n>, i.e.S< >C<X> goes from C<[a_1,...,a_n]> to C<[b_1,...,b_n]> with respect
to the lexicographic ordering. (The formal variable with the highest index
moves the fastest.) If I<flag>C< = 1>, generate only nondecreasing vectors C<X>, and
if I<flag>C< = 2>, generate only strictly increasing vectors C<X>.

The type of C<X> is the same as the type of C<v>: C<t_VEC> or C<t_COL>.

=head2 X<if>ifC<(a,{>I<seq1>C<},{>I<seq2>C<})>

X<Label se:if>evaluates the expression sequence I<seq1> if C<a> is non-zero, otherwise
the expression I<seq2>. Of course, I<seq1> or I<seq2> may be empty:

C<if (a,>I<seq>C<)> evaluates I<seq> if C<a> is not equal to zero
(you don't have to write the second comma), and does nothing otherwise,

C<if (a,,>I<seq>C<)> evaluates I<seq> if C<a> is equal to zero, and
does nothing otherwise. You could get the same result using the C<!>
(C<not>) operator: C<if (!a,>I<seq>C<)>.

Note that the boolean operators C<&&> and C<||> are evaluated
according to operator precedence as explained in L<Label se:operators>, but
that, contrary to other operators, the evaluation of the arguments is stopped
as soon as the final truth value has been determined. For instance

  if (reallydoit && longcomplicatedfunction(), ...)

is a perfectly safe statement.

Recall that functions such as C<break> and C<next> operate on
\emph{loops} (such as C<forxxx>, C<while>, C<until>). The C<if>
statement is \emph{not} a loop (obviously!).

=head2 X<next>nextC<({n = 1})>

X<Label se:next>interrupts execution of current C<seq>,
resume the next iteration of the innermost enclosing loop, within the
current function call (or top level loop). If C<n> is specified, resume at
the C<n>-th enclosing loop. If C<n> is bigger than the number of enclosing
loops, all enclosing loops are exited.

=head2 X<return>returnC<({x = 0})>

X<Label se:return>returns from current subroutine, with
result C<x>. If C<x> is omitted, return the C<(void)> value (return no
result, like C<print>).

=head2 X<until>untilC<(a,>I<seq>C<)>

X<Label se:until>evaluates I<seq> until C<a> is not
equal to 0 (i.e.S< >until C<a> is true). If C<a> is initially not equal to 0,
I<seq> is evaluated once (more generally, the condition on C<a> is tested
\emph{after} execution of the I<seq>, not before as in C<while>).

=head2 X<while>whileC<(a,>I<seq>C<)>

X<Label se:while>while C<a> is non-zero, evaluates the expression sequence I<seq>. The
test is made \emph{before} evaluating the C<seq>, hence in particular if C<a>
is initially equal to zero the I<seq> will not be evaluated at all.

=head1 Programming in GP: other specific functions

X<Label se:gp_program>
In addition to the general PARI functions, it is necessary to have some
functions which will be of use specifically for C<gp>, though a few of these can
be accessed under library mode. Before we start describing these, we recall
the difference between \emph{strings} and \emph{keywords} (see
L<Label se:strings>): the latter don't get expanded at all, and you can type
them without any enclosing quotes. The former are dynamic objects, where
everything outside quotes gets immediately expanded.

=head2 X<Strprintf>StrprintfC<(>I<fmt>C<,{x}*)>

X<Label se:Strprintf>returns a string built from the remaining arguments according to the
format fmt. The format consists of ordinary characters (not %), printed
unchanged, and conversions specifications. See C<printf>.

=head2 X<addhelp>addhelpC<(>I<sym>C<,>I<str>C<)>

X<Label se:addhelp>changes the help message for the symbol C<sym>. The string I<str> is
expanded on the spot and stored as the online help for C<sym>. If C<sym>
is a function \emph{you} have defined, its definition will still be printed
before the message I<str>.  It is recommended that you document global
variables and user functions in this way. Of course C<gp> will not protest
if you skip this.  It is possible to attach a help text to an alias, but it
will never be shown: aliases are expanded by the C<?> help operator and we
get the help of the functions the alias points to.

Nothing prevents you from modifying the help of built-in PARI
functions. But if you do, we would like to hear why you needed to do it!

The library syntax is C<void >B<addhelp>C<(const char *sym, const char *str)>X<addhelp>.

=head2 X<alarm>alarmC<({s = 0})>

X<Label se:alarm>trigger an I<alarmer> exception after C<s> seconds, cancelling any
previously set alarm. Stop a pending alarm if s = 0 or is omitted.

For example, the function C<timefact(N,sec)> below
will try to factor C<N> and give up after I<sec> seconds,
returning a partial factorisation.

  default(factor_add_primes,1);
  default(primelimit,16777216);
  timefact(N,sec)=
  {
    trap(alarmer,factor(N,0),alarm(sec);my(F=factor(N));alarm(0);F);
  }

=head2 X<alias>aliasC<(>I<newsym>C<,>I<sym>C<)>

X<Label se:alias>defines the symbol I<newsym> as an alias for the the symbol I<sym>:

  ? alias("det", "matdet");
  ? det([1,2;3,4])
  %1 = -2

You are not restricted to ordinary functions, as in the above example:
to alias (from/to) member functions, prefix them with `C<_.>';
to alias operators, use their internal name, obtained by writing
C<_> in lieu of the operators argument: for instance, C<_!> and
C<!_> are the internal names of the factorial and the
logical negation, respectively.

  ? alias("mod", "_.mod");
  ? alias("add", "_+_");
  ? alias("_.sin", "sin");
  ? mod(Mod(x,x^4+1))
  %2 = x^4 + 1
  ? add(4,6)
  %3 = 10
  ? Pi.sin
  %4 = 0.E-37

Alias expansion is performed directly by the internal GP compiler.
Note that since alias is performed at compilation-time, it does not
require any run-time processing, however it only affects GP code
compiled \emph{after} the alias command is evaluated. A slower but more
flexible alternative is to use variables. Compare

  ? fun = sin;
  ? g(a,b) = intnum(t=a,b,fun(t));
  ? g(0, Pi)
  %3 = 2.0000000000000000000000000000000000000
  ? fun = cos;
  ? g(0, Pi)
  %5 = 1.8830410776607851098 E-39

with

  ? alias(fun, sin);
  ? g(a,b) = intnum(t=a,b,fun(t));
  ? g(0,Pi)
  %2 = 2.0000000000000000000000000000000000000
  ? alias(fun, cos);  \\ Oops. Does not affect *previous* definition!
  ? g(0,Pi)
  %3 = 2.0000000000000000000000000000000000000
  ? g(a,b) = intnum(t=a,b,fun(t)); \\ Redefine, taking new alias into account
  ? g(0,Pi)
  %5 = 1.8830410776607851098 E-39

A sample alias file C<misc/gpalias> is provided with
the standard distribution.

The library syntax is C<void >B<alias0>C<(const char *newsym, const char *sym)>X<alias0>.

=head2 X<allocatemem>allocatememC<({s = 0})>

X<Label se:allocatemem>this very special operation
allows the user to change the stack size \emph{after} initialization. C<x>
must be a non-negative integer. If C<x  ! = 0>, a new stack of size
C<16*\ceil{x/16}> bytes is allocated. If C<x = 0>, the size of
the new stack is twice the size of the old one. The old stack is discarded.

B<Warning> This function should be typed at the C<gp> prompt in
interactive usage, or left by itself at the start of batch files.
It cannot be used meaningfully in loop-like constructs, or as part of a
larger expression sequence, e.g

     allocatemem(); x = 1;   \\ This will not set x!

In fact, all loops are immediately exited, user functions terminated, and
the rest of the sequence following C<allocatemem()> is silently
discarded, as well as all pending sequences of instructions. We just go on
reading the next instruction sequence from the file we're in (or from the
user). In particular, we have the following possibly unexpected behavior: in

     read("file.gp"); x = 1

were C<file.gp> contains an C<allocatemem> statement,
the C<x = 1> is never executed, since all pending instructions in the
current sequence are discarded.

The technical reason is that this routine moves the stack, so temporary
objects created during the current expression evaluation are not correct
anymore. (In particular byte-compiled expressions, which are allocated on
the stack.) To avoid accessing obsolete pointers to the old stack, this
routine ends by a C<longjmp>.

=head2 X<apply>applyC<(f, A)>

X<Label se:apply>Apply the C<t_CLOSURE> C<f> to the entries of C<A>. If C<A>
is a scalar, return C<f(A)>. If C<A> is a polynomial or power series,
apply C<f> on all coefficients. If C<A> is a vector or list, return
the elements C<f(x)> where C<x> runs through C<A>. If C<A> is a matrix,
return the matrix whose entries are the C<f(A[i,j])>.

  ? apply(x->x^2, [1,2,3,4])
  %1 = [1, 4, 9, 16]
  ? apply(x->x^2, [1,2;3,4])
  %2 =
  [1 4]

  [9 16]
  ? apply(x->x^2, 4*x^2 + 3*x+ 2)
  %3 = 16*x^2 + 9*x + 4

Note that many functions already act componentwise on
vectors or matrices, but they almost never act on lists; in this
case, C<apply> is a good solution:

  ? L = List([Mod(1,3), Mod(2,4)]);
  ? lift(L)
    ***   at top-level: lift(L)
    ***                 ^-------
    *** lift: incorrect type in lift.
  ? apply(lift, L);
  %2 = List([1, 2])

X<genapply>The library syntax is B<genapply>C<(void *E, GEN (*fun)(void*,GEN), GEN a)>.

=head2 X<default>defaultC<({>I<key>C<},{>I<val>C<})>

X<Label se:default>returns the default corresponding to keyword I<key>. If I<val> is
present, sets the default to I<val> first (which is subject to string
expansion first). Typing C<default()> (or C<\d>) yields the complete
default list as well as their current values. See L<Label se:defaults> for an
introduction to GP defaults, L<Label se:gp_defaults> for a
list of available defaults, and L<Label se:meta> for some shortcut
alternatives. Note that the shortcuts are meant for interactive use and
usually display more information than C<default>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<default0>C<(const char *key = NULL, const char *val = NULL)>X<default0>.

=head2 X<error>errorC<({>I<str>C<}*)>

X<Label se:error>outputs its argument list (each of
them interpreted as a string), then interrupts the running C<gp> program,
returning to the input prompt. For instance

  error("n = ", n, " is not squarefree !")

=head2 X<extern>externC<(>I<str>C<)>

X<Label se:extern>the string I<str> is the name of an external command (i.e.S< >one you
would type from your UNIX shell prompt). This command is immediately run and
its output fed into C<gp>, just as if read from a file.

=head2 X<externstr>externstrC<(>I<str>C<)>

X<Label se:externstr>the string I<str> is the name of an external command (i.e.S< >one you
would type from your UNIX shell prompt). This command is immediately run and
its output is returned as a vector of GP strings, one component per output
line.

=head2 X<getheap>getheapC<()>

X<Label se:getheap>returns a two-component row vector giving the
number of objects on the heap and the amount of memory they occupy in long
words. Useful mainly for debugging purposes.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<getheap>C<()>X<getheap>.

=head2 X<getrand>getrandC<()>

X<Label se:getrand>returns the current value of the seed used by the
pseudo-random number generator X<random>C<random>. Useful mainly for debugging
purposes, to reproduce a specific chain of computations. The returned value
is technical (reproduces an internal state array), and can only be used as an
argument to X<setrand>C<setrand>.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<getrand>C<()>X<getrand>.

=head2 X<getstack>getstackC<()>

X<Label se:getstack>returns the current value of C<top-avma>, i.e.S< >the number of
bytes used up to now on the stack. Useful mainly for debugging purposes.

The library syntax is C<long >B<getstack>C<()>X<getstack>.

=head2 X<gettime>gettimeC<()>

X<Label se:gettime>returns the time (in milliseconds) elapsed since either the last call to
C<gettime>, or to the beginning of the containing GP instruction (if
inside C<gp>), whichever came last.

The library syntax is C<long >B<gettime>C<()>X<gettime>.

=head2 X<global>globalC<(>I<list> I<of> I<variables>C<)>

X<Label se:global>obsolete. Scheduled for deletion.

=head2 X<input>inputC<()>

X<Label se:input>reads a string, interpreted as a GP expression,
from the input file, usually standard input (i.e.S< >the keyboard). If a
sequence of expressions is given, the result is the result of the last
expression of the sequence. When using this instruction, it is useful to
prompt for the string by using the C<print1> function. Note that in the
present version 2.19 of C<pari.el>, when using C<gp> under GNU Emacs (see
L<Label se:emacs>) one \emph{must} prompt for the string, with a string
which ends with the same prompt as any of the previous ones (a C<"? ">
will do for instance).

=head2 X<install>installC<(>I<name>C<,>I<code>C<,{>I<gpname>C<},{>I<lib>C<})>

X<Label se:install>loads from dynamic library I<lib> the function I<name>. Assigns to it
the name I<gpname> in this C<gp> session, with argument code I<code>
(see the Libpari Manual for an explanation of those). If I<lib> is
omitted, uses C<libpari.so>. If I<gpname> is omitted, uses
I<name>.

This function is useful for adding custom functions to the C<gp> interpreter,
or picking useful functions from unrelated libraries. For instance, it
makes the function X<system>C<system> obsolete:

  ? install(system, vs, sys, "libc.so")
  ? sys("ls gp*")
  gp.c            gp.h            gp_rl.c

But it also gives you access to all (non static) functions defined in the
PARI library. For instance, the function C<GEN addii(GEN x, GEN y)> adds
two PARI integers, and is not directly accessible under C<gp> (it is
eventually called by the C<+> operator of course):

  ? install("addii", "GG")
  ? addii(1, 2)
  %1 = 3

Re-installing a function will print a warning and update the prototype code
if needed. However, it will not reload a symbol from the library, even it the
latter has been recompiled.

B<Caution> This function may not work on all systems, especially
when C<gp> has been compiled statically. In that case, the first use of an
installed function will provoke a Segmentation Fault, i.e.S< >a major internal
blunder (this should never happen with a dynamically linked executable).
Hence, if you intend to use this function, please check first on some
harmless example such as the ones above that it works properly on your
machine.

=head2 X<kill>killC<(>I<sym>C<)>

X<Label se:kill>restores the symbol C<sym> to its ``undefined'' status, and deletes any
help messages associated to C<sym> using C<addhelp>. Variable names
remain known to the interpreter and keep their former priority: you cannot
make a variable ``less important" by killing it!

  ? z = y = 1; y
  %1 = 1
  ? kill(y)
  ? y            \\ restored to ``undefined'' status
  %2 = y
  ? variable()
  %3 = [x, y, z] \\ but the variable name y is still known, with y > z !

For the same reason, killing a user function (which is an ordinary
variable holding a C<t_CLOSURE>) does not remove its name from the list of
variable names.

If the symbol is associated to a variable --- user functions being an
important special case ---, one may use the X<quote>quote operator
C<a = 'a> to reset variables to their starting values. However, this
will not delete a help message associated to C<a>, and is also slightly
slower than C<kill(a)>.

  ? x = 1; addhelp(x, "foo"); x
  %1 = 1
  ? x = 'x; x   \\ same as 'kill', except we don't delete help.
  %2 = x
  ? ?x
  foo

On the other hand, C<kill> is the only way to remove aliases and installed
functions.

  ? alias(fun, sin);
  ? kill(fun);

  ? install(addii, GG);
  ? kill(addii);

The library syntax is C<void >B<kill0>C<(const char *sym)>X<kill0>.

=head2 X<print>printC<({>I<str>C<}*)>

X<Label se:print>outputs its (string) arguments in raw format, ending with a newline.

=head2 X<print1>print1C<({>I<str>C<}*)>

X<Label se:print1>outputs its (string) arguments in raw
format, without ending with a newline. Note that you can still embed newlines
within your strings, using the C<\n> notationS< >!

=head2 X<printf>printfC<(>I<fmt>C<,{x}*)>

X<Label se:printf>This function is based on the C library command of the same name.
It prints its arguments according to the format I<fmt>, which specifies how
subsequent arguments are converted for output. The format is a
character string composed of zero or more directives:

\item ordinary characters (not C<%>), printed unchanged,

\item conversions specifications (C<%> followed by some characters)
which fetch one argument from the list and prints it according to the
specification.

More precisely, a conversion specification consists in a C<%>, one or more
optional flags (among C<#>, C<0>, C<->, C<+>, ` '), an optional
decimal digit string specifying a minimal field width, an optional precision
in the form of a period (`C<.>') followed by a decimal digit string, and
the conversion specifier (among C<d>,C<i>, C<o>, C<u>,
C<x>,C<X>, C<p>, C<e>,C<E>, C<f>, C<g>,C<G>, C<s>).

B<The flag characters> The character C<%> is followed by zero or
more of the following flags:

\item C<#>: The value is converted to an ``alternate form''. For
C<o> conversion (octal), a C<0> is prefixed to the string. For C<x>
and C<X> conversions (hexa), respectively C<0x> and C<0X> are
prepended. For other conversions, the flag is ignored.

\item C<0>: The value should be zero padded. For
C<d>,
C<i>,
C<o>,
C<u>,
C<x>,
C<X>
C<e>,
C<E>,
C<f>,
C<F>,
C<g>, and
C<G> conversions, the value is padded on the left with zeros rather than
blanks. (If the C<0> and C<-> flags both appear, the C<0> flag is
ignored.)

\item C<->: The value is left adjusted on the field boundary. (The
default is right justification.) The value is padded on the right with
blanks, rather than on the left with blanks or zeros. A C<-> overrides a
C<0> if both are given.

\item C<` '> (a space): A blank is left before a positive number
produced by a signed conversion.

\item C<+>: A sign (+ or -) is placed before a number produced by a
signed conversion. A C<+> overrides a space if both are used.

B<The field width> An optional decimal digit string (whose first
digit is non-zero) specifying a \emph{minimum} field width. If the value has
fewer characters than the field width, it is padded with spaces on the left
(or right, if the left-adjustment flag has been given). In no case does a
small field width cause truncation of a field; if the value is wider than
the field width, the field is expanded to contain the conversion result.
Instead of a decimal digit string, one may write C<*> to specify that the
field width is given in the next argument.

B<The precision> An optional precision in the form of a period
(`C<.>') followed by a decimal digit string. This gives
the number of digits to appear after the radix character for C<e>,
C<E>, C<f>, and C<F> conversions, the maximum number of significant
digits for C<g> and C<G> conversions, and the maximum number of
characters to be printed from an C<s> conversion.
Instead of a decimal digit string, one may write C<*> to specify that the
field width is given in the next argument.

B<The length modifier> This is ignored under C<gp>, but
necessary for C<libpari> programming. Description given here for
completeness:

\item C<l>: argument is a C<long> integer.

\item C<P>: argument is a C<GEN>.

B<The conversion specifier> A character that specifies the type of
conversion to be applied.

\item C<d>, C<i>: A signed integer.

\item C<o>, C<u>, C<x>, C<X>: An unsigned integer, converted
to unsigned octal (C<o>), decimal (C<u>) or hexadecimal (C<x> or
C<X>) notation. The letters C<abcdef> are used for C<x>
conversions;  the letters C<ABCDEF> are used for C<X> conversions.

\item C<e>, C<E>: The (real) argument is converted in the style
C<[ -]d.ddd e[ -]dd>, where there is one digit before the decimal point,
and the number of digits after it is equal to the precision; if the
precision is missing, use the current C<realprecision> for the total
number of printed digits. If the precision is explicitly 0, no decimal-point
character appears. An C<E> conversion uses the letter C<E> rather
than C<e> to introduce the exponent.

\item C<f>, C<F>: The (real) argument is converted in the style
C<[ -]ddd.ddd>, where the number of digits after the decimal point
is equal to the precision; if the precision is missing, use the current
C<realprecision> for the total number of printed digits. If the precision
is explicitly 0, no decimal-point character appears. If a decimal point
appears, at least one digit appears before it.

\item C<g>, C<G>: The (real) argument is converted in style
C<e> or C<f> (or C<E> or C<F> for C<G> conversions)
C<[ -]ddd.ddd>, where the total number of digits printed
is equal to the precision; if the precision is missing, use the current
C<realprecision>. If the precision is explicitly 0, it is treated as 1.
Style C<e> is used when
the decimal exponent is C< E<lt> -4>, to print C<0.>, or when the integer
part cannot be decided given the known significant digits, and the C<f>
format otherwise.

\item C<c>: The integer argument is converted to an unsigned char, and the
resulting character is written.

\item C<s>: Convert to a character string. If a precision is given, no
more than the specified number of characters are written.

\item C<p>: Print the address of the argument in hexadecimal (as if by
C<%#x>).

\item C<%>: A C<%> is written. No argument is converted. The complete
conversion specification is C<%%>.

Examples:

  ? printf("floor: %d, field width 3: %3d, with sign: %+3d\n", Pi, 1, 2);
  floor: 3, field width 3:   1, with sign:  +2

  ? printf("%.5g %.5g %.5g\n",123,123/456,123456789);
  123.00 0.26974 1.2346 e8

  ? printf("%-2.5s:%2.5s:%2.5s\n", "P", "PARI", "PARIGP");
  P :PARI:PARIG

  \\ min field width and precision given by arguments
  ? x = 23; y=-1/x; printf("x=%+06.2f y=%+0*.*f\n", x, 6, 2, y);
  x=+23.00 y=-00.04

  \\ minimum fields width 5, pad left with zeroes
  ? for (i = 2, 5, printf("%05d\n", 10^i))
  00100
  01000
  10000
  100000  \\ don't truncate fields whose length is larger than the minimum width
  ? printf("%.2f  |%06.2f|", Pi,Pi)
  3.14  |  3.14|

All numerical conversions apply recursively to the entries
of vectors and matrices:

  ? printf("%4d", [1,2,3]);
  [   1,   2,   3]
  ? printf("%5.2f", mathilbert(3));
  [ 1.00  0.50  0.33]

  [ 0.50  0.33  0.25]

  [ 0.33  0.25  0.20]

B<Technical note> Our implementation of X<printf>C<printf>
deviates from the C89 and C99 standards in a few places:

\item whenever a precision is missing, the current C<realprecision> is
used to determine the number of printed digits (C89: use 6 decimals after
the radix character).

\item in conversion style C<e>, we do not impose that the
exponent has at least two digits; we never write a C<+> sign in the
exponent; 0 is printed in a special way, always as C<0.E>I<exp>.

\item in conversion style C<f>, we switch to style C<e> if the
exponent is greater or equal to the precision.

\item in conversion C<g> and C<G>, we do not remove trailing zeros
from the fractional part of the result; nor a trailing decimal point;
0 is printed in a special way, always as C<0.E>I<exp>.

=head2 X<printtex>printtexC<({>I<str>C<}*)>

X<Label se:printtex>outputs its (string) arguments in TeX format. This output can then be
used in a TeX manuscript.
The printing is done on the standard output. If you want to print it to a
file you should use C<writetex> (see there).

Another possibility is to enable the X<log>C<log> default
(seeS< >L<Label se:defaults>).
You could for instance do:X<logfile>

  default(logfile, "new.tex");
  default(log, 1);
  printtex(result);

=head2 X<quit>quitC<({>I<status>C< = 0})>

X<Label se:quit>exits C<gp> and return to the system with exit status
C<status>, a small integer. A non-zero exit status normally indicates
abnormal termination. (Note: the system actually sees only
C<status> mod C<256>, see your man pages for C<exit(3)> or C<wait(2)>).

=head2 X<read>readC<({>I<filename>C<})>

X<Label se:read>reads in the file
I<filename> (subject to string expansion). If I<filename> is
omitted, re-reads the last file that was fed into C<gp>. The return
value is the result of the last expression evaluated.

If a GP X<binary file>C<binary file> is read using this command (see
L<Label se:writebin>), the file is loaded and the last object in the file
is returned.

In case the file you read in contains an X<allocatemem>C<allocatemem> statement (to be
generally avoided), you should leave C<read> instructions by themselves,
and not part of larger instruction sequences.

=head2 X<readvec>readvecC<({>I<filename>C<})>

X<Label se:readvec>reads in the file
I<filename> (subject to string expansion). If I<filename> is
omitted, re-reads the last file that was fed into C<gp>. The return
value is a vector whose components are the evaluation of all sequences
of instructions contained in the file. For instance, if I<file> contains

  1
  2
  3

then we will get:

  ? \r a
  %1 = 1
  %2 = 2
  %3 = 3
  ? read(a)
  %4 = 3
  ? readvec(a)
  %5 = [1, 2, 3]

In general a sequence is just a single line, but as usual braces and
C<\> may be used to enter multiline sequences.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<gp_readvec_file>C<(const char *filename)>X<gp_readvec_file>.
The underlying library function
C<GEN >B<gp_readvec_stream>C<(FILE *f)>X<gp_readvec_stream> is usually more flexible.

=head2 X<select>selectC<(f, A)>

X<Label se:select>Given a vector, list or matrix C<A> and a C<t_CLOSURE> C<f>,
returns the elements C<x> of C<A> such that C<f(x)> is non-zero. In other
words, C<f> is seen as a selection function returning a boolean value.

  ? select(x->isprime(x), vector(50,i,i^2+1))
  %1 = [2, 5, 17, 37, 101, 197, 257, 401, 577, 677, 1297, 1601]
  ? select(x->(x<100), %)
  %2 = [2, 5, 17, 37]

returns the primes of the form C<i^2+1> for some C<i E<lt>= 50>,
then the elements less than 100 in the preceding result. The following
function lists the elements in C<(>B<Z>C</N>B<Z>C<)^*>:

  ? invertibles(N) = select(x->gcd(x,N) == 1, vector(N,i,i))

Finally

  ? select(x->x, M)

selects the non-0 entries in C<M>. If the latter is a
C<t_MAT>, we extract the matrix of non-0 columns. Note that \emph{removing}
entries instead of selecting them just involves replacing the selection
function C<f> with its negation:

  ? select(x->!isprime(x), vector(50,i,i^2+1))

X<genselect>The library syntax is B<genselect>C<(void *E, long (*fun)(void*,GEN), GEN a)>.

=head2 X<setrand>setrandC<(n)>

X<Label se:setrand>reseeds the random number generator using the seed C<n>. No value is
returned. The seed is either a technical array output by C<getrand>, or a
small positive integer, used to generate deterministically a suitable state
array. For instance, running a randomized computation starting by
C<setrand(1)> twice will generate the exact same output.

The library syntax is C<void >B<setrand>C<(GEN n)>X<setrand>.

=head2 X<system>systemC<(>I<str>C<)>

X<Label se:system>I<str> is a string representing a system command. This command is
executed, its output written to the standard output (this won't get into your
logfile), and control returns to the PARI system. This simply calls the C
C<system> command.

=head2 X<trap>trapC<({e}, {>I<rec>C<}, >I<seq>C<)>

X<Label se:trap>tries to
evaluate I<seq>, trapping runtime error C<e>, that is effectively preventing
it from aborting computations in the usual way; the recovery sequence
I<rec> is executed if the error occurs and the evaluation of I<rec>
becomes the result of the command. If C<e> is omitted, all exceptions are
trapped. See L<Label se:errorrec> for an introduction to error recovery
under C<gp>.

  ? \\ trap division by 0
  ? inv(x) = trap (gdiver, INFINITY, 1/x)
  ? inv(2)
  %1 = 1/2
  ? inv(0)
  %2 = INFINITY

Note that I<seq> is effectively evaluated up to the point that produced
the error, and the recovery sequence is evaluated starting from that same
context, it does not "undo" whatever happened in the other branch (restore
the evaluation context):

  ? x = 1; trap (, /* recover: */ x, /* try: */ x = 0; 1/x)
  %1 = 0

B<Note> The interface is currently not adequate for trapping
individual exceptions. In the current version B<2.2.0>, the following keywords
are recognized, but the name list will be expanded and changed in the
future (all library mode errors can be trapped: it's a matter of defining
the keywords to C<gp>):

C<alarmer>: alarm time-out

C<archer>: not available on this architecture or operating system

C<errpile>: the PARI stack overflows

C<gdiver>: division by 0

C<impl>: not yet implemented

C<invmoder>: impossible inverse modulo

C<overflower>: all forms of arithmetic overflow, including length
or exponent overflow (when a larger value is supplied than the
implementation can handle).

C<syntaxer>: syntax error

C<talker>: miscellaneous error

C<typeer>: wrong type

C<user>: user error (from the C<error> function)

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<trap0>C<(const char *e = NULL, GEN rec = NULL, GEN seq = NULL)>X<trap0>.

=head2 X<type>typeC<(x)>

X<Label se:type>this is useful only under C<gp>. Returns the internal type name of
the PARI object C<x> as a  string. Check out existing type names with the
metacommand C<\t>. For example C<type(1)> will return "C<t_INT>".

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<type0>C<(GEN x)>X<type0>.
The macro C<typ> is usually simpler to use since it returns a
C<long> that can easily be matched with the symbols C<t_*>. The name
C<type> was avoided since it is a reserved identifier for some compilers.

=head2 X<version>versionC<()>

X<Label se:version>returns the current version number as a C<t_VEC> with three integer
components (major version number, minor version number and patchlevel);
if your sources were obtained through our version control system, this will
be followed by a more precise version string, e.g.S< >C<git->\emph{commit
hash}.

This function is present in all versions of PARI following releases 2.3.4
(stable) and 2.4.3 (testing).

Unless you are working with multiple development versions, you probably only
care about the 3 first numeric components. In any case, the C<lex> function
offers a clever way to check against a particular version number, since it will
compare each successive vector entry, numerically or as strings, and will not
mind if the vectors it compares have different lengths :

     if (lex(version(), [2,3,5]) >= 0,
       \\ code to be executed if we are running 2.3.5 or more recent.
     ,
       \\ compatibility code
     );

On a number of different machines, C<version()> could return either of

   %1 = [2, 3, 4]    \\ released version, stable branch
   %1 = [2, 4, 3]    \\ released version, testing branch
   %1 = [2, 6, 0, "git-2cce227"] \\ development

In particular the first line of the gp introductory message can be essentially
emulated by

     v = version();
     n = Str(v[1], ".", v[2], ".", v[3]);
     s = if (#v > 3, v[4], "");
     print("GP/PARI CALCULATOR Version ", n, " (", s, ")");

If you \emph{are} working with many development versions of
PARI/GP, the last component can be profitably included in the name of
your logfile, for instance.

The library syntax is C<GEN >B<pari_version>C<()>X<pari_version>.

=head2 X<warning>warningC<({>I<str>C<}*)>

X<Label se:warning>outputs the message ``user warning''
and the argument list (each of them interpreted as a string).
If colors are enabled, this warning will be in a different color,
making it easy to distinguish.

  warning(n, " is very large, this might take a while.")

=head2 X<whatnow>whatnowC<(>I<key>C<)>

X<Label se:whatnow>if keyword I<key> is the name of a function that was present in GP
version 1.39.15 or lower, outputs the new function name and syntax, if it
changed at all (C<387> out of C<560> did).

=head2 X<write>writeC<(>I<filename>C<,{>I<str>C<}*)>

X<Label se:write>writes (appends) to I<filename> the remaining arguments, and appends a
newline (same output as C<print>).

=head2 X<write1>write1C<(>I<filename>C<,{>I<str>C<}*)>

X<Label se:write1>writes (appends) to I<filename> the remaining arguments without a
trailing newline (same output as C<print1>).

=head2 X<writebin>writebinC<(>I<filename>C<,{x})>

X<Label se:writebin>writes (appends) to
I<filename> the object C<x> in binary format. This format is not human
readable, but contains the exact internal structure of C<x>, and is much
faster to save/load than a string expression, as would be produced by
X<write>C<write>. The binary file format includes a magic number, so that such a
file can be recognized and correctly input by the regular X<read>C<read> or C<\r>
function. If saved objects refer to (polynomial) variables that are not
defined in the new session, they will be displayed in a funny way (see
L<Label se:kill>).

If C<x> is omitted, saves all user variables from the session, together with
their names. Reading such a ``named object'' back in a C<gp> session will set
the corresponding user variable to the saved value. E.g after

  x = 1; writebin("log")

reading C<log> into a clean session will set C<x> to C<1>.
The relative variables priorities (see L<Label se:priority>) of new variables
set in this way remain the same (preset variables retain their former
priority, but are set to the new value). In particular, reading such a
session log into a clean session will restore all variables exactly as they
were in the original one.

User functions, installed functions and history objects can not be saved via
this function. Just as a regular input file, a binary file can be compressed
using X<gzip>C<gzip>, provided the file name has the standard C<.gz>
extension.X<binary file>

In the present implementation, the binary files are architecture dependent
and compatibility with future versions of C<gp> is not guaranteed. Hence
binary files should not be used for long term storage (also, they are
larger and harder to compress than text files).

The library syntax is C<void >B<gpwritebin>C<(const char *filename, GEN x = NULL)>X<gpwritebin>.

=head2 X<writetex>writetexC<(>I<filename>C<,{>I<str>C<}*)>

X<Label se:writetex>as C<write>, in TeX format.

=head1 GP defaults

X<Label se:gp_defaults> This section documents the GP defaults

=head2 TeXstyle

\label{se:def,TeXstyle}the bits of this default allow
C<gp> to use less rigid TeX formatting commands in the logfile. This
default is only taken into account when C<log = 3>. The bits of
C<TeXstyle> have the following meaning

2: insert C<\right> / C<\left> pairs where appropriate.

4: insert discretionary breaks in polynomials, to enhance the probability of
a good line break.

The default value is C<0>.

=head2 breakloop

\label{se:def,breakloop}if true, enables the ``break loop'' debugging mode, see
L<Label se:break_loop>.

The default value is C<1> if we are running an interactive C<gp>
session, and C<0> otherwise.

=head2 colors

\label{se:def,colors}this default is only usable if C<gp>
is running within certain color-capable terminals. For instance C<rxvt>,
C<color_xterm> and modern versions of C<xterm> under X Windows, or
standard Linux/DOS text consoles. It causes C<gp> to use a small palette of
colors for its output. With xterms, the colormap used corresponds to the
resources C<Xterm*colorn> where C<n> ranges from C<0> to C<15> (see the
file C<misc/color.dft> for an example). Accepted values for this
default are strings C<"a_1,...,a_k"> where C<k E<lt>= 7> and each
C<a_i> is either

B<*> the keyword C<no> (use the default color, usually
black on transparent background)

B<*> an integer between 0 and 15 corresponding to the
aforementioned colormap

B<*> a triple C<[c_0,c_1,c_2]> where C<c_0> stands for foreground
color, C<c_1> for background color, and C<c_2> for attributes (0 is default, 1
is bold, 4 is underline).

The output objects thus affected are respectively error messages,
history numbers, prompt, input line, output, help messages, timer (that's
seven of them). If C<k E<lt> 7>, the remaining C<a_i> are assumed to be C<no>. For
instance

  default(colors, "9, 5, no, no, 4")

typesets error messages in color C<9>, history numbers in color C<5>, output in
color C<4>, and does not affect the rest.

A set of default colors for dark (reverse video or PC console) and light
backgrounds respectively is activated when C<colors> is set to
C<darkbg>, resp.S< >C<lightbg> (or any proper prefix: C<d> is
recognized as an abbreviation for C<darkbg>). A bold variant of
C<darkbg>, called C<boldfg>, is provided if you find the former too
pale.

X<EMACS>In the present version, this default is incompatible with PariEmacs.
Changing it will just fail silently (the alternative would be to display
escape sequences as is, since Emacs will refuse to interpret them).
You must customize color highlighting from the PariEmacs side, see its
documentation.

The default value is C<""> (no colors).

=head2 compatible

\label{se:def,compatible}The GP function names and syntax
have changed tremendously between versions 1.xx and 2.00. To help you cope
with this we provide some kind of backward compatibility, depending on the
value of this default:

S< >S< >C<compatible> = 0: no backward compatibility. In this mode, a very
handy function, to be described in L<Label se:whatnow>, is C<whatnow>,
which tells you what has become of your favourite functions, which C<gp>
suddenly can't seem to remember.

S< >S< >C<compatible> = 1: warn when using obsolete functions, but
otherwise accept them. The output uses the new conventions though, and
there may be subtle incompatibilities between the behavior of former and
current functions, even when they share the same name (the current function
is used in such cases, of course!). We thought of this one as a transitory
help for C<gp> old-timers. Thus, to encourage switching to C<compatible> = 0,
it is not possible to disable the warning.

S< >S< >C<compatible> = 2: use only the old function naming scheme (as
used up to version 1.39.15), but \emph{taking case into account}. Thus
C<I> (C< = > F<sqrt> C<{-1}>) is not the same as C<i> (user variable, unbound
by default), and you won't get an error message using C<i> as a loop
index as used to be the case.

S< >S< >C<compatible> = 3: try to mimic exactly the former behavior. This
is not always possible when functions have changed in a fundamental way.
But these differences are usually for the better (they were meant to,
anyway), and will probably not be discovered by the casual user.

One adverse side effect is that any user functions and aliases that have
been defined \emph{before} changing C<compatible> will get erased if this
change modifies the function list, i.e.S< >if you move between groups
C<{0,1}> and C<{2,3}> (variables are unaffected). We of course strongly
encourage you to try and get used to the setting C<compatible> = 0.

Note that the default X<new_galois_format>C<new_galois_format> is another compatibility setting,
which is completely independent of C<compatible>.

The default value is C<0>.

=head2 datadir

\label{se:def,datadir}the name of directory containing the optional data files. For now,
this includes the C<elldata>, C<galdata>, C<galpol>, C<seadata>
packages.

The default value is \datadir (the location of installed precomputed data,
can be specified via C<Configure --datadir = >).

=head2 debug

\label{se:def,debug}debugging level. If it is non-zero, some extra messages may be printed,
according to what is going on (seeS< >C<\g>).

The default value is C<0> (no debugging messages).

=head2 debugfiles

\label{se:def,debugfiles}file usage debugging level. If it is non-zero, C<gp> will print
information on file descriptors in use, from PARI's point of view
(seeS< >C<\gf>).

The default value is C<0> (no debugging messages).

=head2 debugmem

\label{se:def,debugmem}memory debugging level. If it is non-zero, C<gp> will regularly print
information on memory usage. If it's greater than 2, it will indicate any
important garbage collecting and the function it is taking place in
(seeS< >C<\gm>).

B<Important Note:> As it noticeably slows down the performance,
the first functionality (memory usage) is disabled if you're not running a
version compiled for debugging (see AppendixS< >A).

The default value is C<0> (no debugging messages).

=head2 echo

\label{se:def,echo}this toggle is either 1 (on) or 0 (off). When C<echo>
mode is on, each command is reprinted before being executed. This can be
useful when reading a file with the C<\r> or C<read> commands. For
example, it is turned on at the beginning of the test files used to check
whether C<gp> has been built correctly (see C<\e>).

The default value is C<0> (no echo).

=head2 factor_add_primes

X<def,factor_add_primes>\label{se:def,factor_add_primes}this toggle is either 1 (on) or 0 (off). If on,
the integer factorization machinery calls X<addprimes>C<addprimes> on primes
factor that were difficult to find (larger than C<2^24>), so they are
automatically tried first in other factorizations. If a routine is performing
(or has performed) a factorization and is interrupted by an error or via
Control-C, this lets you recover the prime factors already found. The
downside is that a huge C<addprimes> table unrelated to the current
computations will slow down arithmetic functions relying on integer
factorization; one should then empty the table using X<removeprimes>C<removeprimes>.

The defaut value is C<0>.

=head2 factor_proven

X<def,factor_proven>\label{se:def,factor_proven}this toggle is either 1 (on) or 0 (off). By
default, the factors output by the integer factorization machinery are
only pseudo-primes, not proven primes. If this toggle is
set, a primality proof is done for each factor and all results depending on
integer factorization are fully proven. This flag does not affect partial
factorization when it is explicitly requested. It also does not affect the
private table managed by X<addprimes>C<addprimes>: its entries are included as is in
factorizations, without being tested for primality.

The defaut value is C<0>.

=head2 format

\label{se:def,format}of the form xC<.n>, where x (conversion style)
is a letter in C<{e,f,g}>, and C<n> (precision) is an
integer; this affects the way real numbers are printed:

\item If the conversion style is C<e>, real numbers are printed in
X<scientific format>scientific format, always with an explicit exponent,
e.g.S< >C<3.3 E-5>.

\item In style C<f>, real numbers are generally printed in 
X<fixed floating point format>fixed
floating point format without exponent, e.g.S< >C<0.000033>. A large
real number, whose integer part is not well defined (not enough significant
digits), is printed in styleS< >C<e>. For instance C<10.^100> known to
ten significant digits is always printed in style C<e>.

\item In style C<g>, non-zero real numbers are printed in C<f> format,
except when their decimal exponent is C< E<lt> -4>, in which case they are printed in
C<e> format. Real zeroes (of arbitrary exponent) are printed in C<e>
format.

The precision C<n> is the number of significant digits printed for real
numbers, except if C<n E<lt> 0> where all the significant digits will be printed
(initial default 28, or 38 for 64-bit machines). For more powerful formatting
possibilities, see X<printf>C<printf> and X<Strprintf>C<Strprintf>.

The default value is C<"g.28"> and C<"g.38"> on 32-bit and
64-bit machines, respectively.

=head2 graphcolormap

\label{se:def,graphcolormap}a vector of colors, to be
used by hi-res graphing routines. Its length is arbitrary, but it must
contain at least 3 entries: the first 3 colors are used for background,
frame/ticks and axes respectively. All colors in the colormap may be freely
used in X<plotcolor>C<plotcolor> calls.

A color is either given as in the default by character strings or by an RGB
code. For valid character strings, see the standard C<rgb.txt> file in X11
distributions, where we restrict to lowercase letters and remove all
whitespace from color names. An RGB code is a vector with 3 integer entries
between 0 and 255. For instance C<[250, 235, 215]> and C<"antique
white"> represent the same color. RGB codes are a little cryptic but often
easier to generate.

The default value is [C<"white">, C<"black">, C<"blue">,
C<"violetred">, C<"red">, C<"green">, C<"grey">,
C<"gainsboro">].

=head2 graphcolors

\label{se:def,graphcolors}entries in the
X<graphcolormap>C<graphcolormap> that will be used to plot multi-curves. The successive
curves are drawn in colors

C<graphcolormap[graphcolors[1]]>, C<graphcolormap[graphcolors[2]]>,
...

cycling when the C<graphcolors> list is exhausted.

The default value is C<[4,5]>.

=head2 help

\label{se:def,help}name of the external help program which will be used from within
C<gp> when extended help is invoked, usually through a C<??> or
C<???> request (see L<Label se:exthelp>), or C<M-H> under readline (see
L<Label se:readline>).

The default value is the local of the C<gphelp> script.

=head2 histfile

\label{se:def,histfile}name of a file where
C<gp> will keep a history of all \emph{input} commands (results are
omitted). If this file exists when the value of C<histfile> changes,
it is read in and becomes part of the session history. Thus, setting this
default in your gprc saves your readline history between sessions. Setting
this default to the empty string C<""> changes it to
C< E<lt> undefined E<gt> >

The default value is C< E<lt> undefined E<gt> > (no history file).

=head2 histsize

\label{se:def,histsize}C<gp> keeps a history of the last
C<histsize> results computed so far, which you can recover using the
C<%> notation (see L<Label se:history>). When this number is exceeded,
the oldest values are erased. Tampering with this default is the only way to
get rid of the ones you do not need anymore.

The default value is C<5000>.

=head2 lines

\label{se:def,lines}if set to a positive value, C<gp> prints at
most that many lines from each result, terminating the last line shown with
C<[+++]> if further material has been suppressed. The various C<print>
commands (see L<Label se:gp_program>) are unaffected, so you can always type
C<print(%)> or C<\a> to view the full result. If the actual screen width
cannot be determined, a ``line'' is assumed to be 80 characters long.

The default value is C<0>.

=head2 log

\label{se:def,log}this can be either 0 (off) or 1, 2, 3
(on, see below for the various modes). When logging mode is turned on, C<gp>
opens a log file, whose exact name is determined by the C<logfile>
default. Subsequently, all the commands and results will be written to that
file (see C<\l>). In case a file with this precise name already existed, it
will not be erased: your data will be \emph{appended} at the end.

The specific positive values of C<log> have the following meaning

1: plain logfile

2: emit color codes to the logfile (if C<colors> is set).

3: write LaTeX output to the logfile (can be further customized using
X<TeXstyle>C<TeXstyle>).

The default value is C<0>.

=head2 logfile

\label{se:def,logfile}name of the log file to be used when the C<log> toggle is on.
Environment and time expansion are performed.

The default value is C<"pari.log">.

=head2 new_galois_format

X<def,new_galois_format>\label{se:def,new_galois_format}this toggle is either 1 (on) or 0 (off). If on,
the X<polgalois>C<polgalois> command will use a different, more
consistent, naming scheme for Galois groups. This default is provided to
ensure that scripts can control this behavior and do not break unexpectedly.

The defaut value is C<0>. This value will change to C<1> (set) in the next
major version.

=head2 output

\label{se:def,output}there are three possible values: 0
( = S< >I<raw>), 1 ( = S< >I<prettymatrix>), or 3
( = S< >I<external> I<prettyprint>). This
means that, independently of the default C<format> for reals which we
explained above, you can print results in three ways:

B<*> X<raw format>I<raw format>, i.e.S< >a format which is equivalent to what you
input, including explicit multiplication signs, and everything typed on a
line instead of two dimensional boxes. This can have several advantages, for
instance it allows you to pick the result with a mouse or an editor, and to
paste it somewhere else.

B<*> X<prettymatrix format>I<prettymatrix format>: this is identical to raw format, except
that matrices are printed as boxes instead of horizontally. This is
prettier, but takes more space and cannot be used for input. Column vectors
are still printed horizontally.

B<*> X<external prettyprint>I<external prettyprint>: pipes all C<gp>
output in TeX format to an external prettyprinter, according to the value of
X<prettyprinter>C<prettyprinter>. The default script (X<tex2mail>C<tex2mail>) converts its input
to readable two-dimensional text.

Independently of the setting of this default, an object can be printed
in any of the three formats at any time using the commands C<\a> and C<\m>
and C<\B> respectively.

The default value is C<1> (I<prettymatrix>).

=head2 parisize

\label{se:def,parisize}C<gp>, and in fact any program using the PARI
library, needs a X<stack>I<stack> in which to do its computations. C<parisize>
is the stack size, in bytes. It is strongly recommended you increase this
default (using the C<-s> command-line switch, or a X<gprc>C<gprc>) if you can
afford it. Don't increase it beyond the actual amount of RAM installed on
your computer or C<gp> will spend most of its time paging.

In case of emergency, you can use the X<allocatemem>C<allocatemem> function to
increase C<parisize>, once the session is started.

The default value is 4M, resp.S< >8M on a 32-bit, resp.S< >64-bit machine.

=head2 path

\label{se:def,path}this is a list of directories, separated by colons C<':'>
(semicolons C<';'> in the DOS world, since colons are preempted for drive names).
When asked to read a file whose name is not given by an absolute path
(does not start with C</>, C<./> or C<../>), C<gp> will look for
it in these directories, in the order they were written in C<path>. Here,
as usual, C<.> means the current directory, and C<..> its immediate
parent. Environment expansion is performed.

The default value is C<".:~:~/gp"> on UNIX systems,
C<".;C:\;C:\GP"> on DOS, OS/2 and Windows, and C<"."> otherwise.

=head2 prettyprinter

\label{se:def,prettyprinter}the name of an external prettyprinter to use when
C<output> isS< >3 (alternate prettyprinter). Note that the default
X<tex2mail>C<tex2mail> looks much nicer than the built-in ``beautified
format'' (C<output = 2>).

The default value is C<"tex2mail -TeX -noindent -ragged -by_par">.

=head2 primelimit

\label{se:def,primelimit}C<gp> precomputes a list of
all primes less than C<primelimit> at initialization time. These are used
by many arithmetic functions, usually for trial division purposes. If you do
not plan to invoke any of them, you can just set this to 1. The maximal value
is a little less than C<2^{32}> (resp C<2^{64}>) on a 32-bit (resp.S< >64-bit)
machine.

Since almost all arithmetic functions eventually require some table of prime
numbers, PARI currently guarantees that the first 6547 primes, up to and
including 65557, are precomputed, even if C<primelimit> is C<1>.

The default value is C<500k>.

=head2 prompt

\label{se:def,prompt}a string that will be printed as
prompt. Note that most usual escape sequences are available there: C<\e> for
Esc, C<\n> for Newline,..., C<\\> for C<\>. Time expansion is
performed.

This string is sent through the library function X<strftime>C<strftime> (on a
Unix system, you can try C<man strftime> at your shell prompt). This means
that C<%> constructs have a special meaning, usually related to the time
and date. For instance, C<%H> = hour (24-hour clock) and C<%M> = 
minute [00,59] (use C<%%> to get a real C<%>).

If you use C<readline>, escape sequences in your prompt will result in
display bugs. If you have a relatively recent C<readline> (see the comment
at the end of \secref{se:def,colors}), you can brace them with special sequences
(C<\[> and C<\]>), and you will be safe. If these just result in
extra spaces in your prompt, then you'll have to get a more recent
C<readline>. See the file C<misc/gprc.dft> for an example.

X<EMACS>B<Caution>: PariEmacs needs to know about the prompt pattern to
separate your input from previous C<gp> results, without ambiguity. It is
not a trivial problem to adapt automatically this regular expression to an
arbitrary prompt (which can be self-modifying!). See PariEmacs's
documentation.

The default value is C<"? ">.

=head2 prompt_cont

X<def,prompt_cont>\label{se:def,prompt_cont}a string that will be printed
to prompt for continuation lines (e.g. in between braces, or after a
line-terminating backslash). Everything that applies to C<prompt>
applies to C<prompt_cont> as well.

The defaut value is C<"">.

=head2 psfile

\label{se:def,psfile}name of the default file where
C<gp> is to dump its PostScript drawings (these are appended, so that no
previous data are lost). Environment and time expansion are performed.

The default value is C<"pari.ps">.

=head2 readline

\label{se:def,readline}switches readline line-editing
facilities on and off. This may be useful if you are running C<gp> in a Sun
X<cmdtool>C<cmdtool>, which interacts badly with readline. Of course, until readline
is switched on again, advanced editing features like automatic completion
and editing history are not available.

The default value is C<1>.

=head2 realprecision

\label{se:def,realprecision}

=head2 realprecision 

the number of significant digits and, at the same
time, the number of printed digits of real numbers (seeS< >C<\p>). Note that
PARI internal precision works on a word basis (32 or 64 bits), hence may not
coincide with the number of decimal digits you input. For instance to get 2
decimal digits you need one word of precision which, on a 32-bit machine,
actually gives you 9 digits (C<9 E<lt> > F<log> C<_{10}(2^{32}) E<lt> 10>):

  ? default(realprecision, 2)
        realprecision = 9 significant digits (2 digits displayed)

The default value is C<28>, resp.S< >C<38> on a 32-bit, respS< >.64-bit,
machine.

=head2 recover

\label{se:def,recover}this toggle is either 1 (on) or 0 (off). If you change this to C<0>, any
error becomes fatal and causes the gp interpreter to exit immediately. Can be
useful in batch job scripts.

The default value is C<1>.

=head2 secure

\label{se:def,secure}this toggle is either 1 (on) or 0 (off). If on, the X<system>C<system> and
X<extern>C<extern> command are disabled. These two commands are potentially
dangerous when you execute foreign scripts since they let C<gp> execute
arbitrary UNIX commands. C<gp> will ask for confirmation before letting
you (or a script) unset this toggle.

The default value is C<0>.

=head2 seriesprecision

\label{se:def,seriesprecision}number of significant terms
when converting a polynomial or rational function to a power series
(seeS< >C<\ps>).

The default value is C<16>.

=head2 simplify

\label{se:def,simplify}this toggle is either 1 (on) or 0 (off). When the PARI library computes
something, the type of the
result is not always the simplest possible. The only type conversions which
the PARI library does automatically are rational numbers to integers (when
they are of type C<t_FRAC> and equal to integers), and similarly rational
functions to polynomials (when they are of type C<t_RFRAC> and equal to
polynomials). This feature is useful in many cases, and saves time, but can
be annoying at times. Hence you can disable this and, whenever you feel like
it, use the function C<simplify> (see Chapter 3) which allows you to
simplify objects to the simplest possible types recursively (seeS< >C<\y>).
X<automatic simplification>

The default value is C<1>.

=head2 strictmatch

\label{se:def,strictmatch}this toggle is either 1 (on) or 0 (off). If on, unused characters after a
sequence has been
processed will produce an error. Otherwise just a warning is printed. This
can be useful when you are unsure how many parentheses you have to close
after complicated nested loops. Please do not use this; find a decent
text-editor instead.

The default value is C<1>.

=head2 timer

\label{se:def,timer}this toggle is either 1 (on) or 0 (off). If on, every instruction
sequence (anything ended by a
newline in your input) is timed, to some accuracy depending on the hardware
and operating system. The time measured is the user X<CPU time>CPU time,
\emph{not} including the time for printing the results (see C<#> and
C<##>).

The default value is C<0>.
